Objection to the Mani' Condition of Definitions
Objection to the condition that definitions must be exclusive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of exclusivity (mani') in definitions and evaluate the objector's linguistic evidence against it.

Core Idea
Definitions need not always exclude everything—nominal definitions often use broader terms, but the majority prefers exclusivity.

This passage presents the first objection against the matn's condition that a valid definition must be mani' (exclusive). The objector, drawing on linguistic practice, argues that definitions, especially nominal ones, can be broader and still serve their purpose. By examining the objector's evidence from dictionaries and incomplete definitions, you will understand the key usul dispute about the conditions of ta'rif.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
Read the objector's opening claim: the matn's condition that a definition must be preventive (mani') is rejected. Note the exact condition under dispute.
The condition comes from the matn (Sadr al-Shariah) and is supported by the majority.
2
Examine the objector's first evidence
Consider the example of ‘al-Sa'danah is a plant’: a specific term (Sa'danah) defined by a general term (plant). This shows that nominal definitions in dictionaries are often broader.
The objector uses this to argue that definitions need not be exclusive.
3
Analyze the second evidence—incomplete definitions
The objector cites cases where an incomplete definition (had naqis) is used to distinguish two things, e.g., ‘al-asl is that which is needed, al-far' is that which needs.’ Here the aim is not to exclude everything else but to differentiate two items.
This shows that a definition can be broader yet still effective for its intended purpose.
4
State the matn's response
The matn's response is twofold: (1) he follows the majority (jumhur) who require the condition, and (2) he did not declare Imam Razi's definition wrong, only that it would be better to be preventive.
The matn does not rebut the objector's examples logically but relies on the majority position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the condition is required, definitions that are broader (like 'Sa'danah is a plant') would be considered incomplete or imperfect; if not required, such definitions are fully valid.
This affects how definitions are evaluated in usul and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یہاں سے شارح ماتن پر مختلف اعتبارات سے اعتراض کرنا چاہتے ہیں ۔ اعتراض اول کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ آپ کا کہنا تعریف کا مانع ہونا شرط ہے ہم یہ شرط تسلیم نہیں کرتے اس لیے کہ مطلق تعریفات بالعموم اور تعریف اسمی کا بالخصوص مانع ہونا ضروری نہیں کیونکہ لغت کی کتابیں جن میں الفاظ کی تعریفات اسمی ہوا کرتی ہیں اس قسم کی تعریفات سے پر ہیں کہ لفظ خاص ہو اور اس کی تعریف عام ہو مثلا کہا جاتا ہے السعدانة نبت كم سعدانہ ایک بوٹی ہے یہاں سعدانة خاص بوٹی ہے لیکن اس کی تشریح نبت سے کی جاتی ہے جس کا معنی ہے بوئی۔ مزید یہ کہ متقین کی تصریحات موجود ہیں کہ تعریف ناقص ( حد ناقص ) اعم کے ساتھ کی جاسکتی ہے کیونکہ کبھی کبھی ایک چیز کو بعض مساعدًا (بعض چیزوں) سے امتیاز کرنا مقصود ہوتا ہے، تا کہ سامع کے ذہن میں کسی انداز سے بیٹھ جائے ، جیسے کہ اصل اور فرع کے درمیان فرق سامع کو ذہن نشین کرانا مقصود ہو تو کہا جاتا ہے " الاصل المحتاج اليه والفرع "هو المحتاج - چونکہ یہاں پر اصل اور فرع کے درمیان فرق بیان کرنا مقصود تھا وہ حاصل ہو چکا ہے جمیع ماعدا سے امتیاز مقصود نہیں تا کہ تعریف کے غیر مانع ہونے کا اعتراض کیا جائے ۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ امام رازی کی تعریف قابل اعتراض نہیں ہو سکتی ماتن کی طرف سے مختصرا یہ جواب دیا جا سکتا ہے کہ مائن نے جمہور کا مسلک اختیار کیا ہے اور جمہور کے نزدیک تعریف کا مانع ہونا شرط ہے۔ دوسری بات یہ کہ مائن نے امام رازی کی تعریف کو بالکل غلط تو نہیں کہا بلکہ یہ کہا کہ اولی یہ تھا کہ تعریف مانع ہوتی۔
Here the commentator wants to object to the matn from different perspectives. The gist of the first objection is: Your statement that a definition must be preventive (mani') is a condition; we do not accept this condition because absolute definitions in general and nominal definitions in particular do not necessarily need to be preventive. For example, dictionaries contain nominal definitions where the term is specific but its definition is general, e.g., 'al-Sa'danah is a plant.' Here Sa'danah is a specific plant but its explanation is given with 'plant' which means vegetation. Furthermore, the statements of the scholars exist that an incomplete definition (had naqis) can be given with a broader term because sometimes the aim is to distinguish a thing from some other things, so that it settles in the listener's mind in some way. For example, if the aim is to make the listener understand the difference between asl and far', it is said: 'al-asl is that which is needed, and al-far' is that which needs.' Since here the aim was to explain the difference between asl and far', that has been achieved; distinguishing from everything else was not intended so that the objection of the definition not being preventive could be raised. In summary, Imam Razi's definition cannot be objected to. From the matn's side, a brief answer can be given: the matn adopted the position of the majority, and according to the majority, it is a condition for a definition to be preventive. Secondly, the matn did not say Imam Razi's definition is absolutely wrong, but rather said that it would have been better if the definition were preventive.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection raised by the commentator (Sharh) against the matn's (Sadr al-Shariah's) position that a definition must be mani' (preventive/exclusive). The objector argues that definitions, especially nominal ones, do not require being exclusive; they can be broader. Evidence is presented from dictionaries and from the practice of scholars using incomplete definitions to distinguish between two things (like asl and far') without excluding everything else. The matn's response is that he followed the majority (Jumhur) who do require that condition, and that he did not reject Imam Razi's definition outright but only said it would have been better to be preventive. This is a typical usul dispute about the conditions of a valid definition.

Key Terms
تعریف (ta'rif): A definition that explains the meaning of a term.
مانع (mani'): A definition that prevents the inclusion of anything other than the defined term.
حد ناقص (had naqis): An incomplete definition that uses a broader term and does not fully exclude all other things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (Jumhur), what is a condition for a valid definition (ta'rif)?
A) It must be broader than the defined term.
B) It must be mani' (preventive/exclusive).
C) It must be a complete definition (had tam).
D) It must be a nominal definition.

Key Takeaway
The majority requires definitions to be exclusive (mani'), but exceptions exist for nominal and incomplete definitions used for partial distinction.

Defining Asl: Foundational vs. Effectual Dependence
Objection and response on the exclusivity of the definition of 'asl'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between foundational (asasi) and effectual (ta'thiri) dependence and explain why the doer (fa'il) is not considered an asl in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Not all dependence is foundational — the doer effects the action but does not serve as its basis.

The matn defines 'asl' as 'that upon which something else is based.' An objector argues this also fits the doer of an action, since the action depends on him. To preserve the exclusivity of the definition, the commentator introduces a critical distinction: 'ibtina'' can be foundational (asasi) or effectual (ta'thiri). This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for usul terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the objector's claim: the definition of asl applies to the doer because the action is dependent upon and attributed to him. Paraphrase this challenge in your own words.
The objector is testing the definition's exclusivity (mani'iyyah).
2
State the Matn's Definition
The matn defines asl as 'ما يبتنی علیه غیرہ' (that upon which something else is based). Write this phrase and its literal translation.
This is the base definition being contested.
3
Understand the Two Types of Ibtina'
The commentator distinguishes ibtina' asasi (foundational basis) from ibtina' ta'thiri (effectual basis). Explain each type with an example.
Foundational: the very structure depends on it. Effectual: the agent brings it about but does not serve as its foundation.
4
Apply the Distinction to Fa'il
Using the distinction, explain why the doer is not an asl. The doer causes the action (effectual), but the action does not rest on the doer as a foundation; rather, the matter (madah) is the foundation.
Thus the definition remains exclusive when properly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : " الثانى عدم منع صدق الأصل على الفاعل كيف والفعل مترتب عليه ، ومستند إليه ولا معنى للابتناء إلا ذلك " شریح دوسرے اعتراض کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ آپ کی تعریف بھی تو مانع نہیں اس لیے کہ آپ نے اصل کی تعريف "ما يبتنی علیه غیرہ " کے ساتھ کی ہے جبکہ فاعل پر بھی فعل مرتب ہوتا ہے اور اس کی طرف منسوب ہوتا ہے یہی تو ” ابتنساء الغیر “ کا مطلب ہے، لہذا آپ کی تعریف بھی فاعل پر صادق آگئی، حالانکہ ماسوائے مادہ کے اور کسی چیز کو بھی اصل نہیں کہا جاتا۔ ماتن کی طرف سے اس اعتراض کا جواب دیا جا سکتا ہے کہ اصل کی تعریف میں ابتناء سے مراد ابتناء اساسی ہے نہ کہ ابتناء تاثیری ۔ فاعل پر فعل کا ترتب تو بلا شک ہوتا ہے لیکن فاعل اس کے لیے بنیاد نہیں بنتا بلکہ مؤثر ہوتا ہے مزید تفصیلات متعلقہ حاشیہ میں دیکھی جا سکتی ہیں۔
The commentator said: 'Second: the definition fails to prevent the application of 'asl' to the doer (fa'il). How is that? The action is dependent upon and attributed to the doer, and there is no meaning of 'ibtina'' except that.' Sharh: The gist of the second objection is that your definition is also not preventive (mani') because you defined 'asl' as 'that upon which something else is based' while the action is dependent upon the doer and attributed to him. That is precisely the meaning of 'ibtina' al-ghayr'. Therefore, your definition also applies to the doer, whereas nothing other than matter is called 'asl'. From the matn's side, this objection can be answered by saying that in the definition of 'asl', the term 'ibtina'' refers to foundational basis, not effectual basis. The action is indeed dependent upon the doer, but the doer is not the foundation for it; rather, he is the effecter. Further details can be seen in the relevant footnote.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: that the matn's own definition of 'asl' (that upon which something else is based) is not exclusive because it also applies to the doer (fa'il) since the action depends on the doer. The matn's response distinguishes between 'ibtina' asasi' (foundational basis) and 'ibtina' ta'thiri' (effectual basis). The doer is an effecter, not a foundation; matter is the foundation. This preserves the exclusivity of the definition.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): That upon which something else is based (foundational basis).
ابتناء (ibtina'): Dependence or foundation; in definition of asl, it means foundational basis.
فاعل (fa'il): The doer of an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinction does the matn make to exclude the doer (fa'il) from being called 'asl'?
A) The doer is not a matter (madah).
B) The doer has effectual basis, not foundational basis.
C) The doer is not a substance.
D) The doer is not a cause.

Key Takeaway
The exclusivity of the definition of 'asl' is preserved by restricting 'ibtina'' to foundational (asasi) dependence, excluding effectual (ta'thiri) relations like that between doer and action.

Fourth Objection: Definition of 'Asl' Not Comprehensive – The Case of Thought (Fikr)
Objection to definition of 'asl' via thought (fikr)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth objection against the matn's definition of 'asl', and explain why the example of thought (fikr) is used to challenge its comprehensiveness.

Core Idea
The definition of 'asl' fails for thought because its dependence is neither sensory nor intellectual as defined.

In this passage, the commentator (sharh) presents the fourth objection to the matn's definition of 'asl' – that which a ruling is based upon. The objector argues that the definition is not comprehensive (jami') because it does not cover the case of thought (fikr). Known matters are the material for thought, but the dependence (ibtina') of thought on them is neither sensory nor intellectual according to the matn's own interpretation. This challenges the definition's ability to include all valid types of 'asl'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the objection
Determine what exactly is being criticized. The objection targets the matn's definition of 'asl' (foundation) as that upon which another thing is based foundationally.
2
Understand the example: thought (fikr)
The objector uses the example of thought: fikr is the arrangement of known matters. Known matters serve as the material (madah) for thought, but the nature of their relationship (ibtina') is not sensory (hissi) nor intellectual ('aqli) as defined by the matn.
Sensory ibtina' means physical stacking; intellectual ibtina' means the arrangement of a ruling on its evidence.
3
Analyze why the definition fails
Since the dependence is neither sensory nor intellectual, the definition (which presumably requires one of these two types) does not include the case of thought. Therefore, the definition is not comprehensive.
The objector claims that the definition cannot account for all valid 'asl' relationships.
4
Evaluate the structure of the objection
The objection follows a standard pattern: it presents a counterexample that fits the definiendum (here, 'asl') but does not fall under the definition given. The counterexample is tested against the definition's conditions.
This is a common critique method in usul: showing that a definition fails to be jami' (comprehensive) or mani' (exclusive).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : " الرابع أنا إذا قلنا : الفكر ترتيب أمور معلومة، فلا شك أن الأمور المعلومة مادة للفكر، وأصل له مع أن ابتناء الفكر عليها ليس حسيا، وهو ظاهر، ولا عقليا بتفسير المصنف ، وهو ترتب الحكم على دليله . تشریح چوتھے اعتراض کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ جس طرح امام رازی کی تعریف قابل اعتراض ہے آپ کی تعریف پر بھی اعتراض وارد ہے۔ وہ یہ کہ آپ کی تعریف جامع نہیں، کیونکہ فکر ، امور معلومہ ترتیب دینے کا کام ہے۔ امور معلومہ، فکر کے لیے مادہ ہوئے، حالانکہ یہاں پر فکر کا امور معلومہ پر نہ تو اجتنا حسی پایا جاتا ہے چنانچہ وہ ظاہر ہے، کیونکہ ابتنا حسی کا مطلب یہ تھا کہ ایک بھی نیچے رکھی ہوئی محسوس ہو اور دوسری اس کے اوپر ہو یہاں ایسا نہیں۔ اس طرح اجتناء عقلی جس کا مطلب مصنف نے بیان کیا تھا کنر نسب الحکم على دلیلہ وہ بھی نہیں ہوسکتا کیونکہ فکر جدا چیز ہے اور حکم فی جدالہذ افکر پر ابتاء عقلی کی تعریف
The commentator said: 'Fourth: If we say that thought (fikr) is the arrangement of known matters, there is no doubt that known matters are the material for thought and an asl for it, even though the ibtina' of thought upon them is not sensory, and that is obvious, nor intellectual according to the author's interpretation, which is the arrangement of the ruling upon its evidence.' Sharh: The gist of the fourth objection is that just as Imam Razi's definition is objectionable, your definition also faces objection. That is, your definition is not comprehensive (jami'), because thought is the act of arranging known matters. Known matters are the material for thought, yet here the ibtina' of thought upon known matters is neither sensory (hissi) – as that is obvious, because sensory ibtina' means one thing placed below another sensibly, which is not the case here – nor intellectual ('aqli) according to the author's explanation, which is the arrangement of the ruling upon its evidence, because thought is a separate thing and the ruling in itself...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This objection challenges the matn's definition of 'asl' by using the example of thought (fikr). The objector argues that known matters are the material (madah) for thought, but the dependence (ibtina') of thought on them is neither sensory nor intellectual (as defined by the matn). Therefore, the matn's definition of 'asl' (as that upon which something is based foundationally) fails to include this case, meaning the definition is not comprehensive. The objection is incomplete in the text provided.

Key Terms
فکر (fikr): Thought; arranging known matters.
ابتناء حسی (ibtina' hissi): Sensory dependence; one thing physically placed on another.
ابتناء عقلی (ibtina' 'aqli): Intellectual dependence; arrangement of ruling on its evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, why is the matn's definition of 'asl' not comprehensive (jami')?
A) It does not include sensory dependence.
B) It does not include the case of thought (fikr).
C) It is too narrow.
D) It includes too much.

Key Takeaway
A definition in usul must be comprehensive (jami'); a counterexample like thought reveals gaps that require refinement of the definition or clarification of its scope.

Five Interpretations of 'Mā Lahā wa Mā 'Alayhā' in the Definition of Fiqh
Explaining the possible meanings of 'mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the five interpretations of 'mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā' in the Hanafi definition of fiqh, and explain which interpretations are comprehensive and why.

Core Idea
The Hanafi definition of fiqh uses 'mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā' to denote otherworldly benefit and harm, with three out of five interpretations covering all categories of action.

This passage unpacks a key phrase in the Hanafi definition of fiqh attributed to Imam Abu Hanifah. The commentator, al-Taftazani, explains the possible meanings of 'what is for it and what is against it' (mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā) and classifies them according to their comprehensiveness (ashmal). Understanding these interpretations is essential for grasping how the Hanafi school views the underlying benefit and harm of every ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline meaning
Recognize that 'lam' in 'mā lahā' indicates benefit (intifā') and 'alā' in 'mā 'alayhā' indicates harm (tadarru'), both restricted to the afterlife (ukhrawī). This is because fiqh is a religious science dealing with otherworldly consequences.
The restriction to the afterlife avoids confusion with worldly pleasures and pains.
2
List the five interpretations
Enumerate the five interpretations given: (1) reward/punishment, (2) absence of punishment/punishment, (3) reward/absence of reward, (4) permissible/obligatory, (5) permissible/prohibited.
The first three are based on the same benefit/harm axis; the last two switch to permissibility/obligation and permissibility/prohibition.
3
Distinguish comprehensive from non-comprehensive
Check each interpretation against the five categories of action (wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh tanzīhī, makruh tahrīmī, harām) for both doing and leaving. A comprehensive interpretation assigns a distinct consequence (reward, punishment, or neither) to every cell.
Interpretations 2, 3, and 5 cover all cells; 1 and 4 have gaps (e.g., leaving mandub has no consequence in 1).
4
Analyze the tables for the first and second interpretations
Examine the tables in the source for interpretation 1 (reward/punishment) and 2 (absence of punishment/punishment). Note which cells are left empty or filled with 'nothing'. This concretely shows why 1 is not comprehensive (missing for mandub leave, etc.) while 2 is comprehensive.
The tables use 'thawāb', 'uqāb', and 'kuch nahi' (nothing) to indicate consequences.
5
Conclude on preferred interpretations
Based on the analysis, interpretations 2 (absence of punishment/punishment), 3 (reward/absence of reward), and 5 (permissible/prohibited) are comprehensive. The commentator implies these are preferable as they cover all categories of actions without gaps.
Interpretation 4 (permissible/obligatory) is not comprehensive because it fails to cover mandub, mubah, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : " وذهب في قوله " ما لها وما عليها " إلى ما يقال : أن " اللام" للانتفاع و " على " للتضرر . وقيدهما بالأخر وى احترازا عما ينتفع به ، أو يتضرر منه في الدنيا من اللذات والآلام . والمشعر بهذالقيد شهرة أن الفقه من العلوم الدينية - فذكر لهما على هذ التقدير ثلثة معان ، ثم ذكر معنيين آخرین فصارت المعاني المحتملة خمسة . ثلاثة منها تشمل جميع اقسام ما يأتى به المكلف ، واثنان لا يشملا نها كلها .

تشریح
یہاں سے علامہ تفتازانی امام ابو حنیفہ سے منقول فقہ کی تعریف میں لفظ ما لها وما عليها کی تشریح و توضیح کرنا چاہتے ہیں۔ یہ تشریح در حقیقت ماتن نے بیان کر ہی دی ہے۔ صرف اس کی نوک پلک سنوارنا چاہتے ہیں۔ شارح کے کلام کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مائن نے "مالها " کی لام انتفاع کے لیے بنایا ہے اور ما عليها میں علی ضرر کیے لیے بنایا ہے، پھر ضرر اور نفع سے مراد اخروی لیا ہے جس کا قرینہ فقہ ہے کیونکہ فقہ علوم اخرویہ میں سے ہے تو اس اعتبار سے کل پانچ صوتیں بنیں۔

(1) نفع سے مراد ثواب ہوا اور ضرر سے عقاب۔
(۲) نفع سے مراد عدم عقاب کیونکہ انسان اخروی سزا سے بچ جائے تو یہ بھی ایک قسم کا نفع ہے اور ضرر سے مراد عقاب ہو۔
(۳) نفع سے مراد ثواب اور ضرر سے مراد عدم ثواب۔
(۴) مالها سے مراد ما يجوز اور ما عليها سے مراد ما يجب -

The commentator said: "And he (the author) went in his statement 'what is for it and what is against it' to what is said: that 'lam' is for benefit and 'ala' is for harm. And he restricted both to the afterlife as a precaution against what benefits or harms in this world of pleasures and pains. And the indication of this restriction is the fame that fiqh is among the religious sciences. So he mentioned for them, on this estimation, three meanings, then he mentioned two more meanings, so the possible meanings became five. Three of them encompass all categories of what the obligated person does, and two do not encompass them all.

Explanation: Here, 'Allamah Taftazani wants to explain and clarify the word 'ma laha wa ma 'alayha' in the definition of fiqh narrated from Imam Abu Hanifah. This explanation is in reality what the author (matn) has already stated; he only wants to polish it. The gist of the commentator's speech is that the author made 'lam' in 'ma laha' for benefit and 'ala' in 'ma 'alayha' for harm. Then he took harm and benefit to be otherworldly, because the context is fiqh, since fiqh is among the religious sciences. On this basis, five interpretations arise:
(1) Benefit means reward, harm means punishment.
(2) Benefit means absence of punishment, because if a person escapes otherworldly punishment, that too is a kind of benefit; harm means punishment.
(3) Benefit means reward, harm means absence of reward.
(4) 'Ma laha' means what is permissible, 'ma 'alayha' means what is obligatory.
(5) 'Ma laha' means what is permissible, 'ma 'alayha' means what is prohibited.

First interpretation: 'Ma laha' means reward, 'ma 'alayha' means punishment.
Rules: Do (do) Leave (leave)
Wajib: reward, punishment
Mandub: reward, nothing
Mubah: nothing, nothing
Makruh Tanzihi: nothing, nothing
Makruh Tahrimi: punishment, nothing
Haram: punishment, nothing
Serial number: 1, 2, +, 4
Note: In this, the fourth is not comprehensive.

Second interpretation: 'Ma laha' means absence of punishment, 'ma 'alayha' means punishment.
Rules: Do (do) Leave (leave)
Wajib: absence of punishment, punishment
Mandub: absence of punishment, absence of punishment
Mubah: absence of punishment, absence of punishment
Makruh Tanzihi: absence of punishment, absence of punishment
Makruh Tahrimi: punishment, absence of punishment
Serial number: 2, 4
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by the commentator (Taftazani) on the phrase 'ma laha wa ma 'alayha' in the Hanafi definition of fiqh. The commentator explains that the prepositions 'lam' and 'ala' indicate benefit and harm respectively, and these are restricted to the afterlife because fiqh is an otherworldly science. He then enumerates five possible interpretations of 'what is for it' (ma laha) and 'what is against it' (ma 'alayha') in the definition. Three of these interpretations are comprehensive (ashmal) in that they cover all categories of actions (wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram), while two are not. The first interpretation (reward/punishment) fails to cover mandub leaving (no punishment) and other cases. The fourth (permissible/obligatory) fails to cover many actions. The second, third, and fifth are comprehensive because they assign appropriate consequences to each category. The tables for the first and second interpretations illustrate how each category of action is assigned reward, punishment, or neither.

Key Terms
ما لها (mā lahā): What is for it: the benefit or reward associated with an action in the afterlife.
ما عليها (mā 'alayhā): What is against it: the harm or punishment associated with an action in the afterlife.
انتظام (intifa'): Benefit; the meaning of the preposition 'lam' in 'ma laha'.
تضرر (tadarru): Harm; the meaning of the preposition 'ala' in 'ma 'alayha'.
اشمل (ashmal): More comprehensive; an interpretation that covers all categories of actions (wajib, mandub, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, how many of the five interpretations of 'mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā' are considered comprehensive (ashmal)?
A) All five
B) Three
C) Two
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh is designed to cover every action with an otherworldly consequence, and the preferred interpretations of 'mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā' are those that assign distinct outcomes to all categories of action.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
beginner
12 min
Five Forms of Legal Rulings in Hanafi Usul
Tables of five forms with reward/punishment patterns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five forms of legal rulings in Hanafi usul and identify which forms are comprehensive (ashmal).

Core Idea
Five forms map all possible reward and punishment outcomes for doing or omitting an act.

This lesson examines the five forms (suwar) that classify legal rulings (ahkam) in terms of reward (thawab) and punishment (iqab) for performing or leaving an act. Understanding these forms is essential for grasping how the Hanafi school systematically evaluates actions and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Forms
Read the passage and list the five forms. Notice how each form redefines the terms 'ma laha' (for it) and 'ma 'alayha' (against it) to describe different reward/punishment pairings.
The forms are presented in tables; ensure you understand the column headings: 'doing' (فعل) and 'leaving' (ترک).
2
Define 'ma laha' and 'ma 'alayha' for Each Form
For the first form, 'ma laha' = reward, 'ma 'alayha' = punishment. For the second, 'ma laha' = no punishment, 'ma 'alayha' = punishment. For the third, 'ma laha' = reward, 'ma 'alayha' = no reward. For the fourth, 'ma laha' = permissible (ja'iz), 'ma 'alayha' = obligatory (wajib). For the fifth (implied but not fully described), refer to the passage.
The definitions change across forms, so track them carefully.
3
Identify Comprehensive Forms
The passage states that the second, third, and fifth forms are comprehensive (ashmal), meaning they cover all categories of obligated persons. The first and fourth are not comprehensive. Note the 'Note' rows in each table.
Comprehensiveness relates to whether the form accounts for all possible action types (obligatory, recommended, permissible, etc.).
4
Apply the First Form to an Example
Take an obligatory act (e.g., the five daily prayers). According to the first form, doing it brings reward, leaving it brings punishment. Then try a forbidden act: doing it brings punishment, leaving it brings no punishment (since 'ma 'alayha' for leaving is not stated as punishment).
The tables use 'واسطة' (wasta) meaning no reward/no punishment.
5
Recognize the Usul Significance
This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul for understanding the nature of hukm taklifi (obligating rulings). It shows how the legal value of an act is determined by its category and the consequences attached.
Later lessons build on these forms to detail the specific rulings for each action type.

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Talwih presents a detailed classification of the five forms (suwar) of legal rulings (ahkam) according to Hanafi usul. The author explains that among the five, the second, third, and fifth forms are comprehensive (ashmal), meaning they cover all categories of obligated persons (mukallafun) that will be detailed later. The first and fourth are not comprehensive. The passage then provides tables for each form, showing the relationship between the legal category (e.g., obligatory, recommended, etc.) and the reward or punishment for performing or omitting the act. In the first form, 'for it' (ma laha) means reward and 'against it' (ma 'alayha) means punishment. This pattern applies to actions like obligatory (reward for doing, punishment for leaving) and forbidden (punishment for doing, no punishment for leaving). The second form defines 'for it' as no punishment and 'against it' as punishment. The third form defines 'for it' as reward and 'against it' as no reward. The fourth form defines 'for it' as permissible and 'against it' as obligatory. The notes indicate which forms are comprehensive. This classification is central to understanding how actions are evaluated in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اشمل (ashmal): Comprehensive; a form that covers all categories of rulings.
مالها (ma laha): Literally 'what is for it'; the reward or legal consequence of performing an action.
ما عليها (ma 'alayha): Literally 'what is against it'; the punishment or legal consequence of omitting an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul classifies legal rulings into five forms based on how reward and punishment apply to performing and omitting actions, with the second, third, and fifth forms being comprehensive.

Hanafi Classification of Actions (Ahkam Shari'iyya)
Classifying actions using 'ma laha' and 'ma 'alayha' scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any action of a mukallaf into one of the six Hanafi categories (wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi, haram) based on the criteria of preference (doing vs. leaving) and prohibition (with or without) and the strength of evidence.

Core Idea
The Hanafi classification of actions is determined by three factors: preference, prohibition, and proof strength.

In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi classification of actions, a foundational topic in Usul al-Fiqh. Using the 'ma laha' and 'ma 'alayha' scenarios, the text presents a structured way to determine the hukm of any act based on reward, punishment, permissibility, and obligation. Understanding this framework is essential for deriving and applying legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Consider the act being performed or abandoned by the mukallaf. Is it an act of worship, a transaction, or a habitual action?
The classification applies to actions within the capacity of the mukallaf.
2
Determine preference: doing vs. leaving
Is doing the act more preferable, or is leaving it more preferable? Or are they equal? For example, doing of wajib is preferable; leaving of haram is preferable; for mubah, both are equal.
This preference comes from the primary sources or scholarly consensus.
3
Check prohibition on the opposite
If doing is preferable, is leaving prohibited? If leaving is preferable, is doing prohibited? For wajib, leaving is prohibited; for haram, doing is prohibited; for mandub, leaving is not prohibited.
Prohibition can be either definitive (qat'i) or presumptive (zanni).
4
Assess the strength of prohibition
If the prohibition is by a definitive proof (qat'i al-dalalah wa al-thubut), the act is haram (if doing is prohibited) or fard (if leaving is prohibited, but fard is stronger than wajib). If by a presumptive proof (zanni), it is makruh tahrimi (if doing is prohibited) or wajib (if leaving is prohibited).
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul.
5
Assign the category
Combine the above: If doing is preferable and leaving is prohibited by a zanni proof → wajib. If doing is preferable and leaving is not prohibited → mandub. If doing and leaving are equal → mubah. If leaving is preferable and doing is prohibited by a qat'i proof → haram. If by a zanni proof → makruh tahrimi. If leaving is preferable and doing is not prohibited → makruh tanzihi.
The tables in the source illustrate this mapping.

The commentator said: 'The divisions are twelve, because that which the legally responsible person performs: if its doing and leaving are equal, then it is permissible. Otherwise, if its doing is preferable, then with prohibition of leaving it is obligatory, and without it it is recommended. If its leaving is preferable, then with prohibition of doing by a definitive proof it is forbidden, and by a presumptive proof it is abominable with the abomination of prohibition; and without prohibition of doing it is abominable with the abomination of disrecommendation. This is according to the view of Muhammad, and it is appropriate here, because the author made the abominable of disrecommendation something that its doing is permissible, and the abominable of prohibition something that its doing is not permissible, rather its leaving is obligatory, like the forbidden. And this is not correct according to the view of the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], which is that that whose leaving is preferable than its doing: then with prohibition of doing it is forbidden, and without it it is abominable with the abomination of disrecommendation if it is closer to the permissible, meaning that its doer is not punished but the clothes of its abandoner have lesser reward. And abomination of prohibition if it is closer to the forbidden, meaning that its doer deserves a danger short of punishment by fire.' Explanation.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a classification exercise for the five (or six) legal categories of actions in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It presents three scenarios (third, fourth, fifth) where the meanings of 'ma laha' (what is for it) and 'ma 'alayha' (what is against it) are varied to show how different interpretations affect the ruling. In each scenario, a table lists the six categories—wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi, and haram—and assigns to each a pair of values for 'doing' and 'abandoning', such as reward/no reward or permissible/obligatory. The notes indicate which scenario is considered 'applicable' (معمل). The commentator then provides the well-known classification from Imam Muhammad: there are twelve divisions based on whether the act and its abandonment are equal, or one is preferable, and whether there is a prohibition. He also mentions the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding makruh tanzihi and tahrimi, which differs slightly. The passage thus teaches the nuanced way Hanafi scholars map legal categories onto acts using the criteria of preference and prohibition.

Key Terms
مالها (ma laha): What is for the act: reward or permissibility
ما عليها (ma 'alayha): What is against the act: no reward or obligation/haram
واجب (wajib): An act whose doing is rewarded and leaving is punished
مندوب (mandub): An act whose doing is rewarded but leaving is not punished
مباح (mubah): An act whose doing and leaving are equal, neither rewarded nor punished
مکروہ تنزیہی (makruh tanzihi): An act whose leaving is preferable but doing is not prohibited; doer not punished
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): An act whose leaving is obligatory by a presumptive proof; doer deserves punishment short of hellfire
حرام (haram): An act whose doing is forbidden by a definitive proof; doer is punished

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's quote, how many total divisions of actions are there based on preference and prohibition?
A) Five
B) Twelve
C) Six
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of actions is a nuanced system based on preference and prohibition, with the strength of proof distinguishing between haram and makruh tahrimi.

Deriving the Twelve Shar'i Rulings
Explaining derivation of shar'i rulings from preference and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six primary shar'i rulings, explain how they become twelve via the aspects of doing and omitting, and distinguish between the definitions of haram and makruh tahrimi based on strength of proof and intra-Hanafi differences.

Core Idea
Six shar'i rulings become twelve when considering both performing and omitting each.

This passage resolves a common confusion: why does the Hanafi classification list only six rulings when we know terms like fard, sunnah, and nafl? The commentator shows that 'wajib' includes fard, 'mandub' includes sunnah and nafl, and each of the six has two aspects—doing and not doing—making twelve. He also highlights a nuanced intra-Hanafi difference regarding the definition of makruh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational preference
Analyze the action's preference (ula) between doing and leaving. If neither is preferred, it is mubah. If doing is preferred, check if leaving is prohibited (wajib) or allowed (mandub). If leaving is preferred, check if doing is prohibited by a definitive proof (haram) or conjectural proof (makruh tanzihi).
This logical classification covers all possibilities.
2
List the six categories arising from the preference scheme
The six are: wajib (including fard), mandub (including sunnah/nafl), haram, mubah, makruh tahrimi (prohibited by zanni proof), and makruh tanzihi (disliked but not prohibited).
Wajib and mandub are used in a general sense.
3
Understand the dual aspects (fi'l and tark)
Each category applies both to performing the action and to omitting it. For example, performing wajib is an obligatory ruling; omitting wajib is a prohibited ruling. Thus, 6 × 2 = 12 rulings.
The text counts these as distinct shar'i rulings.
4
Clarify the inclusive terminology
The commentator explains that 'wajib' in common usage includes fard, and 'mandub' includes sunnah and nafl. This resolves the objection that fard, sunnah, etc., are omitted from the sixfold list.
This is a key to understanding the framework.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference on makruh
Imam Muhammad defines makruh tahrimi as prohibited by a non-definitive proof (placed in 'la yajuz'), and makruh tanzihi as disliked but not prohibited (in 'yajuz'). The shaykhayn define them based on closeness to haram or halal, placing makruh tahrimi approximately in 'yajuz' with minor blame. The text follows Imam Muhammad's definitions.
These are both valid Hanafi positions; the text chooses one for consistency with the matn.

حرام، مکروہ تحریمی ، تنزیہی کی مذکورہ تعریفات امام محمد کے مطابق کی گئی ہیں چنانچہ مقام کے مناسب بھی یہی تعریف ہے، کیونکہ مائن نے حرام اور مکروہ تحریمی کو لا يجوز میں داخل فرمایا ہے اور مکروہ تنزیہی کو يجوز میں امام محمد کے تعریفات سے بھی ایسا ہی معلوم ہوتا ہے جبکہ شیخین نے کچھ اور طرح سے تعریف کی ہیں ، وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر اس کا ترک اولی ہو لیکن اس کے کرنے سے منع کیا گیا ہو، پھر دیکھیں گے کہ اگر حرام کے زیادہ قریب ہے تو اس کو مکروہ تحریمی کہیں گے اور اگر حلال کے زیادہ قریب ہے تو اس کو مکروہ تنزیہی کہیں گے۔ ان حضرات نے مکروہ تحریمی کو تقریبای جوز میں داخل کیا ہے اور مکروہ تحریمی کے کرنے سے کوئی بڑی سزا یعنی عقوبت بالنار نہ ہوگی بلکہ سرزنش یا سفارش سے محرومی کا سامنا کرنا پڑے گا۔
قال الشارح : " ثم المراد بالواجب ما يشمل الفرض أيضا ، لأن استعماله بهذ المعنى شائع عندهم ، كقولهم الزكاة واجبة ، والحج واجب ، بخلاف إطلاق الحرام على المكروه تحريما . والمراد بالمندوب ما يشمل السنة ، والنفل ، فصارت الأقسام ستة ولكل منها طرفان : فعل أى إيقاع على ما هو المعنى المصدرى ، وترك أى عدم فعل

تشریح
یہاں سے ایک سوال کا جواب دینا چاہتے ہیں کہ آپ نے فرمایا شرعی احکام کل بارہ ہیں حالانکہ ابھی تک کچھ اور باقی ہیں جن کو آپ نے شمار نہیں کیا مثلا فرض نفل اور سنت وغیرہ۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ واجب سے عام مراد ہے جس کو کرنا ضروری ہو، چاہے دلیل قطعی کے ساتھ ہو یا ظنی کے ساتھ ۔ اس عمومی معنی کے لفظ سے واجب کا اطلاق فرض پر کیا جاتا ہے جبکہ حرام کا اطلاق مکروہ تحریمی پر عام طور پر نہیں کیا جاتا ، اس لیے ان کو الگ الگ اعتبار کیا جاتا ہے مثلا کہا جاتا ہے الزکوة واجبة ياالحج واجب حالانکہ حج اور زکوۃ فرض ہیں۔ اسی طرح مندوب سے مراد عام معنی ہے جو کہ نفل وسنت سب کو شامل ہے۔ حاصل یہ ہے کہ جب شرعی احکام کل چھے ہوئے واجب ، مندوب حرام مباح مکروہ تحریمی ہمکروہ تنزیہی اور ہر ایک کے دو پہلو ہیں کرنا اور نہ کرنا تو اس طرح ڈیل ہو کر بارہ قسم
ہو گئے۔
From here, the commentator wants to explain how the twelve rulings of the Shari'ah that relate to every accountable person are formed. The conclusion is that the action a person does either has its two sides equal, i.e., doing and not doing are both permissible – this is called mubah. If doing it is preferable, then we see: if refraining is prohibited, i.e., "must do", then it is called wajib. If doing it is preferable but leaving it is also permissible, then it is called mandub. If leaving it is preferable but doing it is prohibited, then it is called haram, provided the prohibiting proof is definitive; if not, then it is called makruh tanzihi. The aforementioned definitions of haram, makruh tahrimi, and tanzihi are according to Imam Muhammad – thus this definition is appropriate for the context, because the matn has included haram and makruh tahrimi within 'la yajuz' and makruh tanzihi within 'yajuz'. Imam Muhammad's definitions also appear similar, while the two shaykhs have defined them differently: they said that if leaving it is preferable but doing it is prohibited, then we see: if it is closer to haram, it is called makruh tahrimi; if closer to halal, it is called makruh tanzihi. They have included makruh tahrimi approximately within 'yajuz' and that doing makruh tahrimi does not incur a major punishment, i.e., punishment by fire, but rather scolding or deprivation of intercession. The commentator said: "Then by wajib is meant that which includes fard as well, because its usage in this meaning is common among them, as they say 'zakat is wajib' and 'hajj is wajib', contrary to applying haram to makruh tahrimi. And by mandub is meant that which includes sunnah and nafl. Thus the categories become six, and for each there are two aspects: fi'l i.e., doing according to the masdari meaning, and tark i.e., not doing. So they become twelve." Commentary: From here, they want to answer a question: You said the shar'i rulings are twelve, but there remain others like fard, nafl, sunnah, etc. The answer is that by 'wajib' is meant the general meaning, which is that which must be done, whether the proof is definitive or conjectural. This general meaning of 'wajib' is applied to fard, while 'haram' is not generally applied to makruh tahrimi; therefore they are considered separately. For example, it is said 'zakat is wajib' or 'hajj is wajib' although hajj and zakat are fard. Similarly, 'mandub' is meant in the general sense which includes both nafl and sunnah. The conclusion is that when the shar'i rulings total six – wajib, mandub, haram, mubah, makruh tahrimi, makruh tanzihi – and each has two aspects: doing and not doing, then in this way they become twelve types.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of the shar'i rulings (ahkam). It begins by explaining the logical derivation of six primary categories based on the preference (ula) of performing or omitting an action. If neither is preferred, it is mubah. If doing is preferred, then if omission is prohibited, it is wajib; if omission is allowed, it is mandub. If omission is preferred, then if doing is prohibited by a definitive proof, it is haram; if by a non-definitive proof, it is makruh tanzihi. The text notes that these definitions follow Imam Muhammad. It mentions a difference with the 'shaykhayn' (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding makruh tahrimi and tanzihi: Imam Muhammad bases them on the strength of the prohibition, while the shaykhayn base them on closeness to haram or halal. The commentator follows the matn's usage, including haram and makruh tahrimi under 'la yajuz' and makruh tanzihi under 'yajuz'. Then the commentator clarifies that 'wajib' in this context includes fard, because common usage does not distinguish. Similarly, 'mandub' includes sunnah and nafl. Thus the six categories become twelve when considering both doing and omitting each. This resolves the objection that fard, sunnah, etc. seem omitted. The passage thus firmly establishes the Hanafi framework of the five or six primary rulings and their dual aspects.

Key Terms
واجب (wajib): Here it means that which must be done, including both fard (definitive proof) and wajib (conjectural proof); used in a general sense.
مندوب (mandub): Recommended act; includes sunnah and nafl, doing it is preferable but leaving is permissible.
حرام (haram): Prohibited act with a definitive proof of prohibition; leaving is obligatory.
مکروہ تحریمی (makruh tahrimi): Prohibited by a non-definitive proof; includes severe dislike but no punishment in the afterlife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Shar'i rulings are six primary categories, each with two aspects (doing and omitting), giving twelve; terminology like 'wajib' and 'mandub' includes narrower categories like fard and sunnah.

Fi’l and Tark as Resultant States (Hasil al-Masdar) in Hanafi Usul
Clarifying the referent of fi'l and tark in relation to rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between masdar and hasil al-masdar meanings of fi'l and tark, and apply it to understand how rulings (ahkam) are attributed to omissions in the Hanafi usul system.

Core Idea
Fi’l and tark in rulings refer to resultant states, not mere actions or absences.

This passage from the commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih clarifies a critical nuance in Hanafi usul: when we say an action is wajib or haram, do we mean the doing itself or the resultant state? Similarly, when we say omitting is wajib or haram, how can a non-action be a subject of a ruling? The commentator provides a precise definition that resolves this issue and safeguards the coherence of the twelve-fold classification of ahkam.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two meanings of fi’l
The masdar (verbal noun) meaning is ‘doing’; the hasil al-masdar (derived noun) meaning is the resultant state like prayer or fasting.
The text explicitly says 'al-hasil min al-masdar'.
2
Extend the same to tark
Tark means leaving that resultant state, not merely the absence of action.
Tark is not pure non-existence but a state of non-performance.
3
Recognize the problem
Rulings are attributes of actions. How then can ‘omission of wajib’ be haram? That would attribute haram to a non-action.
The objection is anticipated in the text.
4
Apply the commentator’s solution
By interpreting tark as the resultant state of not performing, the omission becomes a kind of ‘action’ in terms of its attribute.
Thus, rulings apply to omission as a resultant state.
5
Confirm consistency
This allows the system to have both affirmative and negative aspects for each ruling, resulting in the twelve types.
The classification of ahkam is preserved.

والأمور المذكورة ... الخ سے ایک دوسری تحقیق بیان کرنا چاہتے ہیں جس سے متن پروارد ہونے والے ایک اعتراض کا دفعہ بھی ہو جاتا ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ مثلا آپ نے واجب کی جو دو قسم بتلائی ہیں یعنی واجب کا کرنا اور نہ کرنا ، حالانکہ نہ کرتا تو کوئی فعل نہیں جس کی وجوب صفت بن سکے کیونکہ وجوب حرمت فعل کی صفت بنتے ہیں تو اس عبارت سے جواب دیا کہ بلا شک در حقیقت وجوب و حرمت
The commentator said: "And what is meant is what the accountable person brings about from the action in the sense of the derived noun (al-hasil min al-masdar), like the state that is called prayer and the condition that is called fasting, and the like of that which is an effect emanating from the accountable person. The aspect of his doing is its performance, and the aspect of his omitting is its non-performance. And the aforementioned things – wajib, haram, etc. – although in reality they are specifically attributes of the act, yet they may also be applied to the absence of the act. Thus it is said: 'Not performing wajib is haram' and 'Not performing haram is wajib.' That is what is meant here." Commentary: From here, the commentator wants to express a verification (tahqiq) which is that we said each action has two aspects: one side is fi'l and the other is tark. Then fi'l has two meanings: one is the masdari meaning, i.e., doing the action; the second is the derived noun (hasil al-masdar), i.e., the state (hay'ah) that results after doing the action. For example, after standing, a state results which is called qiyam; or after performing prayer, a condition results which is called salat; and similarly for fasting. Here, the intended meaning of fi'l is this last meaning. Similarly, by tark is meant leaving that very quality and state, not merely not doing, which is the masdari meaning. And from 'wa al-umur al-madhkurah...' etc., they want to express a second verification by which an objection that occurs against the matn is also repelled. The objection is: you mentioned two types of wajib – its doing and its not doing – whereas not doing is not an act that can have the attribute of wajib, because obligation and prohibition are attributes of the act. To this, the answer is given from this phrase that, no doubt, in reality obligation and prohibition...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a deep clarification of the terms 'fi'l' and 'tark' used when describing rulings. The commentator explains that 'fi'l' in this context does not mean the verbal noun (masdar) of 'doing', but rather the resultant quality or state (al-hasil min al-masdar) that emerges from the action, such as the state of prayer (salat) or fasting (sawm). Similarly, 'tark' means leaving that state, not merely the absence of action. This nuanced understanding resolves a potential contradiction: since rulings like wajib and haram are primarily attributes of actions (fi'l), how can they be predicated of omission (tark)? The answer is that when we say 'not doing wajib is haram', the haram is attributed to the omission itself, which is considered a kind of action in terms of its resultant state. The commentator defends the matn's classification of twelve rulings (six categories times two aspects) by showing that the attributes apply both to performing the act and to omitting it, but the omission is understood as a 'state' of not performing, not a mere lack of action. This is a typical Hanafi precision in usul al-fiqh, ensuring consistency in the system of ahkam.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l): Here it means the resultant state (al-hasil min al-masdar) like the state of prayer, not the masdar (doing).
ترك (tark): Leaving the resultant state, not merely not doing.

Key Takeaway
Rulings can apply to omissions because in Hanafi usul, omission is understood as a resultant state (hasil al-masdar) of not performing, not a mere absence.

The Twelve Categories of Shari'ah Rulings: Act and Omission
Explaining the division of six ahkam into twelve by considering act and omission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between fi'l as resultant state and fi'l as infinitive, and justify why Hanafi usul divides Shari'ah rulings into twelve categories based on act and omission.

Core Idea
Each of the six ahkam has two dimensions: doing and omitting, forming twelve distinct categories.

In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi classification of Shari'ah rulings. The passage explains that each of the six types—wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh tanzihi, makruh tahrimi, and haram—applies not only to performing an act but also to omitting it, thus doubling the categories. The commentator clarifies the precise meanings of 'act' and 'omission' to resolve potential conceptual difficulties.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate the two meanings of fi'l
Understand that 'fi'l' can mean either the infinitive (the doing) or the resultant state (the quality that arises from the act). In this classification, it refers to the latter.
For example, the posture called prayer is the resultant state, not the process of performing prayer.
2
Understand the definition of tark
Recognize that 'tark' is defined as 'adam al-fi'l (absence of the resultant state), not as self-restraint (kaf al-nafs). This distinction is crucial for keeping the categories separate.
If tark were self-restraint, then omitting haram would be identical to performing wajib.
3
Recognize the metaphorical application of rulings
Note that rulings like wujub and hurmah are originally attributes of acts, but they are metaphorically applied to omissions. For example, 'not praying is haram' is a valid expression.
This answers the objection that omissions cannot bear rulings.
4
Justify the necessity of twelve categories
Understand that if the six categories were generalized to include both act and omission, the statement 'wajib is that for which one is rewarded' would not hold for 'wajib' in the sense of constantly avoiding haram (since that carries no reward in itself). Hence, detailed classification is needed.
The twelve-fold division accurately captures the reward structure.

قال الشارح : " والمراد ما ياتى به المكلف الفعل بمعنى الحاصل من المصدر كالهيئة التي تسمى صلاة والحالة التي تسمى صوما ، ونحو ذلك مما هو أثر صادر عن المكلف ، و طرف فعله إيقاعه ، وطرف تركه عدم إيقاعه، والأمور المذكورة من الواجب والحرام وغيرهما ، وإن كانت في الحقيقة من صفات الفعل خاصة إلا أنها قد تطلق على عدم الفعل أيضا فيقال عدم مباشرة الواجب حرام ، وعدم مباشرة الحرام واجب هو المراد ههنا . تشريح: يهان سے شارح ايك تحقيق بيان كرنا چاهتے ہیں وه يه ہے كه ہم نے جو هر كام كے دو پہلو بيان كئے ہیں: ايك جانب فعل، دوسرا ترك پھر فعل كے دو معنی ہیں ايك مصدری معنی یعنی كردن (كام كا كرنا دوسرا حاصل مصدر وارد ہونے والے ايك اعتراض كا دفعہ بھی ہو جاتا ہے۔ سوال يه ہے كه مثلا آپ نے واجب كی جو دو قسم بتلائی ہیں یعنی واجب كا كرنا اور نہ كرنا ، حالانكہ نہ كرتا تو كوئی فعل نہیں جس کی وجوب صفت بن سكے كيونكہ وجوب حرمت فعل کی صفت بنتے ہیں تو اس عبارت سے جواب دیا كه بلا شك در حقيقت وجوب و حرمت فعل مكلف کی صفات ہیں مگر كبھی كبھی مجازاً عدم فعل پر بھی ان کا اطلاق ہوتا ہے مثلا كہا جاتا ہے واجب كا نہ كرنا حرام ہے یا حرام كا نہ كرنا واجب ہے، مثلا: نماز كا نہ پڑھنا حرام ہے، یا سود كا نہ لینا واجب ہے۔ قال الشارح: "وإنما فسر الترك بعدم الفعل ليصير قسما آخر ، إذ لو أريد به كف النفس لكان ترك الحرام مثلاً فعل الواجب بعينه ". تشريح: يهان سے ماتن کی تحقيق پر تنبيہ كرنا چاهتے ہیں كه ماتن نے ترك كا معنی "عدم الفعل "كيا ہے "كف النفس " كيونكى نہ كيا؟ اس کی وجہ يه ہے كه اگر ترك كا معنی كف النفس سے كيا جائے تو دوسری قسم عليحدہ نہيں بنتی۔ اس کی وضاحت ايك مثال سے سمجھیں مثلا : زنا يه حرام فعل ہے تو اس کے دو پہلو ہیں ايك زنا كا كرنا جو كه حرام ہے، دوسرا پہلو اسباب مہيا ہوتے ہوئے زنا كا نہ كرنا ہے تو اسی طرح حرام کے دو پہلو نكلے ايك اس كا كرنا اور دوسرا نہ كرنا اگر ترك "كف النفس" كے معنی میں ليتے تو دوسری قسم نہ نكلتی كيونكہ "كف " كا معنی ہے بچتے رہنا اور ظاہر بات ہے كه جب اسباب مہيا ہوں اس وقت زنا سے بچنا بعينہ فعل واجب ہے۔ قال الشارح : فإن قلت أى حاجته إلى اعتبار الفعل والترك وجعل الأقسام اثنى عشر وهلا اقتصر على الستة بأن يراد بالواجب مثلا أعم من الفعل والترك ؟قلت: لأنه إذا قال: الواجب فيما يثاب عليه لم يصح ذلك فى الواجب بمعنى عدم فعل الحرام ، فلا بد من التفصيل المذكور ". تشريح: يهان سے ايك سوال اور اس كا جواب دینا مقصود ہے۔ سوال يه ہے كه هر ايك حكم شرعی کی جانبين یعنی فعل اور ترك كا اعتبار كر کے بارہ اقسام بنانے کی كيا ضرورت تھی ؟ چھ قسم برقرار ركھے جاتے البتہ ان میں تعميم کر دی جاتی مثلا واجب میں تقسیم کر دی جاتی كه خواہ واجب اس كا كرنا ہو یا چھوڑنا تو اس طرح تكثير اقسام سے چھٹكارا حاصل ہو جاتا اس كا جواب يه ہے كه واجب کو "مثاب عليه" میں داخل كيا گيا ہے حالانكہ واجب بمعنی عدم فعل الحرام یعنی حرام کو چھوڑے ركھنا اس پر كوئی ثواب نہيں اس ليے تفصيل مذكور
The commentator said: "What is meant is what the obligated person performs: the act in the sense of the result of the source (verbal noun), like the posture called prayer and the state called fasting, and similar things that are effects emanating from the obligated person. The side of doing it is its performance, and the side of omitting it is its non-performance. And the mentioned matters of wajib, haram, and others, although in reality they are attributes of the act specifically, yet they are sometimes applied also to the non-existence of the act, so it is said: 'Non-performance of wajib is haram' and 'Non-performance of haram is wajib.' That is what is meant here." Explanation: From here, the commentator wishes to present an investigation: that we mentioned two aspects for each action: one side is the act, the other is the omission. Then the term 'act' has two meanings: one is the infinitive meaning (doing the action), the other is the resultant meaning (the state that results after doing the action). For example, after standing, a certain state results called qiyam, or after performing prayer, a certain formed state results called salah and sawm. Here, 'act' means this latter meaning. Similarly, 'omission' means leaving off that quality and state, not mere non-doing which is the infinitive meaning. "And the mentioned matters..." etc. intends to present another investigation by which an objection raised against the matn is also repelled. The question is: You (the author) mentioned two types for wajib, i.e., doing wajib and not doing it, whereas not doing is not an act that could be described with wujub, because wujub and hurmah are attributes of acts. So through this sentence, the answer is given: Without doubt, in reality wujub and hurmah are attributes of the obligated person's act, but sometimes they are metaphorically applied also to non-existence of the act, e.g., it is said: 'Not doing wajib is haram' or 'Not doing haram is wajib.' Example: Not praying is haram, or not taking interest is wajib. The commentator said: "And he defined 'omission' as 'non-existence of the act' only in order for it to become another category, because if he intended by it 'self-restraint', then omitting haram would be exactly the same as doing wajib." Explanation: From here, he intends to alert regarding the author's investigation: why did the author define 'tark' as 'adam al-fi'l' and not as 'kaf al-nafs'? The reason is that if 'tark' were defined as 'self-restraint', then the second category would not be separate. Clarify with an example: Zina is a haram act, so it has two aspects: one is committing zina, which is haram, the second aspect is not committing zina while its causes are available. Thus, two aspects of haram emerged: one is doing it, the other is not doing it. If 'tark' were taken in the sense of 'self-restraint', the second category would not emerge because 'kaf' means to keep away, and it is obvious that when causes are available, refraining from zina is exactly the same as doing wajib. The commentator said: "If you say: What is the need to consider act and omission and make the divisions twelve? Why not restrict to six by meaning by 'wajib', for example, general including both act and omission? I reply: Because when he says 'wajib is that for which one is rewarded', that does not hold true for wajib in the sense of non-action of haram, so the mentioned detail is necessary." Explanation: From here, a question and its answer are intended. The question: What was the need to consider each shari'ah ruling's two sides, act and omission, and make twelve categories? The six categories could have been kept but generalized, e.g., in wajib it could be divided that wajib is either doing it or leaving it, thus avoiding multiplicity of categories. The answer: Because 'wajib' has been included in 'that for which one is rewarded', whereas 'wajib' in the sense of non-action of haram (i.e., constantly leaving haram) carries no reward, so the mentioned detail is necessary.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's discussion on the classification of Shari'ah rulings. The commentator (sharh) explains that each of the six types of rulings (wajib, mandub, haram, makruh tahrimi, makruh tanzihi, mubah) has two dimensions: the act itself (fi'l) and its omission (tark). Thus, six become twelve. First, he clarifies that 'act' here does not mean the infinitive 'doing' but rather the resultant state or quality that arises from the act, such as the posture of prayer or the state of fasting. Similarly, 'omission' means leaving off that resultant state, not mere non-doing. This is important because without this distinction, one might think that 'not doing' is not an act that can bear a ruling. He then addresses an objection: since rulings like wujub and hurmah are attributes of acts, how can we speak of 'not doing' being wajib or haram? He answers that, in reality, rulings are attributes of acts, but metaphorically (majazan) they are applied to the non-existence of an act. For example, 'not praying is haram' and 'not taking interest is wajib' are valid expressions. Next, he explains why the author (matn) defined 'tark' as 'adam al-fi'l' (absence of the act) rather than 'kaf al-nafs' (self-restraint). If 'tark' meant 'self-restraint', then omitting a haram act would be identical to performing a wajib act, because both are cases of self-restraint from evil, so the category of 'tark' would not be distinct. By defining it as 'absence of the act', we keep the two categories separate. Finally, he answers why we cannot simply keep the six categories and generalize them to include both act and omission. He points out that when the rule says 'wajib is that for which one is rewarded' (yuthab 'alayhi), this does not apply to 'wajib' in the sense of 'avoiding haram' (wajib bi ma'na 'adam fi'l al-haram), because constant avoidance of haram carries no reward in itself (reward comes only for the intention and the act of avoidance as a whole, but not for every instance of not committing a sin). Therefore, the detailed twelve-fold division is necessary to accurately describe the rulings.

Key Terms
فعل (fi‘l): In this context, 'fi‘l' refers to the resultant quality or state (al-ḥāṣil min al-maṣdar) that arises from the action, e.g., the posture called prayer or the state called fasting, not the mere infinitive of doing.
ترك (tark): In this context, 'tark' means the non-existence of the act ('adam al-fi‘l), i.e., leaving off the resultant state. It does not mean self-restraint (kaf al-nafs).
واجب (wājib): A shari'ah ruling that requires performance; it is used as a general term including fard, but sometimes also applied to non-action of haram metaphorically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator define 'tark' as 'adam al-fi'l' (absence of the act) rather than 'kaf al-nafs' (self-restraint)?
A) Because 'kaf al-nafs' is a synonym of 'adam al-fi'l'.
B) Because defining it as 'kaf al-nafs' would make the omission category identical to performing a wajib act, thus the two categories would collapse into one.
C) Because 'adam al-fi'l' is easier to understand.
D) Because 'kaf al-nafs' is a term used only for acts of worship.

Key Takeaway
The twelve-fold classification of Shari'ah rulings arises from considering both the act and its omission, with 'omission' defined as absence of the resultant state, not self-restraint.

Reward for Leaving Haram: Simple Abandonment vs Active Restraint
A dispute on the reward for abandoning the forbidden

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple abandonment (tark al-haram) and active self-restraint (nahy al-nafs) and apply the Hanafi rule that only active restraint is rewarded.

Core Idea
Not every abstention from sin is rewarded; only active self-restraint when tempted is a wajib that earns reward.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: whether simply not committing a forbidden act (tark al-haram) brings reward. An objection citing the Quranic verse (79:40-41) claims it does. The Hanafi response clarifies that reward is for the positive act of restraining the soul (nahy al-nafs), not for mere omission. Understanding this distinction avoids the absurdity of constant reward for every uncommitted sin.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the objection
Identify the core question: Is leaving the forbidden rewarded? Recognize the objector's claim that it is wajib and therefore rewarded, supported by the Quranic verse.
2
Examine the objector's proof
Analyze Surah al-Nazi'at (79):40-41, which promises Paradise to those who 'fear their Lord and forbid the soul from desire.' The objector takes this as evidence that leaving haram is rewarded.
The verse is dalil naqli (textual evidence) but its interpretation is contested.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi commentator argues that reward is for performing the wajib act of 'forbidding the soul' (nahy al-nafs), which is a positive action. Simple non-commission without temptation would lead to countless rewards each moment, which is not observed.
The response distinguishes between mere absence of sin and active restraint despite temptation.
4
Apply the distinction
Identify the two categories: (1) simple abandonment (no reward) and (2) self-restraint when causes are present and the soul inclines (rewarded as a wajib act). The Quranic verse refers to the second.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a person with no opportunity to sin earns no reward; one who resists temptation does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : "إلا أن فيه مباحث الأول : أنه جعل ترك الحرام مما يثاب عليه ، ولا يعاقب . واعترض عليه بأنه واجب ، والواجب يثاب عليه وفي التنزيل (وأما من خاف مقام ربه ونهى النفس عن الهوى فإن الجنة هي المأوى ) وجوابه: أن المثاب عليه فعل الواجب لا عدم مباشرة الحرام وإلا لكان لكل واحد في كل لحظه مثوبات كثيرة بحسب كل جرام لا يصدر عنه ، ونهى النفس كفها عن الحرام . وهو من قبيل فعل الواجب ولا نزاع فى أن ترك الحرام بمعنى كف النفس عند تهيؤ الأسباب وميلان النفس إليه مما يثاب عليه ." تشریح: یہاں سے مصنف پر وارد والے چند اعتراضات کا جواب مقصود ہے پہلا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ مصنف نے ترک حرام کے متعلق فرمایا تھا کہ " لا یشاب عـلیـه ولا يعاقب عليه "حالانکہ ترک زنا واجب ہے اور ہر واجب پر ثواب ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ قرآن پاک میں ہے أمـامــن خــاف مــقـام ربــه ونهى انفس عن الهوى ... الخ ) یعنی جس نے اپنے رب کی پیشی کا خوف کرتے ہوئے اپنے نفس کو حرام سے روکا اس کے لیے جنت کا ٹھکانا ہے تو اس آیت سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ ترک حرام پر ثواب ملتا ہے تو مصنف نے کس طرح کہہ دیا کہ " لا يثاب ولا يعاقب "۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ مصنف کی بات درست ہے کہ حرام کے نہ کرنے پر ثواب نہیں ملتا ، بلکہ واجب کے کرنے پر ثواب ملتا ہے اگر حرام کے نہ کرنے پر ثواب ملتا رہتا تو ہمیں ایک لحظہ میں نامعلوم کتنے ثواب ملتے مثلا ہم تو ضیح کا سبق پڑھ رہے ہیں اس وقت ہم نے زنا چھوڑا ہوا ہے اسی طرح چوری، ڈاکہ قتل غر ضیکہ کئی افعال بد ترک کیے بیٹھے ہیں تو ہمیں اتنے سارے افعال کیے نہ کرنے پر کئی ثواب ملنے چاہیے حالانکہ ایسا نہیں ہاں البتہ ایک صورت ترک حرام کی ہے جو قرآن مجید کی آیت میں بیان کی گئی ہے اس پر ثواب ہے وہ ہے کہ حرام مثلا زنا کے تمام تر اسباب حاصل ہو چکے ہیں: مزنیہ آمادہ ہے، کوئی دیکھنے والا نہیں اور جوانی و شباب بھی ہے اب ایک آدمی محض خدا کا خوف مد نظر رکھ کر زنا کو ترک کر دیتا ہے اس پر ثواب ہے لیکن یہ در حقیقت ترک حرام نہیں بلکہ فعل واجب ہے جس کو مصنف نے باب میں داخل فرمایا تھا جواب کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ ایک ہے "ترك الحرام " دوسرا نهى النفس عند تهيأ الاسباب و میلان النفس " یہ ایک مشکل ترین فعل ہے اس پر ثواب ہے پہلے پر ثواب نہیں۔
The commentator said: 'However, the first investigation is that he made leaving the forbidden something that is rewarded and not punished. It was objected that it is obligatory, and the obligatory is rewarded, and in the revelation (But as for he who fears the station of his Lord and forbids the soul from desire, indeed, Paradise will be the refuge). The answer is that what is rewarded is the performance of the obligatory, not the non-commission of the forbidden. Otherwise, every person would have many rewards every moment for every sin he does not commit. Forbidding the soul is restraining it from the forbidden, and it is of the type of performing the obligatory. There is no dispute that leaving the forbidden in the sense of restraining the soul when causes are ready and the soul inclines to it is rewarded.' Explanation: From here, the response to some objections against the author is intended. The first objection is that the author said regarding leaving the forbidden:'It is not rewarded and not punished.' Yet leaving zina (adultery) is obligatory, and every obligatory is rewarded, as in the Quran: 'As for him who fears the station of his Lord and forbids the soul from desire...' i.e., whoever fears standing before his Lord and restrains his soul from the forbidden, for him is Paradise. So this verse indicates that leaving the forbidden is rewarded, so how could the author say 'it is not rewarded and not punished'? The answer is that the author is correct: abandoning the forbidden does not earn reward; rather, reward is for performing the obligatory. If abandoning the forbidden always earned reward, we would receive countless rewards each moment—for example, while we are studying this lesson, we have refrained from zina, theft, robbery, murder, etc., yet we do not receive rewards for all these omissions. However, there is one type of leaving the forbidden mentioned in the Quran that is rewarded: when all means of the forbidden (e.g., zina) are present—the woman is willing, no one is watching, youth and desire are strong—and a person leaves it solely out of fear of God. This is rewarded, but it is not merely leaving the forbidden; it is actually performing an obligatory act, which the author included in the chapter. The summary: one is 'tark al-haram' (simple abandonment) and the other is 'nahy al-nafs 'inda tahayyu' al-asbab wa maylan al-nafs' (restraining the soul when causes and inclination are present). The latter is a most difficult act and is rewarded; the former is not rewarded.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) about whether abandoning the forbidden (tark al-haram) earns reward. The author of the matn had stated that leaving the forbidden is neither rewarded nor punished. An objection is raised: leaving zina is obligatory (wajib), and all wajib acts bring reward; the Quranic verse (79:40-41) is cited as proof. The commentator (sharh) clarifies the Hanafi position: simple abandonment (i.e., not committing sin when there is no temptation) does not earn reward, because reward is for positive acts of obedience, not for mere inaction. If it did, every person would constantly be earning unimaginable rewards for all the sins they avoid each moment. However, there is a specific case—when one is tempted and actively restrains oneself out of fear of Allah—that is rewarded. But this is actually a wajib act (restraining the soul), not mere leaving. The Urdu tashrih illustrates with the example of zina: if all conditions are met and one still refrains, that is an act of worship deserving reward. Thus the apparent contradiction is resolved: the author's statement applies to simple non-commission, while the verse refers to active self-restraint, which is a different category.

Key Terms
ترك الحرام (tark al-haram): Simple non-commission of a forbidden act, without any internal struggle or temptation.
نهى النفس عن الهوى (nahy al-nafs 'an al-hawa): Actively restraining one's soul from desire when tempted, which is considered a wajib act and is rewarded.
واجب (wajib): An action whose performance is rewarded and whose omission is punishable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of 'leaving the forbidden' is rewarded?
A) Any instance of not committing a forbidden act
B) When one refrains from a sin despite strong temptation due to fear of Allah
C) When one has no desire or means to commit the sin
D) When one is compelled to leave the sin

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, reward for abandoning haram is contingent on active self-restraint (nahy al-nafs), not mere omission.

Defining Jawaz: Imkan Khas and Imkan 'Amm in Hanafi Usul
Refinement of jawaz definition using two types of possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-imkan al-khas and al-imkan al-'amm and apply each definition of jawaz correctly in legal analysis.

Core Idea
Jawaz is defined differently depending on what it opposes: against wujub it means both act and omission are not prohibited; against hurma it means only the act is not prohibited.

This commentary sharpens the definition of jawaz by introducing two types of possibility. Understanding this distinction prevents misapplication of the term permissibility, especially when dealing with acts that border on obligation or prohibition. The passage refines the classical Hanafi position by showing that jawaz is not a single concept but must be understood contextually.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts for jawaz
Note that the definition of jawaz changes based on whether it is contrasted with wujub (obligation) or hurma (prohibition). The commentator specifies: for opposing wujub, jawaz means absence of prohibition of both act and omission; for opposing hurma, jawaz means absence of prohibition of the act only.
This corresponds to imkan khas and imkan 'amm respectively.
2
Define imkan khas and imkan 'amm
From the Urdu linguistic investigation: imkan khas is where doing and not doing are equally possible (neither necessary). Imkan 'amm is where only the act is possible (e.g., its omission may be necessary due to prohibition).
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objector points out that if jawaz meant absence of prohibition of both act and omission, then wajib acts (which are not prohibited to do) would be incorrectly called jā'iz. The response: the wording is specified by context to exclude what is already obligatory.
This shows the importance of contextual qarīna in usul.
4
Apply the distinction to a real legal example
Consider the act of eating during Ramadan in daytime. Under imkan 'amm, the act is not jā'iz because it is prohibited. Under imkan khas, it is also not jā'iz because omission (fasting) is obligatory. Thus the definition is context-specific.
This clarifies why a single-sentence definition of jawaz is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : " الثاني أن المراد بالجواز عدم منع الفعل والترك على ما يناسب الإمكان الخاص ليقابل الوجوب وفى الخامس عدم منع الفعل على ما يناسب الإمكان العام ليقابل الحرمة فإن قلت : إذا أريد بالجواز عدم منع الفعل والترك لم يصح قوله ففعل ما سوى الحرام والمكروه تحريما وترك ما سوى الواجب يجوز لها لأن ما سوى الحرام والمكروه تحريما يشمل الواجب مع أنه لا يجوز بهذا المعنى كذا ترك ما سوى الواجب يشمل ترك الحرام والمكروه تحريما مع أنه لا يحوز بهذ المعنى قلت : إذا مخصوص بقرينة التصريح بدخوله فيما يجب عليه " لغوی تحقیق امکان دو قسم پر ہے امکان خاص امکان عام - امکان خاص جس میں سلب ضرورت جانبین سے ہو یعنی اس کا کرنا نہ کرنا یا ہونا نہ ہونا دونوں برابر ہو کوئی جانب ضروری و واجب نہ ہو۔ امکان عام وہ ہے
The commentator said: 'Second: the intended meaning of permissibility (jawaz) is the absence of prohibition of both the act and the omission in accordance with specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas) to correspond to obligation (wujub), and in the fifth [chapter] it is the absence of prohibition of the act in accordance with general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) to correspond to prohibition (hurma). If you say: When jawaz is intended as absence of prohibition of act and omission, his statement 'so acting upon other than the forbidden and the prohibitively disliked, and omitting other than the obligatory, is permissible' is not valid, because 'other than the forbidden and the prohibitively disliked' includes the obligatory, while it is not permissible in this sense. Similarly, 'omitting other than the obligatory' includes omitting the forbidden and the prohibitively disliked, while it is not permissible in this sense. I say: It is specified by the context indicating its inclusion in what is obligatory for him.' Linguistic investigation: Possibility is of two types: specific possibility (imkan khas) and general possibility (imkan 'amm). Specific possibility: where necessity is negated from both sides—i.e., doing and not doing, or being and not being, are equal; no side is necessary or obligatory. General possibility: [text incomplete]
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a definitional refinement of the term jawaz (permissibility) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator explains that jawaz is defined differently depending on whether it opposes wujub (obligation) or hurma (prohibition). To oppose wujub, jawaz means the act and its omission are both not prohibited, which corresponds to 'specific possibility' (imkan khas) where neither doing nor leaving is necessary. To oppose hurma (in the fifth chapter), jawaz means the act itself is not prohibited, corresponding to 'general possibility' (imkan 'amm) where only the act is possible, not necessarily the omission. A potential objection is raised: if jawaz means both act and omission are not prohibited, then the statement that 'acting upon what is not haram or makruh tahriman is permissible' would incorrectly include wajib acts (since wajib acts are not haram but are not permissible in the sense of being optional). Similarly, 'leaving what is not wajib' would include leaving haram, which is not permissible in the sense of being optional. The answer: the phrasing is understood from context to refer to matters that are not already obligatory or prohibited; thus it is specified to exclude those. The Urdu linguistic investigation then defines imkan khas as equal possibility of both act and omission (neither is necessary), and imkan 'amm as only the act being possible (omission may be necessary). This distinction is crucial for correctly applying the term jawaz in legal theory.

Key Terms
الامکان الخاص (al-imkan al-khas): Specific possibility: both doing and not doing are equally possible, with neither side being necessary or obligatory. Used to define jawaz in opposition to wujub.
الامکان العام (al-imkan al-'amm): General possibility: only the act is possible (i.e., it is not prohibited), while the omission may be necessary (e.g., in case of haram). Used to define jawaz in opposition to hurma.
عدم المنع (adam al-man'): Absence of prohibition; the core meaning of jawaz as defined in this passage.

D) Neither act nor omission is possible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of jawaz is context-sensitive: it means absence of prohibition of both act and omission when opposing wujub, and absence of prohibition of the act only when opposing hurma.
