Including Makruh Tahrimi in the Scope of Prohibition
Responds to an objection by redefining the scope of 'prohibited'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi usul term 'haram' in the phrase 'ma yuhramu alayha' encompasses both haram and makruh tahrimi, and apply the criterion of evidence strength to classify an action.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'prohibited' is defined broadly as 'forbidden', whether by definitive or probable evidence.

This passage from a commentary on Al-Tawdih wa al-Talweeh addresses a common objection: that the fifth category of the caster's classification ('what is prohibited for her') cannot include makruh tahrimi because makruh tahrimi is not strictly haram. The commentator clarifies that the term 'haram' here is used in a functional sense meaning 'forbidden', and that the distinction between haram and makruh tahrimi lies solely in the strength of the evidence (qat'i vs. zanni). Understanding this principle is key to navigating Hanafi rulings on prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Carefully read the objector's claim: that makruh tahrimi is excluded from the fifth category because it is neither permissible nor strictly haram. Note that the objector assumes 'haram' in the category refers only to the technical legal category.
The objector's reasoning is based on a narrow definition of 'haram'.
2
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator redefines 'haram' in the fifth category as 'what is forbidden' (man' 'an al-fi'l), extending its scope. He then explains that the difference between haram and makruh tahrimi is not in the prohibition itself but in the evidence: qat'i evidence yields haram, zanni evidence yields makruh tahrimi.
This is a key Hanafi usul move: the same essential meaning (prohibition) covers both categories.
3
Understand the definition shift
The term 'haram' in 'ma yuhramu alayha' is not the technical legal term but a broader verbal noun indicating any prohibition. This functional definition allows inclusion of both degrees.
In Hanafi usul, lafz can have a general meaning unless specified otherwise.
4
Apply the criterion of evidence strength
Given a specific action claimed to be prohibited, determine whether the evidence is qat'i (definitive) or zanni (probable). If qat'i, it is haram; if zanni, it is makruh tahrimi. Both fall under the category of prohibited actions.
This criterion is central to Hanafi classification of ahkam.

Source Text

تشریح
اس عبارت میں تیسرے اعتراض کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ پانچویں صورت اشمل تھی مصنف نے فرمایا تھا کہ سب اقسام کو شامل ہے حالانکہ مصنف کا دعوی شمول غلط ہے اس لیے کہ مکروہ تحریمی خارج ہے کیونکہ نہ تو وہ "بحوز" میں داخل کیا جاسکتا ہے جو کہ ظاہر ہے اور نہ "ما يحرم" میں کیونکہ حرام جدا چیز ہے اور مکروہ جدا ۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ پانچویں صورت "ما يحرم" میں حرام کا معنی ہے: " جس کا کرنا ممنوع ہو"۔ یہ اگر دلیل قطعی کے ساتھ ہے تو حرام اور اگر دلیل ظنی کے ساتھ ہو تو مکروہ تحریمی بہر حال "بحرم عليها" دونوں قسموں کو شامل ہے۔
Commentator said: "Third: That 'what is prohibited for her' in the fifth aspect, meaning prevention from action, includes haram and makruh tahrimi." Explanation: This passage is the answer to the third objection. The question is that the fifth form was comprehensive, the author said that it includes all categories, but the author's claim of inclusion is false because makruh tahrimi is excluded, since it cannot be included in 'what is permissible' which is obvious, nor in 'what is prohibited' because haram is a separate thing and makruh is separate. The answer is that in the fifth form 'what is prohibited' the meaning of haram is: 'that whose doing is forbidden'. If this is with definitive evidence then it is haram, and if with probable evidence then it is makruh tahrimi. In any case, 'prohibited for her' includes both categories.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The objector claimed that the fifth category (ما يحرم عليها) as defined by the author does not include makruh tahrimi because makruh tahrimi is neither permissible nor strictly haram. The commentator clarifies that the term 'haram' in the fifth category is used in a broader sense meaning 'forbidden' (منع عن الفعل). The distinction between haram and makruh tahrimi lies only in the strength of the evidence: definitive (قطعي) evidence makes it haram, probable (ظني) evidence makes it makruh tahrimi. Both are prohibited and thus included. The explanation follows the Hanafi view that makruh tahrimi is a lesser degree of prohibition.

Key Terms
حرام (harām): Prohibited action established by definitive evidence (dalīl qaṭʿī).
مكروه كراهة التحريم (makrūh karāhat al-taḥrīm): Prohibited action established by probable evidence (dalīl ẓannī).
ما يحرم عليها (mā yuhramu ʿalayhā): What is prohibited for her, used broadly to include both haram and makruh tahrimi.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the scope of 'prohibited' is determined by the essential meaning of forbiddance, not by the technical category of evidence strength; haram and makruh tahrimi both fall under it.

The Ambiguity of 'Ahkam' in Defining Fiqh
Objection to definitional ambiguity of 'ahkam'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ambiguity in the term 'ahkam' as used in the second definition of fiqh and evaluate the Hanafi critique of using ambiguous terms in definitions.

Core Idea
A definition must avoid ambiguous terms like 'ahkam' that leave the scope of rulings unspecified.

This passage examines an objection raised by the matn (author of the base text) against the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of practical shari' rulings'. The objection focuses on the word 'ahkam' (rulings) being ambiguous: it could mean all rulings, some specific rulings, or ambiguous rulings. In precise definitions, such ambiguity is not commendable. We will explore why this matters in Usul al-Fiqh and how it reflects the requirement that definitions be clear (mubayyan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Second Definition
The second definition of fiqh is 'knowledge of practical shari' rulings' (al-ʿilm bi-l-aḥkām al-ʿamaliyya al-sharʿiyya). This definition is attributed to some Shafi'is.
The Hanafi definition of fiqh includes a reference to detailed evidence, which this definition lacks.
2
Recognize the Ambiguity in 'Ahkam'
The term 'ahkam' (rulings) is ambiguous: it could refer to all rulings (including theological), some specific rulings (e.g., only practical), or ambiguous rulings. The definition does not specify which.
In Hanafi usul, a definition should be clear (mubayyan) and not carry multiple possible meanings without specification.
3
Understand Why Ambiguity is Problematic
Using a word with multiple possible meanings without clarification is not commendable (ghayr mustaḥsan) in definitions. It undermines the purpose of definition, which is to precisely delimit the concept.
This is a methodological principle in usul: definitions must be mubayyan (clear) and avoid tajammuʿ (ambiguity).
4
Note the Intended Clarification
The commentator hints that the intended meaning is 'what is for and against the mukallaf' (mā lahā wa mā ʿalayhā) - i.e., the rulings that obligate or forbid actions.
This clarifies the scope: fiqh is about practical rulings that apply to a morally responsible person.
5
Apply the Principle to Other Definitions
Consider how a Hanafi would define fiqh: 'knowledge of practical shari' rulings derived from detailed evidence.' This avoids ambiguity by specifying the source and the type of rulings.
The Hanafi definition includes 'from detailed evidence' (al-adilla al-tafṣīliyya), which removes the ambiguity about the basis of rulings.

Source Text

The Multiple Meanings of Hukm and Their Impact on Defining Fiqh
Commentator analyses the word 'hukm' in the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of hukm (linguistic, usuli, logical) and apply the correct one to avoid redundancy in the Hanafi definition of fiqh.

Core Idea
The correct meaning of hukm in fiqh's definition is the linguistic attribution, not the usuli or logical sense.

In this passage from Sharh al-Talwih, the commentator analyzes the word 'hukm' as it appears in the definition of fiqh: 'knowledge of the shar'i practical rulings from their detailed evidences.' He distinguishes three possible meanings and argues that only one—the linguistic meaning of attribution—makes the definition coherent without redundancy. Understanding this subtlety is crucial for grasping the precise scope of fiqh as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of hukm
List the linguistic (grammatical: attribution of one thing to another), usuli (Hanafi: Allah's address concerning actions), and logical (mantiqi: perception of relation = tasdiq) definitions.
The linguistic meaning is also called the 'common usage of grammarians'.
2
Test each meaning in the definition of fiqh
Substitute each meaning of hukm into 'knowledge of shar'i practical rulings from their detailed evidences' and see if it causes logical problems or redundancy.
3
Analyze the redundancy argument
Explain why the usuli meaning makes 'shar'i' and 'practical' redundant: 'Allah's address' already implies shar'i, and 'connected to actions' already implies practical.
4
Understand the commentator's preferred solution
Show that the linguistic meaning (attribution) avoids redundancy and aligns with the author's phrase 'it removes conceptualizations and leaves assertions.'
With this meaning, 'knowledge' in the definition becomes 'tasdiq' (assertion), and fiqh is defined as assertion of shar'i propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالأحكام الشرعية العملية من أدلتها التفصيلة .
فوائد قیود
الاحکام" کی قید سے علم ذات و صفات خارج ہو گیا یعنی جو محض تصورات ہوں ان میں حکم نہ ہو۔ الشرعية " کی قید سے غیر شرعی احکام، مثلا، علی جیسے العالم حادث ، یاسی، جیسے: النار محرقة (آگ جلانے والی ہے ) خارج ہو گئے ، یا اصطلاحی و وضعی احکام، جیسے: "كل فاعل مرفوع" یہ سب خارج ہو گئے اور عملیہ کی قید سے احکام نظریہ جن کو اعتقادیہ واصلیہ کہا جاتا ہے خارج ہو گئے جیسے الإجماع حجة الايمان بالله واجب کیونکہ ان کا محل بیان علم الکلام ہوتا ہے " من ادلتها " کی قید سے علم اللہ اور علم جبریل اور علم رسول اللہ نکل گئے، کیونکہ ان کو اگر چہ احکام شرعیہ کا علم تو ہوتا ہے مگر وہ کسی دلیل سے مستقبط شدہ نہیں ہوتا اسی طرح علم مقلد بھی خارج ہو جائے گا کیونکہ اس کو فقہ کے ہر مسئلہ کی ولیل تفصیلی معلوم نہیں ہوتی بلکہ وہ صرف اپنے امام کے قول پر اعتماد کرتا ہے۔
قال الشارح : قوله : يمكن أن يراد بالحكم ... إلخ الحكم يطلق في العرف على إسناد أمر إلى أمر آخر أى نسبته إليه بالإيجاب أو السلب ، وفي اصطلاح الأصول على خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين بالاقتصاء أو التخيير ، وفي اصطلاح المنطق على إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة ويسمى تصديقا وهو ليس بمراد ههنا لأنه علم والفقه ليس علما بالعلوم الشرعية ، والمحققون على أن الثاني أيضا ليس بمراد وإلا لكان ذكر الشرعية والعملية تكرارا بل المراد النسبة التامة بين الأمرين التي العلم بها تصديق و بغيرها تصور . وإلى هذا أشار بقوله يخرج التصورات ويبقى التصديقات ، فيكون الفقه عبارة عن التصديق بالقضايا الشرعية المتعلقة بكيفية العمل تصديقا حاصلا من الأدلة التفصيلة التي نصبت في الشرع على تلك القضايا . وفوائد القيود ظاهرة على هذا التقدير .
تشریح
بعض شوافع کی طرف سے جو ماتن نے فقہ کی تعریف نقل کی تھی اس میں "حکم کا لفظ موجود ہے یہاں سے یہ بتلانا چاہتے ہیں کہ حکم کتنے معانی کے لیے آتا ہے اور یہاں پر کونسا معنی زیادہ موزوں ہو سکتا ہے چنانچہ فرماتے ہیں کہ تحویوں کی عرف میں حکم کا معنی ہے "استاد أمر إلى أمر آخر یعنی ایک لفظ کی دوسرے لفظ کی طرف نسبت ہوا ایجاب یا سلبا، جیسے: زید قائم ،زید لیس بقائم . اصولیین کے نزدیک حکم کا معنی ہے "خطاب المسله ... الخ یعنی اللہ تعالی کا وہ خطاب جو ہندوں کے افعال کے ساتھ تعلق پکڑے خواہ اس میں کسی کام کے کرنے کا تقاضا ہو یا اختیار دیا گیا ہو۔ مناطقہ کی اصطلاح میں "الادراك بـــــان النسبة واقعة أو ليس بواقعة" یعنی یہ جانا کہ نسبت واقع ہونے والی ہے یا واقع ہونے والی نہیں اس کو تصدیق بھی کہتے ہیں۔ مناطقہ والا معنی تو مراد نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ اس وقت حکم اور تصدیق ایک ہی چیز بنتی ہے۔ اور تصدیق علم کی قسم ہے لہذا اس وقت فقہ کی تعریف " هو العلم بالاحكام ... إلخ" کا مطلب ہوگا ھو العلم بالعلوم ... الخ " جو کہ غلط ہے، بلکہ محققین فرماتے ہیں کہ ثانی معنی "خطاب الله ... إلخ " یعنی اصطلاح اصولیین مراد لینا بھی درست نہیں کیونکہ اس وقت فقہ کی تعریف میں لفظ شرعیہ اور عملیہ دونوں تکرار بنتے ہیں وجہ یہ کہ خطاب اللہ کے لفظ سے شرعیہ کی طرف اشارہ ہے اور المتعلق بافعال المكلفين میں افعال اور اعمال کی تذکرہ بھی ہو جاتا ہے لہذا اس تکرار سے بچنے کے لیے نحویوں کا معنی اسناد امرالی أمر آخر والا مراد لیتا زیادہ اولی ہے۔ جب حکم بمعنی نسبت بین امرین ہو تو فقہ کی تعریف میں جو لفظ علم کا موجود ہے بمعنی تصدیق کے ہو جائے گا اس لیے اب فقہ کی تعریف یوں ہوگی هو التصديق بالأحكام (القضايا الشرعية المتعلقة بكيقية العمل تصديقا حاصلا من الادلة التفصيلية التي نصبت في الشرع على تلك القضايا (الاحکام ) چونکہ احکام بمعنی قضایا ہیں اس لیے مصنف نے فرمایا تھا یہ حرج التصورات ويبقى التصديقات اگر حکم کا معنی نحوی مراد لیا جائے تو اس وقت فقہ کی تعریف میں پائے جانے والے قودات کا فوائد بالکل ظاہر ہیں کسی قسم کا خلجان واقع نہیں ہوتا۔
قال الشارح : "والمصنف جوز أن يراد بالأحكام ههنا مصطلح الأصول فاحتاج إلى تكلف في تبيين فوائد القيود ، وتعسف في تقرير مراد القوم ،فذهب إلى أن المراد بالشرعية ما يتوقف على الشرع ولا يدرك لولا خطاب الشارع . والأحكام منها ما هو خطاب بما يتوقف على الشرع كوجوب الصلوة ، ومنها ما هو خطاب بما لا يتوقف عليه ، كوجوب الإيمان بالله ، ووجوب التصديق بالنبى صلى الله عليه وسلم ؛ لأن ثبوت
By the shar'i, practical rulings from their detailed evidences. Benefits of the restrictions: The restriction 'rulings' excludes the knowledge of essence and attributes, i.e., mere concepts have no ruling. The restriction 'shar'i' excludes non-shar'i rulings, e.g., like 'the world is created', or like 'fire burns', or conventional and positive rulings like 'every doer is nominative'. The restriction 'practical' excludes theoretical rulings called creedal or fundamental, like 'consensus is a proof', 'belief in Allah is obligatory', because their locus is the science of kalam. The restriction 'from their detailed evidences' excludes the knowledge of Allah, Jibril, and the Messenger, because even though they have knowledge of shar'i rulings, it is not derived from evidence. Similarly, the knowledge of a muqallid is excluded because he does not know the detailed evidence for every issue, but relies on his imam's statement. The commentator said: His statement: 'It is possible that by hukm is meant...' etc. In common usage, hukm is applied to attributing one thing to another, i.e., relating it positively or negatively. In usul terminology, it is Allah's address connected to the actions of the responsible persons by requirement or option. In logic terminology, it is the perception that the relation exists or does not exist, called tasdiq. This is not intended here because it is knowledge, and fiqh is not knowledge of shar'i sciences. The verifiers hold that the second (usuli meaning) is also not intended, otherwise mentioning 'shar'i' and 'practical' would be redundant. Rather, the intended is the complete relation between two things, knowledge of which is tasdiq and without it is tasawwur. To this he pointed by his statement 'it excludes conceptualizations and leaves assertions', so fiqh is assertion of shar'i propositions related to the manner of action, an assertion derived from the detailed evidences established in the Law for those propositions. The benefits of the restrictions are apparent on this estimation. Explanation: Some Shafi'is from whom the matn transmitted the definition of fiqh [said] the word 'hukm' is present. From here they want to explain how many meanings hukm carries and which meaning is most suitable here. Thus they state that in the common usage of grammarians, the meaning of hukm is attribution of one thing to another, i.e., relating it positively or negatively, like 'Zayd is standing', 'Zayd is not standing'. According to usuliyyun, the meaning of hukm is 'address of Allah...' i.e., Allah's address connected to the actions of the servants, whether it demands an act or gives option. In logic terminology, 'the perception that the relation exists or does not exist' i.e., knowing that the relation is occurring or not occurring; this is also called tasdiq. The logical meaning cannot be intended because then hukm and tasdiq become the same thing, and tasdiq is a type of knowledge, so the definition 'knowledge of rulings...' would mean 'knowledge of sciences...' which is incorrect. Rather, the verifiers say that taking the second meaning (usuli terminology) is also not correct, because then in the definition of fiqh the words 'shar'i' and 'practical' become redundant. The reason is that the word 'address of Allah' indicates 'shar'i', and 'connected to actions of the responsible persons' already mentions actions and deeds. Therefore, to avoid this redundancy, taking the grammarians' meaning of 'attribution of one thing to another' is more appropriate. When hukm means relation between two things, then the word 'knowledge' in the definition of fiqh becomes meaning tasdiq. So the definition of fiqh becomes: 'assertion of rulings (shar'i propositions related to the manner of action), an assertion derived from the detailed evidences established in the Law for those propositions.' Since 'rulings' means propositions, the author said 'this removes conceptualizations and leaves assertions'. If the grammatical meaning of hukm is taken, then the benefits of the restrictions in the definition of fiqh are completely apparent, no confusion occurs. The commentator said: 'And the author allowed that by rulings here is meant the usuli terminology, so he needed to exert effort in explaining the benefits of the restrictions, and went to extremes in establishing the intended meaning of the people. He held that by shar'i is meant that which depends on the Law and cannot be known without the address of the Lawgiver. And rulings include that which is address concerning what depends on the Law, like the obligation of prayer, and that which is address concerning what does not depend on it, like the obligation of belief in Allah and the obligation of affirming the Prophet (peace be upon him), because the establishment...'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of fiqh, specifically analyzing the word 'hukm' in the phrase 'knowledge of the shar'i practical rulings from their detailed evidences' (العلم بالأحكام الشرعية العملية من أدلتها التفصيلية). The commentator, Sharh al-Talwih, explains the different meanings of 'hukm' and which one is intended in this definition to avoid logical problems and redundancy.

The passage begins by listing the benefits (fawa'id) of the restrictions (quyud) in the matn definition. Each restriction excludes certain things:
- 'rulings' (الأحكام) excludes mere concepts (tasawwurat) which have no ruling.
- 'shar'i' (الشرعية) excludes non-shar'i judgments, like 'the world is created' (a rational judgment) or 'fire burns' (a natural law), and also excludes conventional or grammatical rulings like 'every doer is nominative'.
- 'practical' (العملية) excludes theoretical beliefs like 'consensus is a proof' or 'belief in Allah is obligatory', which belong to kalam, not fiqh.
- 'from their detailed evidences' (من أدلتها التفصيلية) excludes the knowledge of Allah, angels, prophets, and also the muqallid (blind follower) who does not know the detailed evidence.

Then the commentator discusses the word 'hukm' itself. In common ('urfi) usage, hukm means attributing one thing to another positively or negatively (like 'Zayd is standing'). In usul al-fiqh, it means Allah's address concerning the actions of responsible persons by requirement or option. In logic, it is the perception that a relation exists or does not exist (called tasdiq).

The commentator argues that neither the logical nor the usuli meaning fits the definition of fiqh without problems. If 'hukm' is taken logically, then 'knowledge of rulings' becomes 'knowledge of sciences', which is incorrect because fiqh is not just any knowledge. If taken usuli, the words 'shar'i' and 'practical' become redundant because 'address of Allah' already implies 'shar'i' and 'actions of the responsible persons' implies 'practical'. Therefore, the correct meaning is the linguistic/grammatical meaning: the relation between two things. With this meaning, 'knowledge' in the definition becomes 'tasdiq' (assertion), and fiqh is defined as 'assertion of shar'i propositions related to the manner of action, derived from detailed evidences.' This avoids redundancy and fits the author's statement 'it removes conceptualizations and leaves assertions' (يخرج التصورات ويبقى التصديقات).

Finally, the commentator notes that the matn author (al-Taftazani) allowed the usuli meaning but had to exert effort (takalluf) to explain the benefits of the restrictions, which is less straightforward.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): In this passage, hukm is analyzed in three senses: (1) Linguistic: attribution of one thing to another (إسناد أمر إلى أمر آخر). (2) Usuli: Allah's address concerning actions of the responsible persons. (3) Logical: perception that a relation exists or not (tasdiq). The intended sense in the definition of fiqh is the linguistic one to avoid redundancy.
تصديق (tasdiq): In logic, it is the perception that a relation exists or does not exist. In the definition of fiqh, it is used to mean 'assertion' of a proposition, which is the result of taking hukm linguistically as a complete relation.
تصور (tasawwur): A mere concept without affirmation or negation. The definition of fiqh 'removes conceptualizations' (يخرج التصورات) meaning it deals only with asserted propositions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the word 'hukm' mean in the definition of fiqh?
A) Allah's address concerning actions of the responsible persons
B) Attribution of one thing to another (linguistic meaning)
C) Perception that a relation exists or does not exist (logical meaning)
D) A command or prohibition

Key Takeaway
To define fiqh precisely, 'hukm' must be taken in its linguistic sense as a complete relation, allowing the definition to mean 'assertion of shar'i propositions.'

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

8 min
The Meaning of Hukm and Its Impact on Defining Fiqh
Analyzing the meaning of hukm to avoid tautology in defining fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of hukm (grammatical, usuli, logical) and evaluate which is most appropriate for the definition of fiqh according to the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Choosing the wrong meaning of hukm leads to redundancy in defining fiqh; the grammatical sense gives the cleanest definition.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the ahkam' hinges on the meaning of 'hukm'. This lesson examines three possible interpretations and the legal reasoning that leads the Hanafi school to prefer the grammatical meaning of isnad (attribution). Understanding this choice clarifies the structure of fiqh as a discipline of propositions (tasdiqat) rather than mere concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
List the three meanings of hukm: grammatical (isnad - attribution), usuli (khitab Allah - divine address), and logical (tasdiq - assent). Understand each definition and its domain.
2
Reject the logical meaning
If hukm means tasdiq, then 'ilm in the definition of fiqh becomes 'knowledge of tasdiq', i.e., 'knowledge of knowledge', which is absurd. The logical meaning is thus excluded.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli).
3
Critique the usuli meaning
If hukm means khitab Allah (divine address), then the qualifiers 'shar'iyyah' and 'amaliyyah' become redundant: 'khitab Allah' already implies shar'iyyah, and 'related to actions' already implies amaliyyah. This redundancy is only avoidable by strained interpretation.
The commentator argues this leads to takalluf (unnecessary effort).
4
Accept the grammatical meaning
If hukm is taken as isnad (relation between two things), then 'ilm in the definition becomes tasdiq (assent to those relations). The definition becomes 'tasdiq of the shar'i propositions', with no redundancy and full clarity.
This is the preferred Hanafi position.

ہے چنانچہ فرماتے ہیں کہ تحویوں کی عرف میں حکم کا معنی ہے "استاد أمر إلى أمر آخر یعنی ایک لفظ کی دوسرے لفظ کی طرف نسبت ہوا ایجاب یا سلبا، جیسے: زید قائم ،زید لیس بقائم . اصولیین کے نزدیک حکم کا معنی ہے "خطاب المسله ... الخ یعنی اللہ تعالی کا وہ خطاب جو ہندوں کے افعال کے ساتھ تعلق پکڑے خواہ اس میں کسی کام کے کرنے کا تقاضا ہو یا اختیار دیا گیا ہو۔ مناطقہ کی اصطلاح میں ”الادراك بـــــان النسبة واقعة أو ليس بواقعة“ یعنی یہ جانا کہ نسبت واقع ہونے والی ہے یا واقع ہونے والی نہیں اس کو تصدیق بھی کہتے ہیں۔ مناطقہ والا معنی تو مراد نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ اس وقت حکم اور تصدیق ایک ہی چیز بنتی ہے۔ اور تصدیق علم کی قسم ہے لہذا اس وقت فقہ کی تعریف ” هو العلم بالاحكام ... إلخ“ کا مطلب ہوگا ھو العلم بالعلوم ... الخ " جو کہ غلط ہے، بلکہ محققین فرماتے ہیں کہ ثانی معنی "خطاب الله ... إلخ " یعنی اصطلاح اصولیین مراد لینا بھی درست نہیں کیونکہ اس وقت فقہ کی تعریف میں لفظ شرعیہ اور عملیہ دونوں تکرار بنتے ہیں وجہ یہ کہ خطاب اللہ کے لفظ سے شرعیہ کی طرف اشارہ ہے اور المتعلق بافعال المكلفين میں افعال اور اعمال کی تذکرہ بھی ہو جاتا ہے لہذا اس تکرار سے بچنے کے لیے نحویوں کا معنی اسناد امرالی أمر آخر والا مراد لیتا زیادہ اولی ہے۔ جب حکم بمعنی نسبت بین امرین ہو تو فقہ کی تعریف میں جو لفظ علم کا موجود ہے بمعنی تصدیق کے ہو جائے گا اس لیے اب فقہ کی تعریف یوں ہوگی هو التصديق بالأحكام (القضايا الشرعية المتعلقة بكيقية العمل تصديقا حاصلا من الادلة التفصيلية التي نصبت في الشرع على تلك القضايا (الاحکام ) چونکہ احکام بمعنی قضایا ہیں اس لیے مصنف نے فرمایا تھا یہ حرج التصورات ويبقى التصديقات اگر حکم کا معنی نحوی مراد لیا جائے تو اس وقت فقہ کی تعریف میں پائے جانے والے قودات کا فوائد بالکل ظاہر ہیں کسی قسم کا خلجان واقع نہیں ہوتا۔
قال الشارح : "والمصنف جوز أن يراد بالأحكام ههنا مصطلح الأصول فاحتاج إلى تكلف في تبيين فوائد القيود ، وتعسف في تقرير مراد القوم ،فذهب إلى أن المراد بالشرعية ما يتوقف على الشرع ولا يدرك لولا خطاب الشارع . والأحكام منها ما هو خطاب بما يتوقف على الشرع كوجوب الصلوة ، ومنها ما هو خطاب بما لا يتوقف عليه ، كوجوب الإيمان بالله ، ووجوب التصديق بالنبى صلى الله عليه وسلم ؛ لأن ثبوت الشرع موقوف على الإيمان بوجود البارى تعالى وعلمه، وقدرته، وكلامه ، وعلى التصديق بنبوة النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم بدلالة معجزاته فلو توقف شئ من هذه الأحكام على الشرع لزم الدور فالتقييد بالشرعية يخرج هذه الأحكام؛ لأنها ليست شرعية بمعنى التوقف على الشرع .
تشریح
یہاں سے ماتن پر اعتراض مقصود ہے کہ ماتن نے حکم کا معنی اصطلاح اصولیین والا مراد لے کر فوائد قیود بیان کرنے میں خواہ مخواہ تکلف کیا اور جمہور کی مراد بیان کرنے میں تعسف (بے جا تکلف ) اختیار کیا ہے مثلا احکام شرعیہ کی دو قسم کیں (۱) شرعیہ جو شریعت پر موقوف ہوں اگر شریعت وارد نہ ہوتی تو ہمیں معلوم نہ ہو سکتے جس طرح وجوب الصلاة والصوم (۲) غیر شرعی اس معنی کہ ان کا وجوب شریعت پر موقوف نہ ہو بلکہ شریعت کا ثبوت ان پر موقوف ہو مثلا وجوب الایمان بالله اور وجوب التصديق بالنبی صلی الله علیه وسلم کیونکہ شریعت کا ثبوت ایمان بوجود الباری تعالی پر موقوف ہے اب اگر ثبوت ایمان بوجود الباری شریعت پر موقوف ہو تو دور ہے جو کہ محال ہوتا ہے الغرض شرعیہ کی قید لگا کر اس قسم کے احکام کو نکالا ہے جو شریعت پر موقوف نہیں مگر جمہور کے نزد یک شرعیہ کا معنی ہوتا ہے ما ورد به خطاب الشارع اس معنی پر کوئی تکلف نہیں لہذا مصنف نے جو شرعیہ کا معنی جمہور کے خلاف کیا ہے وہ تکلف ہے۔
قال الشارح : وإنما قال الخطاب بما يتوقف أو لا يتوقف لأن الحكم المفسر بالخطاب قيد عندهم فكيف يتوقف على الشرع . ولقائل أن يمنع توقف الشرع على وجوب الإيمان بالله ، وصفاته ، وعلى التصديق بنبوة النبى صلى الله عليه وسلم ، ودلالة معجزاته لا يتوقف توقفه على وجوب الإيمان والتصديق ولا على العلم بوجوبها غايته أنه يتوقف على نفس الإيمان والتصديق وهو غير مفيد ، ولا مناف لتوقف وجوب الإيمان ونحوه على الشرع كما هو المذهب عندهم من أن لا وجوب إلا بالسمع “ .
تشریح
Thus they say: In the terminology of grammarians, hukm means 'attributing one thing to another' i.e., relating one word to another either affirmatively or negatively, such as 'Zaid is standing', 'Zaid is not standing'. According to the usuliyyun, hukm means 'the address of Allah...' i.e., Allah's address that relates to the actions of the servants, whether it requires something to be done or gives a choice. In the terminology of logicians, it is 'the perception that the relation exists or does not exist', also called tasdiq. The logicians' meaning cannot be intended because then hukm and tasdiq become the same, and tasdiq is a type of knowledge. Then the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the ahkam...' would become 'knowledge of the sciences...' which is wrong. Rather, the investigators say that taking the second meaning 'the address of Allah...' i.e., the usuli terminology is also not correct because then in the definition of fiqh, the words 'shar'iyyah' and 'amaliyyah' both become redundant: the word 'khitab Allah' already indicates shar'iyyah, and 'related to the actions of the servants' also mentions actions. So to avoid this redundancy, taking the grammarians' meaning of isnad is more appropriate. When hukm means relation between two things, then the word 'ilm' in the definition of fiqh takes the meaning of tasdiq. So the definition of fiqh becomes: tasdiq of the ahkam (shar'i propositions relating to the manner of action), a tasdiq derived from the detailed proofs established in the Shari'ah for those propositions. Since ahkam means propositions, the author said: 'this removes the tasawwurat and leaves the tasdiqat.' If the meaning of hukm is taken grammatically, then the benefits of the restrictions in the definition of fiqh are entirely clear; no confusion occurs.
The commentator said: 'The author allowed that by ahkam here might be meant the usuli terminology, so he needed unnecessary effort in explaining the benefits of the restrictions and strained in interpreting the intent of the scholars. He held that by shar'iyyah is meant what depends on the Shari'ah and cannot be perceived without the address of the Lawgiver. Among the ahkam are those that are an address regarding what depends on the Shari'ah, like the obligation of prayer, and those that are an address regarding what does not depend on it, like the obligation of faith in Allah and the obligation of believing in the Prophet (peace be upon him), because the establishment of the Shari'ah depends on faith in the existence of the Exalted Creator, His knowledge, power, and speech, and on believing in the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him) through the indication of his miracles. If any of these ahkam depended on the Shari'ah, there would be circularity. So the restriction by shar'iyyah excludes these ahkam because they are not shar'iyyah in the sense of depending on the Shari'ah.'
Elaboration: From here, the objection against the matn is intended: that the author took the meaning of hukm as the usuli terminology, and in explaining the benefits of the restrictions he unnecessarily exerted effort and strained in presenting the intent of the majority. For example, he divided shar'i ahkam into two types: (1) those that depend on the Shari'ah and would not be known if the Shari'ah had not come, like the obligation of prayer and fasting; (2) those that are not shar'i in this sense because their obligation does not depend on the Shari'ah, but rather the Shari'ah's establishment depends on them, like the obligation of faith in Allah and the obligation of believing in the Prophet (peace be upon him). Because the Shari'ah's establishment depends on faith in the existence of the Exalted Creator. If the existence of faith in the Exalted Creator depended on the Shari'ah, there would be circularity, which is impossible. In short, by the restriction of shar'iyyah, he excluded those ahkam that do not depend on the Shari'ah. But according to the majority, shar'iyyah means what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver. In this meaning, there is no effort. So the author's interpretation of shar'iyyah contrary to the majority is an effort.
The commentator said: He only said 'the address regarding what depends or does not depend' because hukm, defined as address, is a restriction according to them, so how can it depend on the Shari'ah? One can object that the Shari'ah's dependence on the obligation of faith in Allah and His attributes, and on believing in the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the indication of his miracles, does not depend on the obligation of faith and belief, nor on knowledge of their obligation. At most, it depends on faith and belief themselves, which is not relevant and does not contradict the dependence of the obligation of faith and the like on the Shari'ah, as is their position that there is no obligation except by revelation.'
Elaboration.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the meaning of the term 'hukm' (حكم) in the context of defining 'fiqh' as 'knowledge of the ahkam'. It begins by presenting three possible meanings: the grammatical (nahwi) meaning of isnad (attribution), the usuli meaning as Allah's address (khitab), and the logical meaning as tasdiq (assent). The logical meaning is rejected because it equates hukm with tasdiq, making the definition of fiqh tautological (knowledge of knowledge). The usuli meaning is also critiqued because it causes redundancy in the definition: 'khitab Allah' already implies shar'iyyah, and 'related to actions of the servants' already implies amaliyyah. The author (matn) attempted to justify the usuli meaning by further restricting 'shar'iyyah' to matters dependent on revelation, thereby excluding obligations like belief in God which are preconditions for the Shari'ah itself. However, the commentator (sharh) argues that this leads to unnecessary complexity (takalluf) and deviates from the majority (jumhur) who define shar'iyyah simply as that which comes via the address of the Lawgiver. The commentator prefers the grammatical meaning of isnad, which avoids all these issues: if hukm is a relation, then 'ilm (knowledge) in the definition of fiqh becomes tasdiq (assent) of those relations, and the definition is consistent and clear. The elaboration (tashrih) further clarifies that the author's attempt to distinguish between ahkam dependent on the Shari'ah and those not dependent (like obligation of belief) introduces a circularity issue, but the commentator rebuts that the Shari'ah's dependence is on the actual faith, not on the obligation of faith, so the objection fails. Ultimately, the preferred view is that hukm in the definition of fiqh should be taken in the grammatical sense to achieve conceptual clarity and avoid redundancy.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): A term with multiple meanings: (1) Grammatical: attribution of one thing to another (isnad). (2) Usuli: Allah's address concerning the actions of the servants. (3) Logical: assent (tasdiq) that a relation exists or not.
تصديق (tasdiq): Assent; the mental judgment that a proposition is true or false. In logic, it is the act of affirming or denying a relation.
شرعية (shar'iyyah): That which is derived from or dependent on the Shari'ah (divine law). The author defines it as what depends on revelation, while the majority define it as what is conveyed by the Lawgiver's address.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the grammatical (nahwi) meaning of 'hukm' preferred in the definition of fiqh according to the commentator?
A) Because it is the only meaning accepted by logicians.
B) Because it avoids redundancy of 'shar'iyyah' and 'amaliyyah' in the definition.
C) Because it makes fiqh a branch of grammar.
D) Because it is the meaning used by the author of the matn.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of hukm in the definition of fiqh must be grammatical (isnad) to avoid redundancy; otherwise, the definition becomes tautological or requires forced interpretations.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Defining Shar'i Rulings: Avoiding Circularity
Defining shari'i rulings to avoid circular reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'hukm shar'i' according to the matn of al-Talwih, explain the circularity objection it avoids, and compare it with the Jumhur definition.

Core Idea
A shari'i ruling depends on Shari'ah; prerequisites for Shari'ah are not shari'i — avoiding circularity.

This lesson unpacks the definition of shar'i rulings (ahkam shar'iyya) as presented in the matn of al-Talwih. The author deliberately restricts 'shar'i' to rulings whose knowledge depends on revelation, excluding foundational beliefs like the existence of God and prophethood, for without this restriction a logical circle would arise. The commentary then defends this position against the simpler definition of the majority (Jumhur) and classifies shar'i rulings into theoretical (nazari) and practical (amali).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage defines 'shar'i' rulings. The central problem is: what exactly makes a ruling 'shar'i'? If we define it too broadly, we may fall into circular reasoning (dawr).
Dawr occurs if A depends on B, and B depends on A.
2
Understand the matn's definition
According to the author (matn), 'shar'i' means 'that which depends on the Shari'ah' (ma yatawaqquf 'ala al-shar'). This excludes rulings that are prerequisites for the Shari'ah itself, such as the obligation of belief in Allah and prophethood.
If belief in Allah depended on Shari'ah, and Shari'ah depends on belief, circularity arises.
3
Grasp the circularity argument
The Shari'ah is founded on belief in Allah and His messengers. If the obligation of that belief were itself a shar'i ruling (dependent on Shari'ah), then Shari'ah would depend on belief, and belief would depend on Shari'ah — a vicious circle. Hence the matn excludes such obligations from 'shar'i'.
The matn's definition is a methodological move to preserve logical consistency.
4
Contrast with Jumhur (majority) definition
The Jumhur define 'shar'i' simply as 'that which is addressed by the Lawgiver' (ma warada bihi khitab al-shari'). Under this, obligations of belief are included, and no special exclusion is needed. The commentator notes that the matn's view is more precise, though the Jumhur's is simpler.
The disagreement is over the scope of 'shar'i' and the necessity of avoiding circularity.
5
Apply the classification: nazari vs. amali
Shar'i rulings are then divided into theoretical (nazari) — not related to action, e.g., 'ijma is a proof' — and practical (amali), related to action like prayer and fasting. The restriction 'amali' in the matn's later discussion excludes theoretical rulings from the practical branch of fiqh.
Theoretical rulings belong to usul al-fiqh; practical rulings to furu' fiqh.

Maktaba Tul Ishaat.com
Commentary on al-Talwih: The Shari'ah is dependent on belief in the existence of the Most High, His knowledge, His power, and His speech, and on affirming the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him) by the evidence of his miracles. If any of these rulings were dependent on the Shari'ah, circularity would result. So restricting with 'shar'i' excludes these rulings because they are not shar'i in the sense of being dependent on the Shari'ah. Explanation: From here, an objection to the author (matn) is intended: the author took the meaning of 'hukm' as the terminology of the usuliyyun and unnecessarily exerted effort in stating the benefits of restrictions, and adopted harshness in explaining the meaning of the majority. For example, he divided shar'i rulings into two types: (1) Shar'i – that which depends on the Shari'ah; if the Shari'ah had not come, we could not know them, like the obligation of prayer and fasting. (2) Non-shar'i – meaning their obligation does not depend on the Shari'ah, but rather the Shari'ah's establishment depends on them, like the obligation of belief in Allah and the obligation of affirming the Prophet (peace be upon him) because the establishment of the Shari'ah depends on belief in the existence of the Most High. Now, if belief in the existence of the Most High were dependent on the Shari'ah, circularity would occur, which is impossible. In short, by adding the restriction 'shar'i', he excluded these rulings which are not dependent on the Shari'ah. However, according to the majority, 'shar'i' means what is addressed by the address of the Lawgiver. There is no difficulty in this meaning. Hence, the author's interpretation of 'shar'i' contrary to the majority is forced. The commentator said: Rather, he said 'the address that depends or does not depend' because 'hukm' explained by 'address' is a restriction according to them, so how can it depend on the Shari'ah? One may object that the Shari'ah's dependence on the obligation of belief in Allah and His attributes, and on affirming the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the evidence of his miracles, does not depend on the obligation of belief and affirmation nor on the knowledge of their obligation. At most, it depends on belief and affirmation itself, which is not useful, nor does it contradict the dependence of the obligation of belief and similar on the Shari'ah, as is their doctrine that there is no obligation except through revelation. Explanation: First, some clarification of the author's text: 'hukm' meaning 'the address of Allah' etc. is eternal, and the eternal cannot depend on another. Therefore, the author did not define 'al-hukm al-shar'i' as 'that which depends on the Shari'ah', but rather as 'the address that depends on the Shari'ah', because 'hukm' meaning 'the address of Allah' is eternal, which cannot depend on another. Similarly, the meaning of 'ghayr shar'i' is 'the address that does not depend on the Shari'ah'. Then, from 'one may object...' etc., it is intended to object to the author: the author's division into shar'i and ghayr shar'i to avoid circularity is unnecessary, because 'shar'i' meaning 'the address of Allah' or meaning 'the Muhammadan Shari'ah' in any case depends on belief itself, not on the obligation of belief. Therefore, if the obligation of belief were dependent on the Shari'ah, as some hold that believing is not obligatory until the Shari'ah comes, no circularity would occur, because the establishment of the Shari'ah depends on belief itself, and the obligation of belief depends on the Shari'ah itself; thus the direction of dependence changed, so circularity will not occur. The commentator said: His statement 'then al-shar'i' means that which depends on the Shari'ah, either theoretical – i.e., not related to the modality of action, or practical – related to it. So restricting with 'amaliyya' excludes theoretical ones like 'ijma' is a proof'. This is valid only on the second interpretation if the technical term 'hukm' includes the theoretical – and there is discussion about it. Explanation: From here, he indicates the explanation of the author's text and then the objection. He says: The author made 'shar'i' meaning dependent on the Shari'ah. Then rulings dependent on the Shari'ah are of two types: (1) Theoretical – i.e., they have no relation to action, but are only from reflection and thought, e.g., 'ijma' is a proof' – considering ijma' as a proof is related to belief. (2) Practical – related to action, such as prayer and fasting. So after 'shar'iyya', he added the restriction 'amaliyya' to exclude theoretical rulings. However, the commentator says: The restriction 'amaliyya' excludes theoretical rulings only if the second meaning of 'hukm', i.e., the address of Allah, is general, including both practical and theoretical rulings; then the restriction 'amali' would be a qualifier for it. But we have an objection to this.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the definition of 'hukm shar'i' in the Hanafi usul tradition. The author (matn) defines 'shar'i' as that which depends on the Shari'ah, and 'ghayr shar'i' as that which does not, to avoid circular reasoning: if belief in Allah and prophethood depended on the Shari'ah, then the Shari'ah itself (which depends on belief) would be circular. The commentator explains this and then presents an objection: according to the majority (Jumhur), 'shar'i' simply means 'what is addressed by the Lawgiver', which avoids the whole issue. The commentator then defends the matn's approach by clarifying that 'hukm' is 'khitab Allah', which is eternal and cannot be dependent, so the definition applies to the address, not the ruling itself. Further, the commentator discusses a potential counter-argument: the Shari'ah depends on belief itself, not the obligation of belief, so even if the obligation of belief depends on the Shari'ah, no circularity arises because the dependence is of different things. Finally, the passage classifies 'shar'i' rulings into theoretical (nazari, like the authority of ijma) and practical (amali, like prayer and fasting), noting that the restriction 'amali' excludes theoretical rulings only on certain interpretations of 'hukm'. Throughout, the commentary follows the author's order, first explaining the matn, then addressing criticisms, then giving further clarifications.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): The address of Allah (khitab Allah) related to the actions of the mukallaf, and it is eternal (qadim).
شرعي (shar'i): That which depends on the Shari'ah (ما يتوقف على الشرع).
نظري (nazari): A ruling that does not relate to the modality of action; it is theoretical, like the authority of ijma.
عملي (amali): A ruling that relates to the modality of action, such as prayer and fasting.

A) To include all religious obligations under the term 'shar'i'.
B) To exclude rulings like the obligation of belief in Allah, which are prerequisites for the Shari'ah itself, thus avoiding circularity.
C) To align with the majority of usul scholars.
D) To make the definition easier to memorize.

Key Takeaway
The matn's definition of shari'i rulings is methodologically precise, excluding meta-legal obligations like belief in Allah to avoid circular reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

The Definition of Hukm Shar'i and the Circularity Objection
Explaining the matn's definition of hukm shar'i and refuting a circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi definition of hukm shar'i avoids logical circularity by distinguishing between hukm as eternal divine attribute and hukm as address dependent on shari'a.

Core Idea
Hukm as eternal divine speech cannot depend on anything, but hukm shar'i is the address that depends on shari'a—no circularity.

In this lesson, we analyse the precise wording of the matn's definition of hukm shar'i. The commentator clarifies why the author chose 'al-khitab' (the address) rather than 'ma' (what) to preserve the eternity of Allah's attribute. We also examine an objection about circularity and see how a change in the direction of dependence resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term Being Defined
The passage defines 'al-hukm al-shar'i'. Note that 'hukm' has multiple meanings; here it is the divine address.
2
Distinguish Two Meanings of Hukm
Hukm can mean the eternal attribute of Allah (which cannot depend on anything) or the address (khitab) that is expressed. The matn uses the latter to avoid making the eternal depend on creation.
3
Understand Why the Matn Avoided 'Ma Yatawaqqaf'
If hukm was defined as 'what depends on the shari'a', it would imply the eternal attribute depends on shari'a, which is impossible. So the definition uses 'al-khitab' (the address) as the thing that depends.
This preserves the eternity of Allah's speech.
4
Analyse the Circularity Objection
An objector claims the division into shar'i and ghayr shar'i is unnecessary because even if shari'a depends on iman, and wujub al-iman depends on shari'a, the dependence direction changes, so no circle occurs.
The objector argues that iman itself is the basis, not wujub al-iman.
5
Evaluate the Commentator's Response
The commentator dismisses the objection because the objection fails to recognize that the definition depends on iman (not wujub al-iman), and the direction of dependence is different, but the matn's division remains necessary to avoid any possible circle.
The commentator shows that the objector's reasoning is flawed.

سب سے پہلے ماتن کی عبارت کی کچھ تو ضیح و تشریح کی ہے حکم بمعنی خــطــاب الله ... الخ قدیم ہے اور قدیم کسی دوسری ٹی پر موقوف نہیں ہو سکتا اس لیے ماتن نے الحكم الشرعى كا معنى ما يتوقف . على الشرع نہیں کیا بلکہ الخطاب بما يتوقف على الشرع کیونکہ حکم بمعنی خطاب اللہ قدیم ہے، جو کسی دوسری بھی پر موقوف نہیں ہو سکتا علی ہذا القیاس غیر شرعی کا معنى الخطاب بما لا يتوقف على الشرع پھر ولقائل ... الخ سے مصنف پر اعتراض کرنا مقصود ہے کہ مصنف نے دور سے بچنے کے لیے جو حکم کی تقسیم شرعی و غیر شرعی کی طرف کی ہے اس کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں، کیونکہ شرعی بمعنی خطاب اللہ کے لویا بمعنی شریعت محمدی کے بہر صورت نفس ایمان پر موقوف ہے نہ کہ وجوب ایمان پر لہذا اگر وجوب ایمان شریعت پر موقوف ہو جائے جس طرح کہ بعض حضرات کا مذہب ہے کہ جب تک شریعت نہ آئے اس . وقت تک ایمان لانا واجب نہیں تو کوئی دور لازم نہیں آتا کیونکہ ثبوت شریعت نفس ایمان پر موقوف ہو اور وجوب ایمان نفس شریعت پر موقوف ہوا لہذا جہت تو قف بدل گئی تو دور لازم نہیں آئے گا۔
First, some clarification and explanation of the author's text: hukm in the sense of khitab Allah ... is eternal, and the eternal cannot depend on another thing. Therefore, the author did not define al-hukm al-shar'i as 'what depends on the shari'a' but rather as 'the address that depends on the shari'a' because hukm in the sense of khitab Allah is eternal, which cannot depend on anything else. By analogy, al-ghayr al-shar'i is defined as 'the address that does not depend on the shari'a'. Then with 'wa li-qa'il ...' etc., the objection to the author is intended: that the division of hukm into shar'i and ghayr shar'i made by the author to avoid circularity is unnecessary, because shar'i in the sense of khitab Allah or in the sense of shari'a Muhammadiyya in any case depends on iman itself, not on wujub al-iman. So if wujub al-iman depends on the shari'a as some hold that until the shari'a comes, it is not obligatory to believe, then no circularity occurs. Because the establishment of shari'a depends on iman itself, and wujub al-iman depends on the shari'a itself. Thus the direction of dependence changes, so circularity does not arise.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's definition of hukm shar'i. The author explains that hukm in its primary sense is the eternal speech of Allah, which cannot depend on anything else. Therefore, the matn defined hukm shar'i as 'the address that depends on the shari'a' rather than 'what depends on the shari'a', preserving the eternity of hukm as divine attribute. The same logic applies to ghayr shar'i. Then an objector is introduced who claims the shar'i/ghayr shar'i division is unnecessary to avoid circularity. The objector argues that if shar'i is taken as khitab Allah itself, or as the shari'a of Muhammad, it still depends on iman, not on the obligation of iman. Some hold that before revelation, iman is not wajib; if so, the establishment of shari'a depends on iman, and wujub al-iman depends on shari'a—different directions, hence no circularity. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي (hukm shar'i): The address of Allah that depends on the shari'a.
دور (dawr): Logical circularity where A depends on B and B depends on A.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the matn define hukm shar'i as 'the address that depends on the shari'a' rather than 'what depends on the shari'a'?
A) To avoid making hukm dependent on itself
B) To include both eternal and created aspects
C) To exclude ghayr shar'i rulings
D) To simplify the definition

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hukm shar'i avoids circularity by treating hukm as eternal speech (khitab) that depends on shari'a, while the objection fails because it confuses the direction of dependence.

The Definition of Fiqh: Parsing 'min adillatiha' and the Redundancy of 'bil-istidlal'
Explaining syntactical attachment and debating redundancy in fiqh definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'min adillatiha' attaches to 'ilm and evaluate whether Ibn al-Hajib's addition of 'bil-istidlal' is redundant.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge directly from detailed evidences — the muqallid's indirect knowledge is excluded.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the rulings of Allah derived from detailed evidences' contains a subtle syntactical point: does the phrase 'min adillatiha' modify 'ahkam' or 'ilm'? The commentator argues it must modify 'ilm' to properly exclude the muqallid. Further, Ibn al-Hajib added 'bil-istidlal' to also exclude knowledge by necessity. Some claimed this addition is redundant. Let's parse the definition precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms in the definition
Locate the words 'ilm', 'ahkam', 'adillaha', 'tafsiliyyah', and 'istidlal' in the definition. Understand each term's role.
The definition: 'Fiqh is knowledge of the rulings of Allah derived from detailed evidences (through ijtihad).'
2
Analyze the attachment of 'min adillatiha'
Determine whether 'min adillatiha' attaches to 'ahkam' or 'ilm'. The commentator shows that attaching to 'ahkam' fails to exclude the muqallid. Attaching to 'ilm' ensures that the knowledge itself must come directly from evidence.
Key argument: the muqallid knows rulings from evidences indirectly, but his knowledge is not from direct examination of evidences.
3
Understand the exclusion of the muqallid
The muqallid's knowledge is based on the mujtahid's statement, which itself is based on evidence. But the muqallid does not derive the ruling directly from the evidence. Therefore, his knowledge is not 'fiqh' in the technical sense.
This exclusion is the primary purpose of the condition 'min adillatiha'.
4
Examine the addition of 'tafsiliyyah'
The restriction to 'detailed evidences' excludes knowledge derived from general principles (dalil ijmail), such as 'obligatory because requisite exists' without knowing the specific verse.
Fiqh requires knowledge of the specific text that establishes the ruling.
5
Evaluate the dispute over 'bil-istidlal'
Ibn al-Hajib added 'bil-istidlal' to exclude knowledge by necessity (e.g., angels' knowledge). The matn author considered it redundant because the muqallid is already excluded. The commentator argues it excludes a different category (knowledge by necessity), so it is not redundant.
Know the three types: knowledge by necessity (daruriy), knowledge by inference (istidlali), and indirect knowledge (muqallid).

Dars e Talweeh
Coming soon.
The commentator said: His statement: 'from its evidences' means the knowledge that is obtained. One might mistakenly think that his statement 'from its evidences' is attached to the rulings, and then the knowledge of the muqallid would not be excluded, because it is knowledge of rulings that are obtained from their detailed evidences, even if the muqallid's knowledge is not obtained from the evidences. So he refuted that by stating that it is attached to knowledge, not to the rulings, because what is obtained from the evidence is knowledge of the thing, not the thing itself. Moreover, if by 'rulings' is meant the divine address, it is eternal and cannot be obtained from anything. The meaning of obtaining knowledge from evidence is that one looks into the evidence and from it knows the ruling. Thus, the muqallid's knowledge, although based on the statement of the mujtahid which is based on his knowledge which is based on the evidence of the ruling, is nevertheless not obtained from looking into the evidence. And he restricted the evidences to detailed ones, because knowledge of the obligation of something due to the existence of a requisite, or of its non-obligation due to the existence of a negating factor, is not part of fiqh.
Explanation: Here, the author (matn) has extracted the phrase 'ay: al-ilm al-hasil' after 'min adillatiha' and intends to clarify it. That is, the author (matn) extracted this phrase to indicate that 'min adillatihi' is related to the word 'ilm' in the definition of fiqh, not to 'ahkam', as some have imagined. Because the purpose of this restriction (min adillatiha) is to exclude the knowledge of the muqallid. If it were related to 'ahkam', then this restriction would not serve this purpose, since the muqallid also has knowledge of rulings, and those rulings are indeed obtained from evidences. Therefore, it is necessary to relate 'min adillatiha' to knowledge so that the meaning becomes: fiqh is knowledge of rulings, provided that this knowledge of rulings is directly obtained from evidences. Obviously, knowledge directly from evidences is obtained by the mujtahid, not by the muqallid; thus this restriction becomes effective. Because from evidence arises knowledge of a thing, not the thing itself, just as from seeing smoke you obtain knowledge of fire, not the fire itself. And 'ala annahu ... etc.' presents a second proof that the ruling itself cannot be obtained from evidence, because you said that ruling is the divine address, and the divine address is eternal, and the eternal cannot be obtained from anything. Hence, the meaning of 'obtaining the ruling' is obtaining knowledge of it. 'Wa ma'na hulul al-ilm min al-dalil ... etc.' is intended to answer an objection. The objection is: By the restriction 'min adillatiha' you have excluded the muqallid's knowledge, claiming that his knowledge is not obtained from evidence. The objector can say that your statement is false, because the rulings that the muqallid holds are ultimately derived from some evidence, so how does this restriction exclude the muqallid's knowledge? The commentator responds: It is true that for the muqallid, the statement of his mujtahid is authoritative, and the mujtahid's statement is derived from evidence. However, since the muqallid does not extract rulings by directly reflecting on the evidence, therefore this restriction excludes the muqallid's knowledge. Finally, he explains the benefit of adding 'tafsiliyyah' to 'adillatiha': sometimes knowledge of a ruling is obtained from a general evidence (dalil ijmail), e.g., such-and-such is obligatory because its requisite exists, or such-and-such is prohibited because a negating factor exists. These are general evidences; that is not called fiqh until the detailed evidence (dalil tafsili) for the ruling is known. It is clear that detailed evidence is the specific verse or hadith from which the ruling is derived.
The commentator said: His statement: 'There is no doubt that it is redundant.' Ibn al-Hajib held that obtaining knowledge of rulings from evidences may be by way of necessity, like the knowledge of Jibril and the Messenger (peace be upon them), and may be by way of istidlal and istinbat, like the knowledge of the mujtahid. The first is not called fiqh idiomatically, so it is necessary to add the condition of istidlal and istinbat to avoid it. The author (matn) thought that it is a precaution against the knowledge of the muqallid, so he asserted that it is redundant because the muqallid is already excluded by his statement 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyyah'.
Explanation: Allama Matn said that in the definition of fiqh, Allama Ibn al-Hajib added the condition 'bil-istidlal' after 'min adillatiha', and that is repetition. Because the muqallid's knowledge is already excluded by the condition 'min adillatiha', so re-excluding it with 'bil-istidlal' is removing what has already been removed, which is repetition. Here, the commentator intends to refute the author (matn) by saying that 'bil-istidlal' excludes the knowledge of the muqallid.
Maktaba Tul Ishaat.com
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage from Dars e Talweeh is a detailed commentary on the definition of fiqh. It begins by clarifying that the phrase 'min adillatiha' (from its evidences) in the definition of fiqh is attached to 'al-ilm' (knowledge) and not to 'al-ahkam' (rulings). The commentator explains why this attachment is necessary: if it were attached to 'ahkam', then the knowledge of the muqallid (one who follows a mujtahid) would not be excluded, because the muqallid also knows the rulings that are derived from evidences. By attaching it to 'ilm', the definition requires that the knowledge itself be obtained directly from the evidence, which is not the case for the muqallid, who relies on the mujtahid's statement. The commentator also argues that the ruling itself (the divine address) is eternal and cannot be 'obtained' from anything; only knowledge of it is obtained. Then, the commentator restricts the evidences to 'detailed evidences' (tafsiliyyah) to exclude knowledge derived from general principles (dalil ijmail), such as knowing that something is obligatory because a requisite exists, without knowing the specific verse or hadith. This is not considered fiqh until the detailed evidence is known. The second part of the passage addresses a dispute over whether the added condition 'bil-istidlal' (by inference) by Ibn al-Hajib is redundant. The matn author (likely from al-Tawdih) argues that it is redundant because 'min adillatiha al-tafsiliyyah' already excludes the muqallid. However, the commentator (sharh) points out that Ibn al-Hajib added 'bil-istidlal' to also exclude knowledge by necessity (like that of angels and prophets), not just the muqallid's knowledge. Thus, the addition is not redundant, because it removes a different category. The commentator thus refutes the matn's claim. The passage demonstrates the precision required in defining fiqh, ensuring that it applies only to knowledge obtained through direct ijtihad from detailed evidences.

Key Terms
فقہ (fiqh): Knowledge of the rulings of Allah derived from detailed evidences through ijtihad
مقلد (muqallid): One who follows a mujtahid without knowing the evidence directly
دلیل تفصیلی (dalil tafsili): A specific verse or hadith from which a ruling is derived directly
بالاستدلال (bil-istidlal): Through inferential deduction (istidlal) as opposed to knowledge by necessity
علم ضروری (ilm daruri): Knowledge that is obtained by necessity without inference, e.g., the knowledge of angels and prophets regarding rulings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator insist that 'min adillatiha' must attach to 'ilm' rather than 'ahkam'?
A) Because 'ahkam' is eternal and cannot be derived from evidences.
B) Because the muqallid's knowledge would otherwise be included, since he knows rulings derived from evidences.
C) Because the word 'adillatiha' is grammatically feminine and must attach to a feminine noun.
D) Because the definition of fiqh requires knowledge of rulings, not the rulings themselves.

Key Takeaway
The precise definition of fiqh excludes indirect knowledge (muqallid), knowledge by necessity, and knowledge from general evidence — all must be excluded by appropriate conditions.

Definition of Hukm Shar'i and Khitab
Clarifying the definition of hukm shari as per Shafi'is and the meaning of khitab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Shafi'i definition of hukm shar'i and explain the linguistic and technical meaning of khitab in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Hukm shar'i is defined as khitab Allah related to mukallafin's actions; 'shar'i' is not redundant.

This lesson clarifies a common misunderstanding about the Shafi'i definition of hukm shar'i. The commentator of Dars e Talweeh points out that some erroneously think Shafi'is define 'hukm' without 'shar'i', but in fact they define 'hukm shar'i' specifically. We will also dissect the term 'khitab' to understand its linguistic and technical meaning in usul.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two misconceptions
The author mistakenly thought that Shafi'is define 'hukm' (not 'hukm shar'i') as 'khitab Allah', and that there is disagreement among Shafi'is on this definition. Identify these errors from the passage.
The author likely based this on incomplete reading of Shafi'i texts.
2
Follow the commentator's refutation
The commentator asserts that all Shafi'i books uniformly define 'hukm shar'i' as 'khitab Allah related to actions of mukallafin'. No disagreement exists. Trace how the commentator rebuts the author's claims.
The commentator attributes the errors to lack of familiarity with Shafi'i sources.
3
Dissect the term 'khitab'
Linguistically, khitab means directing speech to another for understanding. In usul, it refers to the eternal internal speech of Allah (kalam nafsi), but some interpret it as directed speech to an addressee. Understand both views.
The concept of kalam nafsi is important for the eternal attribute of speech in Hanafi-Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : قوله : ولما عرف الفقه ، أقول : المذكور في كتب الشافعيه أن خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين تعريف للحكم الشرعي المتعارف بين الأصوليين ، لا للحكم المأخوذ في تعريف الفقه ، والمصنف ذهب إلى أنه تعريف له ، وأن الشرعي قيد زائد على خطاب الله وإن كونه تعريفا للحكم الشرعي إنما هو رأى بعض الأشاعرة . وكل ذالك لعدم تصفحهم كتبهم فنقول : عرف بعض الأشاعرة الحكم الشرعى بخطاب الله المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين "۔ تشریح: اولاً ایک حقیقت بیان کی ہے اور اس کے بعد ماتن پر اعتراض کیا ہے۔ حقیقت یہ ہے کہ تمام کتب شوافع میں "خطاب الله المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين ... إلخ کو احکم الشرعی کی تعریف بنایا گیا ہے نہ کہ صرف حکم کی تعریف، جو فقہ کی تعریف میں ماخوذ ہے، لیکن ماتن کو یہاں پر دو مغالطے لگے ایک یہ کہ شوافع کے نزدیک "خطاب الله ... إلخ " صرف حکم کی تعریف ہے نہ کہ الحکم الشرعی کی لہذا اس وقت شرعی کی قید زائد ہوگی کیونکہ "خطاب الله خود شرعی کے معنی کو ادا کر رہا ہے، دوسرا یہ کہ تعریف بعض شوافع کے نزدیک صرف حکم کی ہے اور بعض دوسرے حضرات کے نزدیک الحکم الشرعی کی یعنی وہ کسی ایک پر متفق نہیں ۔ شارح فرماتے ہیں کہ ماتن نے ان کی کتب کا مطالعہ نہیں کیا اور اگر ان کی کتابوں کی ورق گردانی کرتے تو ان کو یہ مغالطے ہر گز نہ لگتے واقعہ یہ ہے کہ تمام شوافع کے نزدیک خطاب اللہ حکم شرعی متعارف کی تعریف ہے نہ کہ صرف حکم کی۔

قال الشارح : "و الخطاب فى اللغة "توجيه الكلام نحو الغير للإفهام "، ثم نقل إلى ما يقع به التخاطب ، وهو ههنا الكلام النفسى الأزلى ومن ذهب إلى أن الكلام لا يسمى في الأزل خطابا فسر الخطاب بالكلام الموجه للإفهام ، أو الكلام المقصود منه إفهام من هو متهيأ لفهمه . تشریح: حکم کی تعریف میں جو " خطاب" کا لفظ آیا ہے، شارح اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ "خطاب اپنے کلام کو سمجھانے کے لیے غیر کی طرف متوجہ کرنے کو کہتے ہیں پھر ہر اس کلام کو کو بھی
The commentator said: His statement: 'When he defined fiqh…' I say: What is mentioned in the books of the Shafi'is is that the speech of Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons is a definition of the 'hukm shar'i' known among the usuliyun, not of the hukm taken in the definition of fiqh. The author held that it is a definition of it, and that 'shar'i' is an extra restriction on 'khitab Allah', and that its being a definition of 'hukm shar'i' is only the opinion of some Ash'aris. All this is because they did not peruse their books. So we say: Some Ash'aris defined 'hukm shar'i' as 'khitab Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons'. Elaboration: Firstly, a fact is stated, and then the matn is criticized. The fact is that in all the books of the Shafi'is, 'khitab Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons, etc.' is made the definition of 'hukm shar'i', not just the definition of 'hukm' that is taken in the definition of fiqh. But the author fell into two misunderstandings: one that according to the Shafi'is, 'khitab Allah, etc.' is only a definition of 'hukm' not of 'al-hukm al-shar'i', so the restriction 'shar'i' would be redundant because 'khitab Allah' itself conveys the meaning of 'shar'i'; second, that this definition is only according to some Shafi'is as a definition of 'hukm' and according to others as 'al-hukm al-shar'i', meaning they are not agreed. The commentator says that the author did not study their books; if he had, he would not have these misconceptions. The reality is that according to all Shafi'is, 'khitab Allah' is the definition of the common 'hukm shar'i', not just of 'hukm'.

The commentator said: "Al-khitab lexically means 'directing speech towards another for understanding'. Then it is transferred to what indicates the discourse, and here it refers to the eternal internal speech. And whoever holds that speech is not called khitab in pre-eternity interprets khitab as speech directed for understanding, or speech intended to make understood by one prepared to understand it." Elaboration: In the definition of hukm, the word 'khitab' appears. The commentator explains it, saying that khitab is directing one's speech towards another to make him understand, then it is applied to any speech that constitutes discourse. Here it refers to the eternal internal speech (kalam nafsi). Some, who do not consider kalam to be called khitab in pre-eternity, interpret khitab as directed speech or speech intended for an addressee prepared to understand.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage consists of two sections. First, the commentator addresses a misunderstanding by the author (mussannif) regarding the Shafi'i definition of hukm shari. The author mistakenly thought that Shafi'is define 'hukm' (without the adjective 'shar'i') as 'khitab Allah', and that 'shar'i' is an extra restriction. The commentator clarifies that in all Shafi'i books, 'khitab Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons' is specifically the definition of 'hukm shar'i' (the legal ruling) as used by usul scholars, not the general 'hukm' used in defining fiqh. The author's two errors are described, and the commentator attributes them to lack of study of Shafi'i texts. Second, the passage explains the term 'khitab' linguistically and terminologically. Lexically, khitab means directing speech to another for conveyance of meaning. In legal usage, it refers to the eternal internal speech of Allah (kalam nafsi). However, some scholars, who do not consider speech to be called 'khitab' in pre-eternity (because there was no addressee), interpret it as speech directed to an addressee for understanding. The commentator presents both views to clarify the terminology used in the definition.

Key Terms
حکم شرعی (hukm shari): The ruling of Allah as defined by usul scholars: 'khitab Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons'.
خطاب (khitab): Linguistically: directing speech to another for understanding. In legal definition: the eternal internal speech of Allah (kalam nafsi), or directed speech.
كلام نفسى (kalam nafsi): Internal speech, the eternal attribute of speech in Allah, as opposed to verbal speech (kalam lafzi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do all Shafi'i books define as 'khitab Allah related to the actions of the legally responsible persons'?
A) The general term 'hukm' as used in the definition of fiqh
B) The 'hukm shar'i' as used by usul scholars
C) Only the commands of Allah
D) The prohibitions of Allah

Key Takeaway
The definition of hukm shar'i as khitab Allah related to mukallafin is uniform in Shafi'i usul; 'shar'i' is integral.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Definition of Hukm Shar'i: Correcting the Ash'ari Misunderstanding
Corrects the matn's mistake about Ash'ari definition of hukm shar'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct definition of hukm shar'i according to the usuliyyun and explain why the matn's claim about Ash'ari disagreement is flawed.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari definition of hukm shar'i is identical to the usuli definition; the matn's error stems from not reading their books.

This lesson addresses a foundational definition in usul al-fiqh and a common misconception. The matn (based text) assumed that the Ash'aris define only 'hukm' not 'hukm shar'i', but the commentator shows this is false. Understanding this avoids confusions in later usul discussions.

Guided Path
1
State the definition given
Identify the exact definition: 'khiṭāb Allāh al-mutaʿalliq bi-afʿāl al-mukallafīn'. Recognize that this is the conventional definition among usuliyyun, not the one used in fiqh.
2
Identify the matn's claim
The matn claimed that this definition is only for 'hukm' (ruling) and not for 'hukm sharʿī', and that some Ash'aris disagree on this point.
3
Follow the commentator's rebuttal
The commentator states that all Ash'aris use this very definition for 'hukm sharʿī', and the matn's error is due to not having perused their books. The commentator provides evidence from their works.
4
Understand the broader usul point
This definition is conventional (mutāraf) among legal theorists. It sets the scope for usul al-fiqh: evidences for rulings related to actions of mukallafīn.
