First: Because the prohibition prevents the declaratory speech from being a ruling, and it is conventional to call some parts of speech a ruling and not others, so how must he mention 'wad'' in the definition of ruling? Rather, how is that valid? And second: Because he prevents it from being outside the definition and makes the prescriptive speech more general than it, including it, so what harm is there in their concepts differing? Rather, how can the concept of general and specific be the same? Moreover, his statement 'the understood from declaratory speech is the attachment of one thing to another' contains looseness. The meaning is that what is understood from it is the speech about the attachment of one thing to another as a cause, condition, or preventing factor. Commentary: The intent is to first quote the author's investigation and then object to it. The summary of the author's investigation is that to include declaratory ruling, the qualification 'wad'' should be added to the definition of ruling, because ruling is of two types: prescriptive and declaratory. When the qualification 'by way of demand' was added to include prescriptive ruling, it is necessary to also add the qualification 'wad'' so that declaratory ruling can be included. The rest: declaratory ruling cannot be included in prescriptive ruling because their concepts are separate, so they cannot be unified; even if in some cases they coincide, that does not necessitate that the difference between them is completely erased, just as 'human' and 'rational'—despite being unified in terms of reference—differ in concept. 'Human' means 'rational animal', and 'rational' means 'the essence that has rationality'. 'Anast khair' intends to refute the matn, saying that what the author said cannot be a correct interpretation of the Asha'ira's statement. First: because those people are not prepared to consider declaratory ruling as a ruling, so how can the addition of a qualification be correct according to them, even if others consider it a ruling? Second: because they say: even if we accept it as a ruling, it would not be excluded from the definition, because 'iqtida' is general, whether explicit or implicit, as was detailed previously. As for your saying that their concepts are different, that is fine; there is no harm in it, because the concepts of general and specific differ—just as 'human' is specific and 'animal' is general, each has a separate concept, and there is also unification in that in some cases human and animal come together. Finally, with 'ala anna al-mafhum...' etc., they intend to express the author's looseness (error) that the author defined the concept of declaratory speech as 'the attachment of one thing to another', whereas this is wrong; rather, its concept is 'the speech about the attachment of one thing to another as a cause, condition, or preventing factor', i.e., declaring by speech that such-and-such is a cause, condition, or preventing factor for such-and-such.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by the commentator (the author of the sharh) of the author of the matn (Sadr al-Shariah) regarding the definition of hukm (ruling). The author had proposed that to include wad'i (declaratory) hukm, one should add the qualification 'wad'' to the definition. The commentator presents two objections: First, the Asha'ira do not consider wad'i speech as hukm at all—they define hukm only as prescriptive speech—so adding 'wad'' is irrelevant. Second, even if they did accept wad'i as hukm, it would already be covered because 'iqtida' (demand) in the definition is interpreted broadly to include both explicit and implicit demands, and thus wad'i is not excluded. The commentator uses the example of 'human' and 'rational' to show that two concepts can be different yet coincide in some instances, so the author's insistence on adding 'wad'' is unnecessary. He also criticizes the author's definition of wad'i speech as 'attachment of one thing to another', correcting it to 'speech about attachment as a cause, condition, or preventing factor'—a standard Hanafi usul understanding.

Key Terms
الخطاب التكليفي (al-khitab al-taklifi): Prescriptive speech that demands an action (obligation, prohibition, etc.)
الخطاب الوضعي (al-khitab al-wad'i): Declaratory speech that establishes causes, conditions, or preventers without demand
اقتضاء (iqtida): Demand or implication; in the definition of hukm, it refers to the prescriptive aspect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it is included in the definition.
B) No, they do not recognize it as hukm.
C) Only if it involves a demand.
D) Only if it is stated explicitly.

Key Takeaway
The debate over adding 'wad'' to the definition of hukm reveals that the scope of 'iqtida' is central: the Asha'ira use a broad interpretation that eliminates the need for separate mention, while the Hanafis insist on conceptual distinction.

Author's Misinterpretation of the Asha'ira's Definition of Hukm
Correcting the author's confusion between definitions of mutlaq hukm and hukm shar'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Asha'ira's unanimous definition of hukm as hukm shar'i and explain why the author's hypothetical analysis is based on a false premise.

Core Idea
The Asha'ira unanimously define hukm shar'i; the author's confusion arises from mistaking textual abbreviation for substantive disagreement.

In this passage, the commentator corrects a misunderstanding by the author (Sadr al-Shariah) regarding the Asha'ira's definition of hukm. Some Shafi'i usul books abbreviate the definition by omitting the word 'shar'i', but the commentator insists that the Asha'ira are unanimous: they always define hukm shar'i, not mutlaq hukm. The author's analysis of two possible meanings of 'shar'i' is based on a mistaken assumption that there is internal disagreement, which the commentator refutes.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish hukm mutlaq and hukm shar'i
Understand that hukm mutlaq is an unqualified ruling, while hukm shar'i is specifically the speech of Allah concerning the acts of the legally responsible persons. The qualification 'shar'i' is crucial because it excludes matters like the obligation to believe in Allah (wujub al-iman), which is not dependent on the Shari'ah.
Hanafi usul rarely uses 'hukm' without qualification; here the focus is on the Asha'ira's usage.
2
Identify the Asha'ira's actual definition from the commentator's clarification
The commentator states: 'There is no difference among any of the Asha'ira that this is the definition of hukm shar'i.' This means that whether the word 'shar'i' is explicit or implied by context, the definition is always of hukm shar'i.
The commentator emphasizes that the Asha'ira are unanimous.
3
Locate the author's mistaken assumption
The author saw that some books mention 'the speech of Allah' without 'shar'i' and others mention 'hukm shar'i' explicitly. He then assumed that the Asha'ira might have two different definitions: one for absolute hukm and one for legal hukm. The commentator points out that this is a misreading of the texts.
The author's error is a failure to recognize that the shorter phrase is merely an indication (isharah) of the longer definition.
4
Analyze the consequence if the definition were for mutlaq hukm
The author himself notes that if the definition were for absolute hukm, then the word 'shar'i' would have to mean 'dependent on the Shari'ah' to exclude wujub al-iman. But wujub al-iman is not dependent on the Shari'ah; rather, the Shari'ah depends on it. This would cause the definition to be too broad. The commentator uses this to show that the author's hypothetical is unnecessary.
This highlights the danger of misinterpreting the intended scope of a definition.
5
Conclude the correct position
The correct position is that all Asha'ira define hukm shar'i. The term 'shar'i' is either explicit or understood by context. The author's analysis of two possible definitions is based on a false premise.
The commentator sides with the Asha'ira against the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : قوله :”بعضهم عرف ذكر في بعض المختصرات أن الحكم خطاب الله ... الخ إشارة إلى الحكم الشرعى المعهود ، وصرح في كثير من الكتب : بأن الحكم الشرعى خطاب الله ، فتوهم المصنف أن هذا تعريف للحكم عند البعض وللحكم الشرعي عند البعض . ولا خلاف لأحد من الأشاعرة فى أن هذا تعريف للحكم الشرعي . قال المصنف : إذا كان تعريفاً للحكم المطلق فمعنى الشرعي “ما يتوقف على الشرعي ليكون قيد مفيدا مخرجا وجوب الإيمان ونحوه وإذا كان تعريفاً للحكم الشرعي فمعنى الشرعى ما ورد به الخطاب الشرع لا يتوقف على الشرع والالكان الحد أعم من المحدود لتناوله مثل وجوب الإيمان مع أن المحدود لا يتناوله ح لعدم توقفه على الشرع .

شارح یہاں سے مصنف کا رد کرنا چاہتے ہیں تفصیل اس کی ہے کہ شوافع کی بعض مختصر کتابوں میں خطاب اللہ مطلق حکم کی تعریف کی گئی ہے حالانکہ ان کا اشارہ حکم شرعی کی طرف تھا اور بعض دوسری بلکہ اکثر کتابوں میں خطاب الله ... الخ الحكم الشرعی کی تعریف کی گئی ہے یعنی انہوں نے اشارہ پر اکتفاء نہیں بلکہ صراحنا الشرعی کی قید بڑھادی تو مصنف کو تو ہم ہو گیا کہ شاید اشاعرہ کا اس بارے میں اختلا ف ہے کہ بعض حضرات صرف حکم کی تعریف خطاب الله ... الخ سے کرتے ہیں اور بعض دوسرے الحكم الشرعی کی حالانکہ مصنف کا یہ تو ہم غلط ہے اس لیے مصنف نے اپنے تو ہم کی بناء پر متن میں یوں کہا کہ اگر یہ تعریف مطلق حکم کی ہو تو اس وقت "الشرعیہ " کا معنی "ما يتوقف على الشرع کریں گے تا کہ یہ قید مخرج و مفید ہو جائے کیونکہ وجوب الایمان وغیرہ کا نکالنا مقصود ہے کیونکہ وہ شرعی بمعنی شریعت پر موقوف کے نہیں بلکہ شرعیت اس پر موقوف ہے اور اگر الحکم اشرعی کی تعریف بنائی جائے تو اس وقت
The commentator said: His statement: 'Some defined it; it is mentioned in some abridgements that hukm is the speech of Allah... etc.' is an indication of the well-known hukm shar'i, and it is stated explicitly in many books that hukm shar'i is the speech of Allah. So the author understood that this is a definition of hukm for some and of hukm shar'i for others. But there is no difference among any of the Asha'ira that this is the definition of hukm shar'i. The author said: If it is a definition of absolute hukm, then the meaning of 'shar'i' is 'that which depends on the Shari'ah' so that it becomes a useful qualification excluding wujub al-iman and the like. And if it is a definition of hukm shar'i, then the meaning of 'shar'i' is 'that which the legal speech has brought' without depending on the Shari'ah; otherwise, the definition would be more general than the defined, including, for example, wujub al-iman, while the defined does not include it due to its not being dependent on the Shari'ah. Commentary: The commentator intends to refute the author. The detail is that in some brief books of the Shafi'is, 'the speech of Allah' is defined as absolute hukm, but their indication was toward hukm shar'i. And in other, indeed most, books, 'the speech of Allah... etc.' is defined as hukm shar'i, i.e., they did not suffice with indication but explicitly added the qualification 'shar'i'. So the author mistakenly thought that perhaps the Asha'ira have a disagreement: some define only 'hukm' as 'the speech of Allah...' and others define 'hukm shar'i'. However, this misunderstanding of the author is wrong. Therefore, based on his misunderstanding, the author said in the text: If this is a definition of absolute hukm, then at that time the meaning of 'shar'i' would be 'that which depends on the Shari'ah' so that the qualification becomes excluding and beneficial, because the intent is to exclude wujub al-iman and the like, since they are not shar'i in the sense of being dependent on the Shari'ah but rather the Shari'ah depends on them. And if it is a definition of hukm shar'i, then at that time...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage clarifies a misunderstanding by the author (Sadr al-Shariah) regarding the Asha'ira's definition of hukm. The commentator (the author of the sharh) states that the Asha'ira are unanimous: they define hukm shar'i specifically, not mutlaq hukm. Some Shafi'i books mention 'the speech of Allah' without the word 'shar'i' but by way of indication they mean hukm shar'i; other books explicitly state 'hukm shar'i'. The author mistakenly thought there was a disagreement, so he analyzed two possible meanings of 'shar'i' depending on whether the definition is for mutlaq hukm or hukm shar'i. The commentator corrects this: there is no disagreement; the definition is always of hukm shar'i. The author's hypothetical analysis is based on a false premise. The text ends with the author's statement about the implications of each interpretation.

Key Terms
الحكم المطلق (al-hukm al-mutlaq): Absolute ruling, without the qualification 'shar'i'
الحكم الشرعي (al-hukm al-shar'i): Legal ruling defined as 'the speech of Allah concerning the acts of the legally responsible persons'
موقوف على الشرع (mawquf 'ala al-shar'): Depending on the Shari'ah for its existence or bindingness

Key Takeaway
The Asha'ira are unanimous in defining hukm shar'i; textual variations are only in explicitness, not in substance.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Defining Hukm Shar'i: The Ash'ari Consensus and a Hanafi Correction
Commentator corrects author's misunderstanding of the definition of hukm shar'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct definition of hukm shar'i and explain why the qualifier 'al-shar'iyya' changes meaning depending on whether it is attached to absolute hukm or hukm shar'i.

Core Idea
The definition of hukm shar'i is Khitaab Allah; 'shar'i' means 'conveyed by the address', not 'dependent on the Shari'ah'.

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial point in usul al-fiqh: the definition of hukm shar'i (legal ruling). While many students memorize the phrase 'Khitaab Allah al-muta'alliq bi af'al al-mukallafin', they often miss the logical implications of the qualifier 'al-shar'iyya'. Here, the Hanafi commentator corrects a misunderstanding by the author of the matn, Sadr al-Shariah, who thought some Ash'aris define absolute hukm with 'Khitaab Allah'. By the end, you will see how precision in definition affects the scope of what counts as a legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested definition
Locate the phrase 'Khitaab Allah' in the source. Notice that the author thought some Ash'aris use it to define absolute hukm, while others use it for hukm shar'i.
The commentator insists that all Ash'aris agree it defines hukm shar'i.
2
Analyse the two interpretations of 'al-shar'iyya'
If the definition is for absolute hukm, then 'al-shar'iyya' must mean 'dependent on the Shari'ah' (ma yatawaqqaf 'ala al-shar') so it excludes things like the obligation of faith. If for hukm shar'i, it means 'conveyed by the Shari'ah address' (ma warada bihi khitaab al-shar').
This distinction is the core of the commentator's correction.
3
Trace the logical consequence of misinterpreting
If 'al-shar'iyya' is taken as 'dependent on Shari'ah' when defining hukm shar'i, the definition becomes more general than the defined (since the obligation of faith would be included, but it is not a legal ruling).
This violates the rule that the definition must be coextensive with the defined.
4
Apply the qualifier 'al-‘amaliyya'
Even after the correction, a question arises: does the definition wrongly include beliefs? The commentator points out that the qualifier 'al-‘amaliyya' (practical) excludes beliefs like the obligation of faith.
This shows how the definition is refined step by step.
5
Understand the fiqh terminology shift
Finally, the commentator notes that fuqaha use 'hukm' to mean the established ruling (obligation, prohibition) — a metaphorical usage of the language.
This clarifies the difference between usul and fiqh definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال الشارح : قوله :”بعضهم عرف ذكر في بعض المختصرات أن الحكم خطاب الله ... الخ إشارة إلى الحكم الشرعى المعهود ، وصرح في كثير من الكتب : بأن الحكم الشرعى خطاب الله ، فتوهم المصنف أن هذا تعريف للحكم عند البعض وللحكم الشرعي عند البعض . ولا خلاف لأحد من الأشاعرة فى أن هذا تعريف للحكم الشرعي . قال المصنف : إذا كان تعريفاً للحكم المطلق فمعنى الشرعي “ما يتوقف على الشرعي ليكون قيد مفيدا مخرجا وجوب الإيمان ونحوه وإذا كان تعريفاً للحكم الشرعي فمعنى الشرعى ما ورد به الخطاب الشرع لا يتوقف على الشرع والالكان الحد أعم من المحدود
لتناوله مثل وجوب الإيمان مع أن المحدود لا يتناوله ح لعدم توقفه على الشرع .
تشریح
شارح یہاں سے مصنف کا رد کرنا چاہتے ہیں تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ شوافع کی بعض مختصر کتابوں میں خطاب اللہ مطلق حکم کی تعریف کی گئی ہے حالانکہ ان کا اشارہ حکم شرعی کی طرف تھا اور بعض دوسری بلکہ اکثر کتابوں میں خطاب الله ... الخ الحكم الشرعی کی تعریف کی گئی ہے یعنی انہوں نے اشارہ پر اکتفاء نہیں بلکہ صراحنا الشرعی کی قید بڑھادی تو مصنف کو تو ہم ہو گیا کہ شاید اشاعرہ کا اس بارے میں اختلا ف ہے کہ بعض حضرات صرف حکم کی تعریف خطاب الله ... الخ سے کرتے ہیں اور بعض دوسرے الحكم الشرعی کی حالانکہ مصنف کا یہ تو ہم غلط ہے اس لیے مصنف نے اپنے تو ہم کی بناء پر متن میں یوں کہا کہ اگر یہ تعریف مطلق حکم کی ہو تو اس وقت "الشرعیہ " کا معنی "ما يتوقف على الشرع کریں گے تا کہ یہ قید مخرج و مفید ہو جائے کیونکہ وجوب الایمان وغیرہ کا نکالنا مقصود ہے کیونکہ وہ شرعی بمعنی شریعت پر موقوف کے نہیں بلکہ شرعیت اس پر موقوف ہے اور اگر الحکم اشرعی کی تعریف بنائی جائے تو اس وقت

الشرعيه “ کا معنی ”ما ورد به خطاب“ ہوگا بما يتوقف على الشرع نہیں وگرنہ حد یعنی خطاب اللہ عام ہو جائے گی جو وجوب ایمان وغیرہ کو شامل ہو جائے گی جب کہ محدود یعنی الحکم الشرعی وجوب
ایمان کو شامل نہیں لہذا اس وقت حد کا محدود سے اہم ہونا لازم آئے گا جو کہ غلط ہے۔
قال الشارح : قوله : فالحكم على هذا ـ أى على تقدير أن يكون خطاب الله الخ تعريفاً للحكم الشرعي - إسناد أمر إلى أمر آخر لا خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف وإلا لكان ذكر الشرعية تكرارا لما سبق من أن الشرعي على هذا لتقدير ماورد به خطاب الشرع لا مـا يتوقف على الشرع . فإن قيل : فيدخل في الأحكام الشرعية مثل وجوب الإيمان مع أنه ليس من الفقه . قلنا : يخرج بقيد العملية .
تشریح
متن کی تحقیق کی توضیح و تشریح کرنا چاہتے ہیں کہ اگر خطاب الله ... إلخ الحكم الشرعی کی تعریف ہو تو اس وقت صرف حکم کی تعریف ہوگی اسناد أمر إلى أمر آخر ، کیونکہ مطلق حکم کی تعریف بھی "خطاب الله ... الخ ہو تو ظاہر بات ہے کہ الشرعیہ " کی قید تکرار بنے گی جو کہ غلط ہے کیونکہ ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ الشرعیه کا معنی "ماورد به خطاب الشرع“ کے ہے تو حکم کا بھی خطاب اللہ اور الشرعيه " کا معنی بھی " ما ورد به خطاب الله تو یه تکرار محض ہے پھر ان قبل ... الخ سے ایک سوال کیا ہے وہ یہ کہ جب الحکم الشرعی کا معنی آپ نے خطاب اللہ کے ساتھ کیا ہے تو اس وقت وجوب ایمان تو فقہ میں داخل ہو جائے گا حالانکہ وہ فقہ میں داخل نہیں۔ جواب دیا کہ آئندہ آنے والی قید العملية " سے خارج ہو جائے گا چونکہ ایمان کا تعلق قلب سے ہے نہ کہ عمل ہے۔
قال الشارح : " قوله : " والفقهاء يريد أن الحكم في اصطلاح الفقهاء حقيقة ما ثبت بالخطاب من الوجوب والحرمة ونحوهما وهو مجاز لغوى حيث أطلق المصدر أعنى
الحكم على المفعول أعنى المحكوم به ".
تشریح
کہ فقہا کے نزدیک حقیقتا حکم ماثبت بالخطاب کو کہتے ہیں جیسے وجوب اور حرمت اور اس وقت
There is also agreement that in some cases man and animal come together. At the end, 'ala anna al-mafhum... etc., he (the author) wants to express the author's negligence (error) that the author explained the meaning of 'khitaab wad'i' as the relation of a thing to a thing, whereas this is wrong. Rather, its meaning is 'the address concerning the relation of a thing to a thing as being a cause, condition, or preventer' i.e., through the address to inform that such-and-such is a cause for such-and-such, or a condition, or a preventer. The commentator said: His statement: 'Some of them defined, mentioned in some abridgments that hukm is Khitaab Allah... etc.' is an indication to the well-known hukm shar'i, and it is explicitly stated in many books that hukm shar'i is Khitaab Allah. So the author imagined that this is a definition of hukm according to some and of hukm shar'i according to others. There is no disagreement among any of the Ash'aris that this is a definition of hukm shar'i. The author said: If it is a definition of absolute hukm, then the meaning of 'al-shar'iyya' is 'what depends on the Shari'ah' so that it becomes a useful qualifier excluding the obligation of faith and similar things. And if it is a definition of hukm shar'i, then the meaning of 'al-shar'i' is 'that which is conveyed by the Shari'ah address' and does not depend on the Shari'ah. Otherwise, the definition would be more general than the defined thing because it includes the obligation of faith, while the defined thing does not include it due to its not depending on the Shari'ah. Explanation: The commentator wants to refute the author from here. The detail is that in some abridged books of the Shafi'is, 'Khitaab Allah' is given as the definition of absolute hukm, although their indication was to hukm shar'i. And in some other, rather most books, 'Khitaab Allah... etc.' is given as the definition of hukm shar'i, meaning they did not suffice with indication but explicitly added the qualifier 'al-shar'i'. So the author misunderstood that perhaps the Ash'aris disagree about this: some define hukm simply as 'Khitaab Allah... etc.' and others define hukm shar'i. But this misunderstanding of the author is wrong. Therefore, based on his misunderstanding, the author said in the text: If this is a definition of absolute hukm, then the meaning of 'al-shar'iyya' would be 'what depends on the Shari'ah' so that this qualifier becomes exclusive and useful because the intention is to exclude the obligation of faith etc., since they are not shar'i in the sense of depending on the Shari'ah; rather, the Shari'ah depends on them. And if it is made a definition of hukm shar'i, then the meaning of 'al-shar'iyya' would be 'what is conveyed by the address', not depending on the Shari'ah. Otherwise, the definition, i.e., Khitaab Allah, would become general, including the obligation of faith etc., while the defined thing, i.e., hukm shar'i, does not include the obligation of faith. Therefore, it would necessitate the definition being more general than the defined, which is wrong. The commentator said: His statement: 'Thus, hukm according to this — i.e., on the assumption that Khitaab Allah etc. is a definition of hukm shar'i — is the attribution of a matter to another matter, not the address of Allah related to the action of the accountable person. Otherwise, mentioning 'al-shar'iyya' would be a repetition of what preceded: that 'al-shar'i' on this assumption means what is conveyed by the address of the Shari'ah, not what depends on the Shari'ah. If it is said: Then it includes in the legal rulings such as the obligation of faith, although it is not from fiqh. We say: It is excluded by the qualifier 'al-‘amaliyya'. Explanation: He wants to explain and clarify the research of the text: that if 'Khitaab Allah... etc.' is a definition of hukm shar'i, then at that time the definition of hukm would be 'attribution of a matter to another matter', because the definition of absolute hukm is also 'Khitaab Allah... etc.' So it is obvious that the qualifier 'al-shar'iyya' would become a repetition, which is wrong, because it has passed earlier that the meaning of 'al-shar'iyya' is 'what is conveyed by the address of the Shari'ah'. So hukm is also Khitaab Allah and 'al-shar'iyya' also means 'what is conveyed by the address of Allah'. This is pure repetition. Then with 'in qeel... etc.' a question is posed: that when you take the meaning of hukm shar'i as Khitaab Allah, then the obligation of faith would enter into fiqh, although it is not in fiqh. The answer is that it is excluded by the upcoming qualifier 'al-‘amaliyya' since faith relates to the heart, not to actions. The commentator said: His statement: 'And the fuqaha' intend that hukm in the terminology of the fuqaha' haqiqatan is what is established by the address, such as obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a linguistic metaphor, as the source — i.e., hukm — is applied to the object — i.e., al-mahkum bih.' Explanation: That according to the fuqaha', haqiqatan hukm is called what is established by the address, such as obligation and prohibition, and at that time...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the definition of 'hukm' (ruling) and 'hukm shar'i' (legal ruling) in usul al-fiqh. The commentator is responding to a misunderstanding by the author of the matn (likely Sadr al-Shariah) regarding how Ash'ari scholars define these terms. The issue is whether the phrase 'Khitaab Allah' (address of Allah) is a definition of absolute hukm or specifically hukm shar'i. The commentator clarifies that all Ash'aris agree it defines hukm shar'i, not absolute hukm. The author mistakenly thought there was a difference of opinion. The commentator then explains the implications: if it were for absolute hukm, the qualifier 'al-shar'iyya' would mean 'dependent on the Shari'ah', excluding things like obligation of faith which are not dependent on Shari'ah but rather Shari'ah depends on them. If for hukm shar'i, 'al-shar'iyya' means 'conveyed by the address', not dependent. The commentator also addresses a potential objection that this would include obligation of faith in fiqh, but responds that the qualifier 'al-‘amaliyya' (practical) will later exclude it. Finally, the commentator notes that in fiqh terminology, 'hukm' is used literally for the ruling established by the address, e.g., obligation, prohibition, which is a metaphorical use of the linguistic term.

Key Terms
حکم (hukm): In the context of this passage, hukm is being defined either absolutely or as hukm shar'i. The commentator notes that in fiqh terminology, hukm is what is established by the address (e.g., obligation, prohibition).
حکم شرعی (hukm shar'i): The address of Allah related to the actions of the accountable (Khitaab Allah al-muta'alliq bi af'al al-mukallafin). This is the agreed-upon definition among Ash'aris.
خطاب اللہ (Khitaab Allah): The address of Allah, which is the basis of legal rulings. In this definition, it is the source from which hukm is derived.
الشرعیة (al-shar'iyya): A qualifier that can either mean 'dependent on the Shari'ah' (if defining absolute hukm) or 'conveyed by the Shari'ah address' (if defining hukm shar'i).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main reason for insisting that the definition 'Khitaab Allah' is for hukm shar'i, not absolute hukm?
A) Absolute hukm does not exist.
B) If it were for absolute hukm, the qualifier 'al-shar'iyya' would need to mean 'dependent on Shari'ah', which would exclude the obligation of faith, but the Ash'aris include it under absolute hukm.
C) The Ash'aris never use the term 'hukm' without the qualifier 'shar'i'.
D) The commentator's own Hanafi school rejects the definition.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of hukm shar'i is exclusively for hukm shar'i, not absolute hukm, and the qualifier 'al-shar'iyya' must be interpreted as 'conveyed by the Shari'ah address' to avoid logical inconsistency.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Defining al-Hukm al-Shar'i and Answering the Shafi'i Objection
Definition and defense of hukm shar'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of al-hukm al-shar'i, explain why the restriction 'practical' is necessary, and apply the three Hanafi responses to the Shafi'i objection regarding the locus of the ruling.

Core Idea
Al-hukm al-shar'i is Allah's speech concerning acts; jurists metaphorically call its result 'hukm'.

This passage clarifies the precise definition of a legal ruling (al-hukm al-shar'i) and defends it against a key objection raised by Shafi'i usul scholars. The commentator distinguishes between the speech of Allah (the source) and the resulting attributes of acts (wujub, hurmah), showing that the jurists use 'hukm' metaphorically for the result. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying commands in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the definition of al-hukm al-shar'i as 'the speech of Allah related to the act of the obligated person' and note the clarification that it is not mere attribution.
The restriction 'practical' (العملية) excludes beliefs.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Grasp why Shafi'is object: if hukm is defined as Allah's speech, it does not match the jurists' usage where hukm is an attribute of the act (wujub).
This is a fundamental ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh.
3
Analyze the three Hanafi responses
Work through each answer: (1) 'khatab' means content, not the divine attribute; (2) the precise term is iijab/tahrim, not wujub/hurmah; (3) hukm is the same reality considered from two perspectives.
The third answer is often considered preferable.
4
See the terminological metaphor
Recognize that when Hanafi jurists say 'hukm' for wujub or hurmah, it is a linguistic metaphor (مجاز لغوي) – the verbal noun applied to the object.
This does not negate the definition; it explains usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : قوله : فالحكم على هذا ـ أى على تقدير أن يكون خطاب الله الخ تعريفاً للحكم الشرعي - إسناد أمر إلى أمر آخر لا خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف وإلا لكان ذكر الشرعية تكرارا لما سبق من أن الشرعي على هذا لتقدير ماورد به خطاب الشرع لا مـا يتوقف على الشرع . فإن قيل : فيدخل في الأحكام الشرعية مثل وجوب الإيمان مع أنه ليس من الفقه . قلنا : يخرج بقيد العملية . قال الشارح : " قوله : " والفقهاء يريد أن الحكم في اصطلاح الفقهاء حقيقة ما ثبت بالخطاب من الوجوب والحرمة ونحوهما وهو مجاز لغوى حيث أطلق المصدر أعنى الحكم على المفعول أعنى المحكوم به ". قال الشارح : قوله : يرد عليه إشارة إلى اعتراضات على تعريف الحكم مع الجواب عن البعض . الأول : أن المقصود تعريف الحكم المصطلح بين الفقهاء وهو ما ثبت بالخطاب كـالـوجـوب والحرمة وغيرهما مما هو من صفات فعل المكلف ، لا نفس الخطاب الذي هو من صفات الله تعالى وهذا مما أورد فى كتب الشوافعية . أجيب عنه بوجوه : الأول : أنه كما أريد بالحكم ما حكم به أريد بالخطاب "ما خوطب به" للقرينة العقلية على أن الوجوب ليس نفس كلام الله تعالى . الثاني : أن الحكم هو الإيجاب والتحريم ونحوهما ، وإطلاقه على الوجوب والحرمة تسامح . الثالث: أن الحكم نفس خطاب الله فالإيجاب هو نفس قوله "افعل" وليس للفعل منه صفة حقيقية فإن القول ليس لمتعلقه منه صفت لتعلقه بالمعدوم وهو إذا نسب إلى الحاكم يسمى إيحابا وإذا نسب إلى ما فيه الحكم وهو " افعل " يسمى وجوبا. وهما متحدان بالذات ، مختلفان باعتبار . فذلك تراهم يجعلون أقسام الحكم الوجوب والحرمة مرة والإيجاب والتحريم أخرى ؛ وتارة الوجوب والتحريم كما في أصول ابن الحاجب .
The commentator said: His statement: So the ruling on this — i.e., on the assumption that it is the speech of Allah etc. as a definition of the legal ruling — is the attribution of one thing to another, not the speech of Allah related to the act of the obligated person. Otherwise, mentioning "legal" would be a repetition of what preceded, that the legal on this assumption is what is brought by the speech of the Law, not what depends on the Law. For if it is said: Then legal rulings would include such as the obligation of faith although it is not from fiqh. We say: It is excluded by the restriction of being practical. The commentator said: His statement: And the jurists mean that the ruling in the terminology of the jurists is, in reality, what is established by the speech, such as obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a linguistic metaphor where the verbal noun (i.e., hukm) is applied to the object (i.e., the thing ruled upon). The commentator said: His statement: There is an objection to it — an indication of objections to the definition of the ruling along with answers to some. The first: The intended thing is the definition of the ruling in the terminology of the jurists, and it is what is established by the speech, like obligation, prohibition, and others, which are attributes of the obligated person's action, not the speech itself which is an attribute of Allah Almighty. This is something mentioned in the books of the Shafi'is. It is answered in several ways: First: Just as by "ruling" is meant what is ruled, by "speech" is meant "what is spoken with" due to the rational indication that obligation is not the same as the speech of Allah. Second: The ruling is making obligatory, prohibiting, and the like, and applying it to obligation and prohibition is a leniency. Third: The ruling is the very speech of Allah, so making obligatory is the very statement "do," and the act has no real attribute from it, because the statement does not have an attribute from its object due to its relation to the non-existent. When attributed to the one who rules, it is called making obligatory, and when attributed to that in which the ruling resides, i.e., "do," it is called obligation. They are identical in essence, different in consideration. That is why you see they sometimes make the divisions of the ruling as obligation and prohibition, and sometimes as making obligatory and prohibiting, and sometimes as obligation and prohibition, as in Ibn al-Hajib's Usul.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of al-hukm al-shar'i (legal ruling). The commentator first clarifies that if the definition were simply 'the speech of Allah', it would be repetitive because the term 'shar'i' already implies that it is from the Law. However, the correct definition is 'the speech of Allah related to the act of the obligated person' (خطاب الله المتعلق بفعل المكلف). An objection is raised that this would include the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman), which is not a practical ruling of fiqh. The answer is that the restriction 'practical' (العملية) excludes it because faith pertains to the heart, not action. The commentator then explains the jurists' terminology: they use 'hukm' to refer to the result of the speech, such as obligation and prohibition, which is a metaphorical usage (مجاز لغوي) because the verbal noun (hukm) is applied to the object (mahkum bih). Next, the commentator addresses an objection from Shafi'i scholars: the definition defines hukm as Allah's speech, but in fiqh, hukm is an attribute of the act (e.g., obligation), not an attribute of Allah. Three answers are given: (1) Just as 'hukm' is used for the thing ruled, 'khatab' is used for what is spoken (the content), so it is not the literal speech; (2) The precise term is iijab (making obligatory) and tahrim (making prohibited), while wujub and hurma are usages of leniency; (3) Hukm is indeed the speech of Allah, but when considered as coming from the Lawgiver it is iijab, and when considered as a quality of the act it is wujub—they are the same reality viewed differently. This last view is why some scholars list divisions as wujub and hurma, others as iijab and tahrim, as in Ibn al-Hajib's Usul.

Key Terms
الحكم الشرعي (al-hukm al-shar'i): The speech of Allah related to the acts of the obligated persons, either by way of demand, option, or declaration.
الحكم (al-hukm): In the terminology of the jurists, it is what is established by the speech (ما ثبت بالخطاب) such as obligation and prohibition.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The attribute of an act that makes it obligatory; it is the result of the command (افعل).
العملية (al-`amaliyyah): A restriction that limits the definition to practical rulings (those related to actions of the limbs) as opposed to beliefs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi position on the relationship between Allah's speech (the command 'do') and the obligation (wujub) of the act?
A) They are two distinct realities: speech is an attribute of Allah, wujub is an attribute of the act.
B) They are the same reality considered from different perspectives: speech as coming from the Lawgiver is iijab, and as a quality of the act it is wujub.
C) Wujub is a real attribute of the act independent of speech.
D) The command 'do' is neither iijab nor wujub; it is a separate category.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of al-hukm al-shar'i is Allah's speech concerning acts; when jurists call the resulting attributes 'hukm', it is a metaphorical usage that does not conflict with the definition.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Hanafi Definition of Hukm: Defending Khitab Allah as the Ruling
Definitional dispute commentary on hukm's definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hukm and apply the three responses to the Shafi'i objection, identifying the usul principles that justify the definition.

Core Idea
Hukm is Allah's speech itself (khitab Allah); ijab and wujub are the same reality viewed from different relations.

In this passage from Dars-e-Talweeh, the commentator Sadr al-Shariah addresses objections to the classic Hanafi definition of hukm (legal ruling) as 'the address of Allah' (khitab Allah). The Shafi'i school objects that defining hukm as divine speech makes it an attribute of Allah, whereas juristic hukm refers to attributes of acts like obligation. The Hanafi response shows that the definition is consistent through figurative language, identity of ijab and wujub, and rational indication. Understanding this definitional dispute is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school treats rulings, especially regarding acts of minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the First Objection
Locate the Shafi'i objection: hukm in juristic terminology refers to attributes of the mukallaf's act (wujub, hurma), not the divine speech itself. Defining hukm as khitab Allah would be 'definition by opposite' (ta'rif bi al-mubayin).
This objection is cited from Shafi'i usul works.
2
Analyze the Three Hanafi Responses
Work through each response: (1) Figurative usage: just as hukm can mean 'that which is ruled,' khitab can mean 'that which is addressed' (the ruling itself). (2) Hukm in reality is ijab/tahrim, not wujub/hurma; the latter are metaphorical. (3) Ijab and wujub are identical in essence, differing only in relation (attribution to ruler vs. act).
The third response is central to the Hanafi position.
3
Examine the Second Objection
The second objection claims the definition is not inclusive (ghayr mun'akis) because it excludes rulings on minors' acts. The initial response states that such rulings actually pertain to the guardian. Note the author's rejection of this claim.
The author provides two counterarguments to the guardian-based response.
4
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: the Hanafi definition allows rulings to attach directly to minors' acts (e.g., validity of sale, Islam), whereas the Shafi'i view restricts true hukm to responsible adults.
This shows how a usul definition affects furu' outcomes.

Source Text

The commentator said: His saying 'It is objected against it' indicates objections to the definition of hukm along with the answer to some. First: That the intended definition is the hukm terminologically agreed upon among jurists, which is what is established by khitab like obligation, prohibition, and others that are attributes of the mukallaf's act, not the khitab itself which is an attribute of Allah. This is mentioned in the books of the Shafi'is. It is answered with several ways: First: Just as hukm is intended in the sense of 'that which is ruled,' similarly khitab is intended in the sense of 'that which is addressed' due to the rational indication that obligation is not the speech of Allah Himself. Second: That hukm is actually ijab and tahrim and the like, and applying it to wujub and hurma is a tolerance. Third: That hukm is the very khitab of Allah; so ijab is His very saying 'do,' and there is no real attribute of the act from it, because speech does not have an attribute from its object due to its being related to something non-existent. When it is attributed to the ruler, it is called ijab, and when attributed to that in which the ruling exists, i.e., 'do,' it is called wujub. They are identical in essence, differing in consideration. Thus you see them making the divisions of hukm as wujub and hurma at one time, and ijab and tahrim at another; and sometimes wujub and tahrim as in the Usul of Ibn al-Hajib. (Urdu explanation follows). ... Then: Second objection: that the definition is not inclusive because it excludes rulings related to the actions of minors; so it is better to say 'related to the actions of servants.' And it has been answered in their books that the rulings that are imagined to relate to the action of a minor are actually related to the action of the guardian, e.g., he must fulfill rights from the minor's wealth. But the author rejects it firstly that it is not valid regarding the permissibility of his sale, the validity of his Islam, his prayer, his fasting, and their being recommended. And secondly that the attachment of a right to the minor's wealth or his liability is a legal ruling.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of hukm (legal ruling) as presented in the matn. The commentator, Sadr al-Shariah (as the text is from Dars-e-Talweeh), explains that the author of the matn is responding to objections against the definition of hukm as 'the speech of Allah (khitab Allah)'. The first objection, raised by Shafi'i scholars, is that hukm in the terminology of jurists refers to attributes of the mukallaf's act (like obligation and prohibition), not to the divine speech itself, which is an attribute of Allah. Defining hukm as khitab Allah makes the definition contrary to what is defined (ta'rif bil-mubayin). The commentator presents three responses from the matn. First, by analogy, just as hukm is taken in the sense of 'that which is ruled' (ma hukma bihi), so khitab should be taken as 'that which is addressed' (ma khutiba bihi), meaning the obligation or prohibition, due to a rational indication that these are not divine speech itself. Second, hukm in reality is ijab (making obligatory) and tahrim (making prohibited), and terms like wujub and hurma are metaphorical. Third, hukm is identical to divine speech, but when attributed to the ruler it is called ijab, and when attributed to the act it is called wujub; they are essentially the same but considered differently. The passage also addresses a second objection regarding the definition's lack of inclusiveness (adam al-in'ikas) because it excludes rulings related to actions of minors. The response is that rulings concerning minors actually pertain to the guardian. The author rejects this response with two arguments: first, that it does not cover the validity of a minor's sale, Islam, prayer, fasting, and recommendable acts; second, that rights attached to a minor's property or liability are themselves legal rulings (hukm shari). The passage thus defends the definition by showing that the apparent exclusion is not problematic.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): In this passage, hukm is defined as khitab Allah (divine speech) itself, which when related to the act produces attributes like wujub. It is identical with ijab.
خطاب (khitab): Khitab in this context is the address of Allah, and it is taken as 'that which is addressed' (ma khutiba bihi) when necessary to avoid defining hukm as an attribute of Allah.
إيجاب (ijab): Ijab is the act of making obligatory, which is the same as the command 'do' when attributed to the ruler (Allah). It is identical to wujub in essence.
وجوب (wujub): Wujub is the attribute of obligation in the act, i.e., the same reality as ijab but attributed to the act rather than the ruler.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third response in this passage, what is the relationship between ijab (إيجاب) and wujub (وجوب)?
A) They are completely different, with ijab being an act of God and wujub being an attribute of the act.
B) They are identical in essence (mutahidan bi al-dhat) but differ in consideration (mukhtalifan bi al-i'tibar).
C) Ijab is a cause of wujub, as the command creates obligation.
D) Wujub is the metaphorical name for ijab, but they are not the same reality.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hukm as khitab Allah is defended by showing that ijab and wujub are identical in essence, differing only in relation, and that figurative usage of terms is justified.

The Inclusion of Heart Actions and the Role of 'Amaliyyah' in Defining Hukm and Fiqh
Clarifying that the definition includes internal acts and 'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school includes actions of the heart in the definition of hukm and why the term 'amaliyyah' is not redundant in the definition of fiqh.

Core Idea
The word 'fi'l' in the definition of hukm is general; it includes both outward and inward actions, and 'amaliyyah' excludes theoretical matters from fiqh.

This passage addresses two objections to the Hanafi definitions of hukm and fiqh: that heart actions like faith are not included, and that the term 'amaliyyah' is redundant. The Hanafi response relies on the general nature of 'fi'l' and the specific function of 'amaliyyah' to limit fiqh to practical matters. Understanding this clarifies how Hanafi usul subsumes inner acts under legal rulings while keeping fiqh focused on outward practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Objections
Read the passage to find the fourth and fifth objections: first, that the definition of hukm does not cover heart actions; second, that 'amaliyyah' in fiqh is redundant.
2
Analyse the Apparent Conflict
Note the assumption that 'af'al' (actions) in the definition refers only to bodily limbs, which would exclude heart actions and make 'amaliyyah' meaningless.
3
Apply the Hanafi Principle of 'Amm'
Understand that in Hanafi usul, 'fi'l' is a general term (amm) encompassing both outward (physical) and inward (heart) actions. This resolves the first objection.
This is a key principle: al-amm yufidu al-wujub indicates obligation, but here it's about inclusivity.
4
Understand the Role of 'Amaliyyah'
The term 'amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh excludes matters that are not related to bodily actions (e.g., purely theoretical obligations like the obligation of faith itself, as distinct from its practice). Thus it is not redundant.
5
Evaluate the Counterargument
Consider the potential counterargument: if 'hukm' is taken technically, then 'amaliyyah' might be redundant. The passage implies that the broader (non-technical) reading of hukm is intended, preserving the utility of 'amaliyyah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح : 'الرابع: أنه غير شامل للأحكام المتعلقة بأفعال القلب ، مثل : وجوب الإيمان أي : التصديق ، ووجوب الاعتبار أى القياس لأن الظاهر من الأفعال أفعال الجوارح الخامس: أنه لما أخذ في تعريف الحكم المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين أختص بالعملية وخرجت النظريات بناء على اختصاص الفعل بالحوارح وبالعمل ما يختص بالجوارح فلا يخرج مثل وجوب الإيمان والاعتبار عن تعريف الحكم ، ولا يكون ذكر العملية مكررا لإفادته خروج ما لا يتعلق بفعل الجوارح عن تعريف الفقه.' تشریح چوتھا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف افعال قلب ، مثلا : وجوب ایمان اور وجوب اعتبار یعنی قیاس کو شامل نہیں اس لیے کہ بظاہر قید افعال المكلفين سے مراد اعضاء اور جوارح کے افعال مراد ہیں نہ کہ دل کے۔ پانچواں اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جب حکم کی تعریف میں افعال کی قید موجود ہے تو تمام نظریات ، مثلا: وجوب ایمان کہاں سے خارج ہو گئے ۔ لہذا آگے فقہ میں کی تعریف میں جو ' العملية ' کی قید موجود ہے یہ لغو اور ضائع ہے۔ دونوں اعتراضون کا جواب ایک ہی ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف میں جو افعال کا لفظ موجود ہے اس سے مراد عام ہے چاہے افعال ظاہر اعضاء کے ہوں یا افعال قلب ہوں لہذا اب وجوب ایمان اور قیاس وغیرہ بھی تعریف سے نہ نکلے گا اس طرح عملی' کی قید بھی لغو یا مکرر نہ بنے گی کیونکہ اس سے وہ افعال خارج ہو جائیں گے جو متعلق بالبدن والاعضاء نہیں جیسے وجوب ایمان وغیرہ۔ قال الشارح : 'ولقائل أن يقول : إذا حمل الحكم في تعريف الفقه على المصطلح فذكر العملية مكرر قطعا لأن مثل وجوب الإيمان خارج بقيد الشرعية على ما مر ومثل كون الإجماع حجة غير داخل في الحكم المصطلح لخروجه بقيد الاقتضاء أو التخيير . تشریح
The commentator said: The fourth [objection]: It [the definition] is not inclusive of rulings related to actions of the heart, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman), i.e., assent (tasdiq), and the obligation of consideration (al-i'tibar), i.e., qiyas, because the apparent meaning of 'actions' (af'al) is actions of the limbs (jawahir). The fifth [objection]: When it is taken in the definition of hukm related to the actions of the mukallaf, it is restricted to practical matters (amaliyyah) and theoretical matters are excluded, based on the specification of action (fi'l) to limbs, and 'amal' to what is specific to limbs. So such matters like the obligation of faith and consideration are not excluded from the definition of hukm, and the mention of 'amaliyyah' is not redundant because it indicates the exclusion of what does not pertain to the action of limbs from the definition of fiqh. Commentary: The fourth objection is that the definition of hukm does not include actions of the heart, such as the obligation of faith and the obligation of consideration, i.e., qiyas, because apparently the restriction 'actions of the mukallaf' means actions of the organs and limbs, not of the heart. The fifth objection is that when the restriction of actions is present in the definition of hukm, all theoretical matters, e.g., the obligation of faith, are excluded. Therefore, the term 'amaliyyah' that appears later in the definition of fiqh is redundant and useless. The answer to both objections is the same: the word 'actions' (af'al) in the definition of hukm is general, covering both outward actions of the limbs and inward actions of the heart. Hence, the obligation of faith and qiyas are not excluded from the definition. Likewise, the restriction 'amaliyyah' is neither redundant nor repetitive because it excludes those actions that are not related to the body and limbs, such as the obligation of faith. The commentator said: 'One may say: If hukm in the definition of fiqh is taken in the technical sense, then mentioning amaliyyah is definitely redundant, because matters like the obligation of faith are already excluded by the restriction of shar'iyyah as mentioned, and matters like the authority of ijma are not included in the technical hukm because they are excluded by the restriction of iqtida' or takhyir.' Commentary (continues).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents two objections (the fourth and fifth) against the definition of hukm and their unified answer. The fourth objection argues that the definition of hukm as 'the address of Allah concerning the actions of the mukallaf' does not include inward actions of the heart like the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman) or the obligation of using reason (wujub al-i'tibar, i.e., qiyas), because the word 'actions' (af'al) is ordinarily understood to refer to physical actions of the limbs (jawahir). The fifth objection claims that the term 'amaliyyah' (practical) in the definition of fiqh is redundant, since the definition of hukm already restricts itself to actions, and thus theoretical matters are automatically excluded; mentioning 'amaliyyah' adds nothing. The answer to both is that in the definition of hukm, the word 'af'al' is general (amm), covering both outward limb actions and inward heart actions. Therefore, obligations of the heart are included. As for the term 'amaliyyah', it is not redundant; rather, it serves to exclude those matters that are not related to bodily actions, such as the obligation of faith (which is a heart action) from the definition of fiqh (since fiqh is concerned with practical matters). The commentator also notes a potential counterargument: if 'hukm' in the definition of fiqh is taken in the technical sense (as address of Allah), then mentioning 'amaliyyah' would indeed be redundant because the condition of shar'iyyah already excludes matters like the obligation of faith. But the author's response indicates that the definition of hukm is broader. The Urdu commentary clarifies the objection and answer, emphasizing that 'af'al' is general, thus preserving both inward and outward actions under the definition of hukm.

Key Terms
فعل القلب (fi'l al-qalb): Action of the heart, such as belief (iman) and intellectual consideration (i'tibar).
العملية (al-amaliyyah): Practical or operational, referring to matters that involve bodily actions; used in the definition of fiqh to exclude theoretical matters.
فعل (fi'l): Action, which in this context is taken as general (amm) including both outward (physical) and inward (heart) actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the term 'amaliyyah' in the definition of fiqh according to the author?
A) To exclude actions of the heart.
B) To include theoretical matters.
C) To exclude theoretical matters that do not involve bodily actions.
D) To make the definition redundant.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'fi'l' is general, encompassing both inner and outer actions, and 'amaliyyah' refines the scope of fiqh to practical bodily acts without redundancy.

Defending the 'Amaliyah' Restriction in the Definition of Fiqh
Answering objections about the definition of hukm and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle regarding the restriction 'amaliyah' to determine whether a given ruling falls within the definition of fiqh, and critically evaluate objections to its inclusion.

Core Idea
The restriction 'amaliyah' is not redundant; it excludes shari rulings that are theoretical, like permissibility of consensus and obligation of analogy.

In Hanafi usul, the definition of fiqh is carefully crafted to include only practical rulings (amaliyah) derived from detailed evidence. Some scholars objected that the restriction 'amaliyah' is redundant because other restrictions already exclude theoretical rulings. This lesson walks through the objection, the initial response, a refined objection, and the final justification for why 'amaliyah' serves a unique and necessary function.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key definitions
The passage revolves around the Hanafi definitions of hukm (ruling) and fiqh (jurisprudence). The restriction 'amaliyah' modifies fiqh, limiting it to practical rulings. Understand the base definitions before tackling the objections.
Hukm is defined as 'the address of the Lawgiver pertaining to the actions of the obligated [individuals] by way of demand or choice.' Fiqh is defined as 'knowledge of the practical shari rulings derived from detailed evidence.'
2
Understand the two objections
The first objection claims the definition of hukm does not include heart actions like faith; the second argues that because hukm already restricts to actions, 'amaliyah' in fiqh is redundant. Note the difference between external (limb) and internal (heart) actions.
The apparent meaning of 'actions' in the definition of hukm is physical actions of the limbs, hence the objection.
3
Analyze the initial response
The response clarifies that 'actions' in hukm is general, including both limb and heart actions. Therefore, rulings like obligation of faith are not excluded from hukm. However, 'amaliyah' in fiqh is still needed to exclude rulings that are shari but not related to limb actions (e.g., obligation of faith is shari but not practical for fiqh because it is about belief, not limb work).
This response shows that 'amaliyah' excludes rulings that are not tied to physical actions, even if they are shari.
4
Evaluate the refined objection
A refined objection argues that if 'hukm' is taken in its technical sense (with restrictions 'shari' and 'by demand or choice'), then obligation of faith is already excluded by 'shari', and consensus being a proof is not a ruling at all, so 'amaliyah' adds nothing. Understand this nuanced argument.
The refined objection forces a deeper analysis of the scope of 'hukm' and 'fiqh'.
5
Synthesize the commentator's final justification
The commentator refutes the refined objection by showing that rulings like permissibility of consensus (jawaz al-ijma) and obligation of analogy (wujub al-qiyas) are shari but not practical. If 'amaliyah' were absent, these would be included in fiqh via implicit demand (iqtida dhimni), which is improper. Hence 'amaliyah' is not redundant; it excludes those rulings.
The final justification demonstrates that 'amaliyah' specifically targets theoretical shari rulings that would otherwise be misclassified as fiqh.

Source Text

قال الشارح : "الرابع: أنه غير شامل للأحكام المتعلقة بأفعال القلب ، مثل : وجوب الإيمان أي : التصديق ، ووجوب الاعتبار أى القياس لأن الظاهر من الأفعال أفعال الجوارح الخامس: أنه لما أخذ في تعريف الحكم المتعلق بأفعال المكلفين أختص بالعمليات وخرجت النظريات بناء على اختصاص الفعل بالحوارح وبالعمل ما يختص بالجوارح فلا يخرج مثل وجوب الإيمان والاعتبار عن تعريف الحكم ، ولا يكون ذكر العملية مكررا لإفادته خروج ما لا يتعلق بفعل الجوارح عن تعريف الفقه."
تشريح
چوتھا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف افعال قلب ، مثلا : وجوب ایمان اور وجوب اعتبار یعنی قیاس کو شامل نہیں اس لیے کہ بظاہر قید افعال المكلفين “ سے مراد اعضاء اور جوارح کے افعال مراد ہیں نہ کہ دل کے۔
پانچواں اعتراض یہ ہے کہ جب حکم کی تعریف میں افعال کی قید موجود ہے تو تمام نظریات ، مثلا: وجوب ایمان کہاں سے خارج ہو گئے ۔ لہذا آگے فقہ میں کی تعریف میں جو " العملية " کی قید موجود ہے یہ لغو اور ضائع ہے۔ دونوں اعتراضون کا جواب ایک ہی ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف میں جو افعال کا لفظ موجود ہے اس سے مراد عام ہے چاہے افعال ظاہر اعضاء کے ہوں یا افعال قلب ہوں لہذا اب وجوب ایمان اور قیاس وغیرہ بھی تعریف سے نہ نکلے گا اس طرح عملی" کی قید بھی لغو یا مکرر نہ بنے گی کیونکہ
اس سے وہ افعال خارج ہو جائیں گے جو متعلق بالبدن والاعضاء نہیں جیسے وجوب ایمان وغیرہ۔ قال الشارح : "ولقائل أن يقول : إذا حمل الحكم في تعريف الفقه على المصطلح فذكر العملية مكرر قطعا لأن مثل وجوب الإيمان خارج بقيد الشرعية على ما مر ومثل كون الإجماع حجة غير داخل في الحكم المصطلح لخروجه بقيد الاقتضاء أو التخيير .
تشريح
شارح فرماتے ہیں کہ ماتن نے جو أفعال المكلفین “ میں تقسیم کر کے آگے عملیہ کی قید کو مفید بنانے کی کوشش کی ہے، کوئی کہ سکتا ہے کہ یہ غلط ہے کیونکہ مثل وجوب ایمان تو نشر عبہ کی قید سے خارج ہو گیا کیونکہ شرعی کا معنی ہے "ما يتوقف على الشرع " ہے ظاہر ہے وجوب ایمان بایں معنی شرعی نہیں اسی طرح اجماع کا حجت ہونا یہ سرے سے حکم میں داخل نہیں تا کہ اس کو " علمیہ " کی قید سے خارج کیا جا سکے وجہ یہ ہے کہ حکم کی تعریف میں "بالاقتضاء أو التخبير “ کی قید اس کو داخل ہونے سے منع کر رہی ہے تو مطلب یہ ہوا کہ عملیہ کی قید کا کوئی فائدہ نہ ہوا۔
قال الشارح : "لا يقال : معنى كون السنة والإجماع والقياس حجحا وجوب العمل بمقتضاها فيدخل في الاقتضاء الضمني ؛ لأنا نقول فح لا يخرج بقيد العملية ويلزم أن يكـون الـعـلـم بـه مـن الفقه . ويمكن أن يقال : أن التقييد بالعملية يفيد إخراج مثل جواز الإجماع ووجوب القياس وهو حكم شرعى .
تشريح
بعض حضرات نے اوپر مذکور قاتل کے سوال کا جواب دیا تھا ۔ شارح اس کو نقل کر کے رد کرنا چاہتا ہے۔ بعض حضرات نے کہا ہے کہ اجماع سنت اور قیاس کا حجتہ ہونا اس کا مطلب، ان کے تقاضوں پر عمل ہے لہذا اقتضاء غمنی کے طور پر ” بالاقتضاء " کی قید میں داخل ہے پھر عملیہ کی قید سے ان کو خارج کیا گیا ہے، تو اس طور پر عملیہ کی قید لغو نہ ہوئی۔ شارح رد کرتے ہوئے فرماتے ہیں کہ پھر ”عملیہ کی قید سے خارج نہ ہوں گے کیونکہ جب ان میں اقتضاء منی ہے تو عملی بھی ہوں گے لہذا ان کا فقہ میں داخل ہو جانا لازم آئے گا، جو قاتل کے نزدیک بھی غلط ہے، لہذا یہ توجيه القائل بما لا يرضى قائلہ کے قبیل سے ہے۔ آخر میں شارح اپنی طرف سے ”یمکن “ سے توجیہ پیش کر کے اس کو مفید بنانے کی کوشش کرتار ہے خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ عملیہ “ کی قید وجوب ایمان یا اجماع کے حجتہ ہونے کو نکالنے کے لیے نہیں بلکہ جواز اجماع اور وجوب قیاس کو نکالنے کے لیے ہے کیونکہ جواز اجماع یا وجوب قیاس شرعی تو ہیں بقوله تعالى (فاعتبروا یا اولی الالباب مگر عملی نہیں اس لیے "عملیہ " کی اس طرح مفید و مخرج بن جائے گی۔
The commentator said: "Fourth: It does not include rulings related to actions of the heart, such as the obligation of faith i.e. affirmation, and the obligation of reflection i.e. analogy, because the apparent meaning of 'actions' is actions of the limbs. Fifth: When 'actions of the obligated' was taken in the definition of ruling, it was restricted to practical matters and theoretical matters were excluded based on the specification of action to limbs and work to what is specific to limbs. So the like of obligation of faith and reflection are not excluded from the definition of ruling, and mentioning 'practical' is not redundant because it brings about the exclusion of what is not related to action of the limbs from the definition of fiqh."
Commentary: The fourth objection is that the definition of hukm does not include actions of the heart, e.g., obligation of faith and obligation of reflection i.e. analogy, because apparently the restriction 'actions of the obligated' refers to actions of organs and limbs, not the heart. The fifth objection is that when the restriction 'actions' is present in the definition of hukm, then all theoretical matters, e.g., obligation of faith, are excluded. Hence the restriction 'practical' in the following definition of fiqh is useless and wasteful. The answer to both objections is one: that the word 'actions' in the definition of hukm is general, whether they are external actions of limbs or actions of the heart. Therefore, the obligation of faith and analogy etc. are not excluded from the definition, and thus the restriction 'practical' does not become useless or redundant, because it excludes those actions that are not related to the body and limbs, such as the obligation of faith etc. The commentator said: "And someone may say: If the ruling is taken in its technical sense in the definition of fiqh, then mentioning 'practical' is definitely redundant because the like of obligation of faith is excluded by the restriction 'shari' as previously discussed, and the like of consensus being a proof is not included in the technical ruling because of the restriction 'by demand or choice'."
Commentary: The commentator says that the matn author, by dividing 'actions of the obligated' and then trying to make the restriction 'practical' useful, can be said to be wrong because the like of obligation of faith is excluded by the restriction 'shari' (since 'shari' means 'what depends on revelation'), and indeed the obligation of faith is not shari in this sense. Similarly, consensus being a proof is not included in the ruling at all, so that it could be excluded by the restriction 'practical'; the reason is that the restriction 'by demand or choice' in the definition of hukm prevents it from being included. So the meaning is that the restriction 'practical' has no benefit. The commentator said: "It cannot be said: The meaning of the Sunnah, consensus, and analogy being proofs is the obligation to act by their implications, so they are included in the implicit demand; for we say: Then they would not be excluded by the restriction 'practical', and it would be necessary that knowledge of them be part of fiqh. And it can be said: The restriction by 'practical' benefits the exclusion of the like of permissibility of consensus and obligation of analogy, which is a shari ruling."
Commentary: Some people answered the above objector's question. The commentator wants to quote and refute it. Some said that the meaning of consensus, Sunnah, and analogy being proofs is acting upon their implications, so they are included in the implicit demand as 'by demand', and then they are excluded by the restriction 'practical', so in this way the restriction 'practical' is not redundant. The commentator refutes by saying: Then they will not be excluded by the restriction 'practical', because when they have implicit demand, they will also be practical, so it would be necessary that they be included in fiqh, which is also wrong according to the objector. Therefore, this justification is of the type 'justification that does not satisfy the justifier'. At the end, the commentator tries to make it useful by presenting a justification with 'it can be said'. The summary is that the restriction 'practical' is not intended to exclude the obligation of faith or consensus being a proof, but rather to exclude permissibility of consensus and obligation of analogy, because permissibility of consensus or obligation of analogy are shari (by His saying 'So take heed, O people of insight') but not practical, so in this way 'practical' becomes useful and excluding.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on objections raised against the definitions of 'hukm' and 'fiqh'. The commentator (sharh) addresses two objections: (1) that the definition of hukm includes only actions of the limbs, not heart actions like faith, and (2) that the restriction 'ammali (practical) in the definition of fiqh is redundant. The response is that 'actions' in the definition of hukm is general (including heart actions), so the restriction 'practical' in fiqh is not redundant because it excludes theoretical rulings like obligation of faith. Then a further objection is considered: if hukm is taken in its technical sense (with restrictions like 'shari' and 'by demand or choice'), then 'practical' is indeed redundant because obligation of faith is excluded by 'shari' and consensus being a proof is not even part of hukm. The commentator refutes this by showing that if consensus and analogy are considered rulings due to implicit demand, they would be practical and thus would be included in fiqh, which is undesirable; hence the restriction 'practical' is not redundant. Finally, the commentator suggests that 'practical' actually serves to exclude rulings like permissibility of consensus and obligation of analogy (which are shari but not practical), thus proving its utility. The analysis follows the Hanafi usul tradition, focusing on precise technical definitions.

Key Terms
عملیہ (amaliyah): Practical rulings related to actions of the limbs (jawaarih)
افعال المکلفین (af'al al-mukallafin): Actions of the obligated individuals, including both limb actions and heart actions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the purpose of the restriction 'amaliyah' in the definition of fiqh?
A) To exclude the obligation of faith from fiqh
B) To exclude rulings like permissibility of consensus and obligation of analogy
C) To make the definition of hukm broader
D) To emphasize that fiqh only covers limb actions

Key Takeaway
The restriction 'amaliyah' in the Hanafi definition of fiqh is not redundant; it excludes shari rulings that are not practical, such as permissibility of consensus and obligation of analogy.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
Refining the Definition of Hukm: Amali and Shar'i
Commentator defends the 'amali' and 'shar'i' qualifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the function of the qualifiers 'amali' and 'shar'i' in the Hanafi definition of hukm shari and explain their exclusionary effect on specific theoretical rulings.

Core Idea
The qualifiers 'amali' and 'shar'i' are not redundant: they precisely delimit the definition of hukm shari to exclude theoretical rulings and rationally known obligations.

The definition of hukm shari is a foundational technical term in usul al-fiqh. In this passage, the commentator defends the matn author's inclusion of two seemingly redundant qualifiers – 'amali' (practical) and 'shar'i' (legal). By analyzing an objection and its rebuttal, we will see how these terms work together to keep the definition precise and exclude specific edge cases. This examination will sharpen your understanding of how Hanafi usul defines its subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
An objector claims that 'amali' is useless because 'shar'i' already excludes matters like wujub al-iman (obligation of faith). Also, hujjiyyat al-ijma is not even part of the definition of hukm, so 'amali' is not needed to exclude it.
The objection targets the matn's attempt to make 'amali' beneficial.
2
Examine the flawed counter-argument and its refutation
Some respond that hujjiyyat al-ijma carries an 'implied requirement' (iqtida' dimni) to act, so it enters the definition under al-iqtida', and then 'amali' excludes it. The commentator refutes that this would make the ruling amali, leading to its inclusion in fiqh, which the objector also rejects. So this counter-argument is invalid.
The commentator dismisses this as 'justifying something its proponent would not accept'.
3
Comprehend the correct benefit of 'amali'
The commentator then provides the proper function: 'amali' excludes rulings like jawaz al-ijma (permissibility of consensus) and wujub al-qiyas (obligation of analogy). These are shar'i rulings (based on revelation) but not practical actions; they are about the authority of sources, not actions of the mukallaf.
Thus 'amali' removes theoretical rulings that are within the scope of shar'i but not action-based.
4
Analyze the definition of 'shar'i'
The matn defines 'shar'i' as 'ma la yudraku law la khitab al-shari' (what is not understood without the Lawgiver's speech). This definition avoids redundancy with the definition of hukm as 'khitab Allah'. The commentator notes that for the Ash'aris, since they deny rational perception of rulings, their definition of 'shar'i' amounts to the same as 'what revelation says', which would cause redundancy if hukm is defined as 'kalam Allah'.
This clarifies why the Hanafi definition is preferable: it prevents tautology.

كا حسن "مالا يدرك لولا خطاب الشارع " سے کریں یا "ماورد به خطاب الشارع" سے کریں، کیونکہ ہم اوپر بتلا آئے ہیں کہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک عقل کو بالکل احکام میں دخل نہیں ۔ تمام احکام کا
The commentator says: The author (matn) divided actions of the legally responsible, and then tried to make the qualification 'amali' beneficial. Someone could object that this is wrong because, for example, the obligation of faith (wujub iman) was already excluded by the qualification 'shar'i' since 'shar'i' means 'what depends on the law' – clearly obligation of faith is not shar'i in that sense. Similarly, the authority of ijma being hujja is not even included in the definition of hukm, so it cannot be excluded by the qualification 'amali'. The reason is that in the definition of hukm, the qualification 'by requirement or option' prevents it from being included. Thus the qualification 'amali' would be useless. The commentator says: 'It should not be said that the meaning of Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas being proofs is the obligation to act according to their implications, so they enter under implicit requirement; for we say then they are not excluded by the qualification 'amali', and it would necessarily follow that knowledge of them is from fiqh. But it can be said that the qualification by 'amali' benefits the exclusion of something like the permissibility of Ijma and the obligation of Qiyas, which is a shar'i ruling.' Explanation: Some people had answered the objector's question above. The commentator wants to quote and refute it. Some people said that the meaning of Ijma, Sunnah and Qiyas being proofs is that one must act according to their implications, so they enter under implied requirement, and then they are excluded by the qualification 'amali', so the qualification 'amali' is not useless. The commentator refutes saying that then they would not be excluded by the qualification 'amali' because if they contain implied requirement, they are also practical ('amali); therefore it would necessarily follow that they are included in fiqh, which the objector also considers wrong. So this justification is like 'justifying something its proponent would not accept'. Finally, the commentator presents a justification from himself using 'it can be said', trying to make it beneficial. The summary is that the qualification 'amali' is not for excluding obligation of faith or the authority of Ijma being a proof, but rather for excluding the permissibility of Ijma and the obligation of Qiyas, because the permissibility of Ijma or the obligation of Qiyas are shar'i (by the verse 'So take heed, O people of insight') but not practical ('amali). Thus 'amali' becomes beneficial and excluding. The commentator says: 'His statement: And 'shar'iyyah' is what is understood without the speech of the Lawgiver either by the ruling itself or by its original that is analogized upon; thus it excludes like obligation of faith, and includes like the authority of Ijma and Qiyas being a proof, on the assumption that it is a ruling. And he did not define 'shar'iyyah' as 'what the speech of the Lawgiver has come with' because the assumption is that 'hukm' is defined as 'speech of Allah'… etc., so restricting it by 'shar'i' would be redundant. And according to the Ash'aris, 'what the speech of the Lawgiver has come with' is equivalent to 'what is not understood without the speech of the Lawgiver', since for them reason has no capacity to perceive rulings. So if 'speech of Allah' etc. were a definition of absolute hukm (as the author claims some Ash'aris define absolute hukm), then mentioning 'shar'i' would certainly be redundant, whichever interpretation is used.' Explanation: He is explaining and clarifying the matn. The author divided 'shar'iyyah' into whether the speech of the Lawgiver concerns this ruling or its original and analogized base, so analogical rulings also enter under the qualification 'shar'iyyah'. The obligation of faith remains excluded because it is not shar'i in this sense. The authority of Qiyas and Ijma being proofs, if they are considered as rulings, would also enter because they are shar'i, and they would be excluded by the qualification 'amali' as previously mentioned. From 'he did not define' etc., the commentator says: Why did the author define 'shar'iyyah' as 'what is not understood without the speech of the Lawgiver' and not as 'what the speech of the Lawgiver has come with'? He replies: Because 'hukm' means 'speech of Allah' – as if it is in the sense of 'what the speech has come with'. And if we also define 'shar'i' the same way, then the qualification 'shar'i' becomes redundant. As for the Ash'aris, since for them all rulings are shar'i and reason has no capacity to know their goodness or badness (we will explain soon), therefore for them the definition of 'shar'i' as 'what the speech of the Lawgiver has come with' is also equivalent to 'not understood without the speech of the Lawgiver'. So if we make 'speech of Allah' etc. a definition of absolute hukm (as the author thinks some Ash'aris define absolute hukm), then the qualification 'shar'i' is redundant whether we interpret it as 'what is not understood without the Lawgiver's speech' or as 'what the Lawgiver's speech has come with', because we already mentioned that for the Ash'aris reason has absolutely no role in rulings. All rulings...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of hukm shari (legal ruling) and its qualifications. The commentator (sharih) is explaining and defending the matn author's (museannif) inclusion of the qualifiers 'amali (practical) and 'shar'i (legal) in the definition. A potential objection is raised: the qualifier 'amali seems useless because many things are already excluded by 'shar'i'. The commentator responds by first refuting a weak counter-argument, then providing the correct benefit: 'amali excludes matters like the permissibility of ijma (which is shar'i but non-practical) and the obligation of qiyas. Then he discusses the definition of 'shar'iyyah' itself. The matn defines it as 'what is understood without the Lawgiver's speech' to avoid redundancy with the definition of hukm. However, for the Ash'ari school, since reason cannot perceive rulings independently, their definition of 'shar'i' effectively amounts to the same thing. This raises a point about whether the qualifier 'shar'i' would be redundant if one defines hukm as 'speech of Allah' (a definition attributed to some Ash'aris). The commentator explains that indeed it would be redundant for them, but that does not affect the Hanafi position. The lesson thus clarifies the precise scope and intention behind these technical qualifiers in usul al-fiqh, showing how they serve to exclude specific categories from the definition.

Key Terms
عملیہ (amaliyyah): The qualification that a ruling must be practical (amali), i.e., pertains to actions of the mukallaf, to exclude theoretical rulings like hujjiyyat al-ijma and wujub al-qiyas.
شرعیہ (shar'iyyah): The qualification that a ruling must be known only through the Lawgiver's speech (either directly or via qiyas), excluding what reason knows independently.
حکم (hukm): Speech of Allah (or Lawgiver's address) concerning the actions of the legally responsible, consisting of requirement, option, or declaration.
اجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the scholars; its authority (hujjiyya) is a ruling but not practical (amali) in itself.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical deduction; its obligation (wujub) is a ruling derived from Quran (فاعتبروا) but not practical (amali).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's final position, what is the specific benefit of the qualifier 'amali' in the definition of hukm shari?
A) It excludes the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman).
B) It excludes the authority of ijma being a proof (hujjiyyat al-ijma).
C) It excludes the permissibility of ijma (jawaz al-ijma) and the obligation of qiyas (wujub al-qiyas).
D) It excludes all rulings that are not derived from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The qualifiers 'amali' and 'shar'i' in the definition of hukm shari serve to exclude, respectively, non-practical but revelation-based rulings and rationally known obligations, thereby precisely delimiting the subject matter of usul al-fiqh.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
